Jerusalem Talmud Horayot
תלמוד ירושלמי הוריות
The Jerusalem Talmud, translation and commentary by Heinrich W. Guggenheimer. Berlin, De Gruyter, 1999-2015
https://www.nli.org.il/he/books/NNL_ALEPH001901012/NLI

Jerusalem Talmud Horayot



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the Court<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The High Court.</i> ruled to violate one of the commandments spelled out in the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">A commandment clearly spelled out, like the prohibition of eating blood, where the biblical text does not imply an authorization of the rabbinical authorities to define the parameters of the obligation.</i> and a single person went and acted inadvertently<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">When the Court realized its error and changed its ruling, the person acting in good faith on their prior ruling is now faced with the fact that his act violated a biblical commandment, against his intention.</i> following their pronouncement, whether they acted and he acted simultaneously with them, or they acted and he followed their example, or they did not act but he did, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For a purification sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:27-35" href="/Leviticus.4.27-35"><i>Lev.</i> 4:27–35</a>) or, in the absence of a Temple, repentance and an expiatory action.</i> since he depended on the Court. If the Court ruled but one of them knew that they erred, or a student worthy of ordination<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">He knows how to answer when asked any question of religious law.</i> went and acted following their pronouncement, whether they acted and he acted simultaneously with them, or they acted and he followed their example, or they did not act but he did, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For a purification sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:27-35" href="/Leviticus.4.27-35"><i>Lev.</i> 4:27–35</a>) or, in the absence of a Temple, repentance and an expiatory action.</i> since he did not depend on the Court. This is the principle: The person depending on himself is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This rule, which declares that no instruction of the High Court supersedes one’s own certain knowledge, does not contradict the law of the rebellious Elder (<i>Sanhedrin</i> Chapter 8) since by definition the rebellious Elder came to ask the High Court; in the matter he came to ask, his knowledge is not independent of the Court.</i>, but one depending on the Court is not liable.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The text is that of the Leiden ms., the readings are those of the Yerushalmi text in the Bomberg Babli of 1520.</i>“If the Court ruled.” etc. <i>A person who would transgress; one would transgress; acting he would transgress;</i> these are restrictions<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The quotes are correct in the Yerushalmi text of the Babli <i>editio princeps</i>, but the first of the quotes in the Leiden ms. is a misquote, referring to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:2" href="/Leviticus.4.2"><i>Lev.</i>4:2</a> instead of 4:27. The basic text is in <i>Sifra Wayyiqra Parašah</i> 7(1), referred to in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 2b" href="/Horayot.2b">Babli 2b</a>, discussed in detail <i>Šabbat</i>93a.<br>Chapter 4 in <i>Lev.</i> treats the purification sacrifices for unintentional sin first by the High Priest (vv. 1–12), then the High Court (13–21), then a chief, identified in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.1">Mishnah 3:3</a> as a king (22–26), and finally by a commoner (27–35). V. 27 reads: <i>If one</i> <i>person of the populace transgresses inadvertently, by acting on one prohibitions of the Eternal, and feels guilt.</i> It is noted that the sentence seems to be unnecessarily wordy. Why does it not say simply, “if somebody inadvertently transgresses a prohibition of the Eternal”? The additional words must have a meaning; they describe restrictions. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 93a" href="/Shabbat.93a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>93a</a> one derives from the insistence that <i>one</i> person commit the sin that a violation of a commandment cannot be prosecuted if committed by two persons acting in common, so that no single person commits a punishable act but the combined result is a clear violation,. Such a violation cannot be atoned for by a purification sacrifice. It also is clear that only <i>acts</i> are punishable.<br>In the context here the additional terms are interpreted to mean that only a person acting on his own is required to offer a purification sacrifice; this excludes one who is told by a religious authority that his act is permitted.</i>: the person depending on himself is liable, but one dependent on the Court is not liable. Everywhere it is said that a restriction after a restriction is an addition, but here it is said, a restriction after a restriction is to reduce? Rebbi Mattaniah said, it is different here since there is written a restriction after a restriction after a restriction<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">It is a generally recognized principle that a double restriction is an addition and a double addition a restriction (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 6:7:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.6.7.3"><i>Peah</i> 6:9 Note 154</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 12:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.12.1.4"><i>Yebamot</i> 12:1 Note 10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.2.6"><i>Sotah</i>9:2 Note 63</a>, <i>Roš Haššanah</i> 1:1 56a l.58, <i>Megillah</i> 4:4 75b l.14; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 23b" href="/Megillah.23b">Babli <i>Megillah</i>23b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 43a" href="/Yoma.43a"><i>Yoma</i> 43a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 15b" href="/Bava_Kamma.15b"><i>Bava qamma</i> 15b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 15a" href="/Bava_Batra.15a"><i>Bava batra</i> 15a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 15a" href="/Sanhedrin.15a"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 15a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 44b" href="/Sanhedrin.44b">44b</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 66a" href="/Sanhedrin.66a">66a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 9b" href="/Makkot.9b"><i>Makkot</i> 9b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 7b" href="/Shevuot.7b"><i>Ševuot</i> 7b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 9b" href="/Menachot.9b"><i>Menahot</i> 9b</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 67a" href="/Menachot.67a">67a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 132a" href="/Chullin.132a"><i>Hulin</i> 132a</a>.) The principle is extended here to read that any even number of restrictions (additions) is an addition (restriction) while any odd number of restrictions (additions) is a restriction (addition); cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Sanhedrin 15a" href="/Rashi_on_Sanhedrin.15a">Rashi in <i>Sanhedrin</i> 15a</a> <i>s. v.</i> חמשה.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph has a parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.1.3"><i>Terumot</i> 8:1, Notes 14–22</a>. As explained there, the background of the paragraph in <i>Terumot</i> is different from the one presumed here; therefore at a place where the readings there and here seem to be opposite to one another, both are correct in their settings.</i> Rebbi Ḥaggai asked the colleagues: From where that he who eats with permission be not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Referring to Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 8:1; a childless woman of non-priestly birth, married to a Cohen, was eating heave in purity, as is her right and duty, when she was informed of her husband’s death. By this death she returns to her non-priestly status and heave is forbidden to her. Nevertheless, she is not liable for a reparation sacrifice nor to pay for the heave eaten in error. This is the topic of <i>Terumot</i> 8:1, it is not followed up here.</i>? What is the difference between him<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">An Israel to whom heave is forbidden, Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 6:1.</i> who thought that it was profane but it turned out to be heave, who is liable, and him who thought that he was a Cohen but it turned out that he was an Israel, who is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">He had been told from childhood that he was a Cohen; he was stripped of his priesthood by a court on the testimony of two witnesses that one of his female ancestors had been forbidden to her Cohen husband. His case is not different from that of the woman in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.1.3">Note 12</a>.</i>? They said to him, by the instruction of the court. He said to them, still I am having a problem. What is the difference between him who thought that it was weekday but it turned out that it was Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">This is a paradigm of an inadvertent sin as mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:27" href="/Leviticus.4.27"><i>Lev.</i> 4:27</a>.</i>, who is liable, and him who thought that it was a Passover sacrifice but it turned out to be a well-being offering, who is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.1.3"><i>Terumot</i> 8:1, Note 18</a>. The Passover sacrifice must be slaughtered in the afternoon of the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan, whether Sabbath or weekday. A festival wellbeing offering may not be slaughtered on the Sabbath. If the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> was a Sabbath and somebody slaughtered a sheep in the Temple courtyard thinking that it was a designated Passover sacrifice when in fact it had been designated as a well-being offering, R. Joshua, an overriding authority, declares him not liable (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.3.1">Mishnah <i>Pesahim</i> 6:4</a>).</i>. They said to him, because he slaughtered with permission. He said to them, still I am having a problem. What is the difference between him who thought that it was permitted but it turned out to be forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.1.3"><i>Terumot</i> 8:1, Note 18</a>. The Passover sacrifice must be slaughtered in the afternoon of the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan, whether Sabbath or weekday. A festival wellbeing offering may not be slaughtered on the Sabbath. If the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> was a Sabbath and somebody slaughtered a sheep in the Temple courtyard thinking that it was a designated Passover sacrifice when in fact it had been designated as a well-being offering, R. Joshua, an overriding authority, declares him not liable (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.3.1">Mishnah <i>Pesahim</i> 6:4</a>).</i>, who is liable, and him who thought that it was forbidden fat<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Fat of animals which would be burned on the altar if these animals were sacrifices, and the fat into which ischiatic tendons are embedded, is forbidden for human consumption. All other fat is permitted. If a person intended to commit a sin but, unknown to him at the time, failed to commit the sin, he is not liable for a purification sacrifice.</i> and it turned out to be permitted fat, who is not liable. They did not answer at all. He told them, let me tell you from myself<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Translated using the Bomberg Babli text.</i>: <i>Or his transgression in which he sinned was made known to him; he has to bring</i><sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:28" href="/Leviticus.4.28"><i>Lev.</i> 4:28</a>. Since he was informed that he failed to commit the sin, he cannot bring a purification sacrifice. His repentance for his sinful intent will be a private matter between him and God.</i>. Rebbi Yossi came to visit them; they told him, that problem is hard for us. He asked them, why did you not answer him, <i>or his transgression in which he</i><i>sinned was made known to him; he has to bring</i>? They told him, Ḥaggai asked the question, Ḥaggai gave the answer.
In the opinion of Rebbi Ismael, who does not refer this verse to those obligated for purification sacrifices and certain reparation sacrifices for whom the Day of Atonement had passed, it is understandable<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph has no direct connection with the theme of the Mishnah, but is added here to elucidate <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:27" href="/Leviticus.4.27"><i>Lev.</i> 4:27</a>. Since our halakhic Midrashim are all from the school of R. Aqiba, we have to accept the occasional indications of the Yerushalmi on the interpretations of the school of R. Ismael. For him, the verses 4:27–28 detail the conditions on which a private person is permitted and obligated to bring a purification offering.</i>. But what is the opinion of Rebbi Aqiba, who refers this verse to those obligated for purification sacrifices and certain reparation sacrifices for whom the Day of Atonement had passed, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Keritot 6:4" href="/Mishnah_Keritot.6.4">Mishnah <i>Keritut</i> 6:4</a>; <i>Sifra Wayyiqra 2 Paršetah</i> 3(1), 6(1), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:1-18:30" href="/Leviticus.16.1-18.30"><i>Ahare Mot Parašah</i>4(8</a>).<br>Reparation sacrifices are required (1) for sins against a fellow man after restitution (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:20-26" href="/Leviticus.5.20-26"><i>Lev.</i> 5:20–26</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:5-10" href="/Numbers.5.5-10"><i>Num.</i> 5:5–10</a>), (2) misappropriation of <i>sancta</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:14-16" href="/Leviticus.5.14-16"><i>Lev.</i>5:14–16</a>), (3) to regain sanctified status after skin disease (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14" href="/Leviticus.14"><i>Lev.</i> 14</a>). A <i>suspended</i> reparation sacrifice is due if a person suspects that he may have committed an inadvertent sin, without having proof either way. Since the sin is forgiven on the Day of Atonement (with due repentance), such a sacrifice cannot be offered after that day since the scapegoat carries away <i>all</i> iniquities (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:21" href="/Leviticus.16.21"><i>Lev.</i> 16:21</a>).</i>: From where that those obligated for purification sacrifices and certain reparation sacrifices for whom the Day of Atonement had passed, are obligated to bring them after the Day of Atonement, but those obligated for suspended reparation offerings are no longer liable? The verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:28" href="/Leviticus.4.28"><i>Lev.</i> 4:28</a>. Since he was informed that he failed to commit the sin, he cannot bring a purification sacrifice. His repentance for his sinful intent will be a private matter between him and God.</i>, <i>or his transgression in which he sinned was made known to him; he has to bring</i>, even after the Day of Atonement. <sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">This copy from the first paragraph has no discernible meaning here.</i> From the following: <i>A person who would transgress; one would transgress; acting he would transgress;</i> these are restrictions<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The quotes are correct in the Yerushalmi text of the Babli <i>editio princeps</i>, but the first of the quotes in the Leiden ms. is a misquote, referring to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:2" href="/Leviticus.4.2"><i>Lev.</i>4:2</a> instead of 4:27. The basic text is in <i>Sifra Wayyiqra Parašah</i> 7(1), referred to in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 2b" href="/Horayot.2b">Babli 2b</a>, discussed in detail <i>Šabbat</i>93a.<br>Chapter 4 in <i>Lev.</i> treats the purification sacrifices for unintentional sin first by the High Priest (vv. 1–12), then the High Court (13–21), then a chief, identified in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.1">Mishnah 3:3</a> as a king (22–26), and finally by a commoner (27–35). V. 27 reads: <i>If one</i> <i>person of the populace transgresses inadvertently, by acting on one prohibitions of the Eternal, and feels guilt.</i> It is noted that the sentence seems to be unnecessarily wordy. Why does it not say simply, “if somebody inadvertently transgresses a prohibition of the Eternal”? The additional words must have a meaning; they describe restrictions. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 93a" href="/Shabbat.93a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>93a</a> one derives from the insistence that <i>one</i> person commit the sin that a violation of a commandment cannot be prosecuted if committed by two persons acting in common, so that no single person commits a punishable act but the combined result is a clear violation,. Such a violation cannot be atoned for by a purification sacrifice. It also is clear that only <i>acts</i> are punishable.<br>In the context here the additional terms are interpreted to mean that only a person acting on his own is required to offer a purification sacrifice; this excludes one who is told by a religious authority that his act is permitted.</i>: the person depending on himself is liable, {but one dependent}<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">It is a generally recognized principle that a double restriction is an addition and a double addition a restriction (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 6:7:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.6.7.3"><i>Peah</i> 6:9 Note 154</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 12:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.12.1.4"><i>Yebamot</i> 12:1 Note 10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.2.6"><i>Sotah</i>9:2 Note 63</a>, <i>Roš Haššanah</i> 1:1 56a l.58, <i>Megillah</i> 4:4 75b l.14; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 23b" href="/Megillah.23b">Babli <i>Megillah</i>23b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 43a" href="/Yoma.43a"><i>Yoma</i> 43a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 15b" href="/Bava_Kamma.15b"><i>Bava qamma</i> 15b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 15a" href="/Bava_Batra.15a"><i>Bava batra</i> 15a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 15a" href="/Sanhedrin.15a"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 15a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 44b" href="/Sanhedrin.44b">44b</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 66a" href="/Sanhedrin.66a">66a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 9b" href="/Makkot.9b"><i>Makkot</i> 9b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 7b" href="/Shevuot.7b"><i>Ševuot</i> 7b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 9b" href="/Menachot.9b"><i>Menahot</i> 9b</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 67a" href="/Menachot.67a">67a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 132a" href="/Chullin.132a"><i>Hulin</i> 132a</a>.) The principle is extended here to read that any even number of restrictions (additions) is an addition (restriction) while any odd number of restrictions (additions) is a restriction (addition); cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Sanhedrin 15a" href="/Rashi_on_Sanhedrin.15a">Rashi in <i>Sanhedrin</i> 15a</a> <i>s. v.</i> חמשה.</i> on the Court is not liable.
<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Here one returns to a discussion of the theme of the Tractate, <i>viz.,</i> the obligation of the High Court, as representatives of the people, to offer a purification sacrifice for a wrong ruling as described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:13-21" href="/Leviticus.4.13-21"><i>Lev.</i> 4:13–21</a>.<br>It is sinful to bring an animal into the Temple precinct which is not dedicated as a sacrifice. For voluntary offerings this presents no problem; one simply has to dedicate them when bringing. But for obligatory offerings it implies that a sacrifice may be presented only if all conditions which make it obligatory are actually fulfilled.</i> They only are liable<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">To bring the sacrifice.</i> for something<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">An official ruling by the Court.</i> that was clear to them and then covered from them<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">They forgot either a precedent or their own ruling.</i>. What is the reason? <i>something was hidden</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:13" href="/Leviticus.4.13"><i>Lev.</i> 4:13</a>. An erring High Priest (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:1-12" href="/Leviticus.4.1-12"><i>Lev.</i> 4:1–12</a>) or ruler (22–26) have to offer a sacrifice if they err inadvertently; the condition that a ruling must have been forgotten is introduced only for the Court.</i>, something that was clear to them and then hidden from them. <sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">There is no problem with the explanation just given. One tries to connect the statement with a discussion about similar rules regarding sacrifices due for violations of either Temple purity or oaths (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1-13" href="/Leviticus.5.1-13"><i>Lev.</i> 5:1–13</a>), where the same condition in mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:2" href="/Leviticus.5.2"><i>Lev.</i> 5:2</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:3" href="/Leviticus.5.3">3</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:4" href="/Leviticus.5.4">4</a>. R. Ismael and R. Aqiba differ in <i>Ševuot</i> 1:2 about the interpretation of the verses, but not about the actual rules.</i> In the opinion of Rebbi Ismael who said, <i>it became hidden from him</i>, therefore he had known, <i>and he knew</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:4" href="/Leviticus.5.4"><i>Lev.</i> 5:4</a>: …<i>an oath which a man would utter without thinking, it became hidden from him, and he knew and realized his guilt</i> …</i>, these are two knowledges<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">One when he uttered the oath and one when he remembered it, separated by a period of oblivion.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Aqiba who said, <i>it became hidden, it became hidden,</i> two times<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">R. Aqiba and R. Ismael actually are not differing in their interpretations; only R. Aqiba argues about violations of Temple purity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:2-3" href="/Leviticus.5.2-3"><i>Lev.</i> 5:2–3</a>) where in both verses oblivion is mentioned but not remembering. However, in Babylonian sources [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 14b" href="/Shevuot.14b"><i>Ševuot</i> 14b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 19a" href="/Keritot.19a"><i>Keritut</i> 19a</a>, <i>Sifra Wayyiqra 2, Pereq</i> 12(7)], R. Ismael is reported to read one about oblivion the impurity and the second oblivion about being in the Temple.</i>, therefore he had knowledge at the beginning and knowledge at the end and oblivion in between, <sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Returning to our topic, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.1.5">Note 22</a>.</i> something that was clear to them and then hidden from them.
They are not liable unless they void part and confirm part<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">If they declared a biblical prohibition as void, nobody would follow them since even schoolchildren would know that this is wrong. But if they were to abolish traditional restrictions, they would be followed. This is explicit in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 4a" href="/Horayot.4a">Babli 4a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Horayot 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Horayot.1.5">Tosephta 1:7</a>, <i>Sifra Wayyiqra 2, Parašah</i> 4(7).</i>. Samuel said, only if they ruled that it was permitted; but not if they ruled that it was not prosecutable<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">This is a commentary on the preceding sentence. Abolishing a biblical commandment entirely means declaring the prohibition as void; declaring it valid but unenforceable means partly confirming it.</i>. They are not liable unless instruction was given from the ashlar hall<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The seat of the High Court; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 1:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.1.4.3"><i>Sanhedrin</i> Chapter 1, Note 345</a>.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the reason of this Tanna: <i>From that place which the Eternal will choose</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 17:10" href="/Deuteronomy.17.10"><i>Deut.</i> 17:10</a>.</i><i>.</i> Rebbi Mana bar Tanḥum said, if a hundred came together, only if they ruled unanimously<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The ruling triggers the obligation of a purification sacrifice only if it was unanimous, including the opinions of the law students sitting before the 72 members of the Court. Mishnah 4 requires in addition that the president of the court be present and voting [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 4b" href="/Horayot.4b">Babli 4b</a>, <i>Sifra Wayyiqra 2, Parašah</i> 4(4).]</i>. There<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 1:2:13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.1.2.13"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 1:2, Note 166</a>. He holds that a ruling of the High Court to intercalate a month based on the testimony of laymen is valid only if it not only is unanimous in fact but also in reason. Concurrent opinions, reaching the same conclusion for different reasons, invalidate the judgment. Why is this not mentioned here? The text confirms the reading of the ms. in <i>Roš Haššanah</i>(2:6 58b l.25) against the one in Sanhedrin.</i>, Rebbi Zeˋira said, only if they all rule for the same reason. And here, what<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">B has the usual formula, “and here he says so?”</i>?
“And a single person went and acted inadvertently<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">When the Court realized its error and changed its ruling, the person acting in good faith on their prior ruling is now faced with the fact that his act violated a biblical commandment, against his intention.</i> following their pronouncement.” Is there an intentional inadvertent action concerning an instruction by the Court<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The formulation of the Mishnah does not make any sense. The person who follows the instructions of the Court does this intentionally; how can he be inadvertent?</i>? Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Our Mishnah, for example, if Simeon ben Azzai<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The paradigm of the know-all; he was ready to answer any question about religious law on the spot. The Babli knows of a number of famous rabbis who tried to imitate him but quickly were confronted with a question they could not answer. He never was ordained; therefore he could not have been part of the Court, but as an outsider he could have pointed out the Court’s error.</i> was sitting before them. Where do we hold? If he knows the entire Torah but does not know this detail, he is not Simeon ben Azzai. If he knows this particular subject but not the entire Torah, he is Simeon ben Azzai for this particular subject. But we must hold that he knows the entire Torah and knows the particular subject, but he errs to believe that the Torah said, after them, after them<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">This is the expression which R. Jehudah ben Bathyra used to convince Hanania ben Hanania, the foremost authority in Babylonia, to accept the overriding authority of the patriarch’s court in Palestine (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.3.1"><i>Nazir</i> 6:13, Note 128</a>; <i>Sanhedrin</i> 1:2, p. 36).</i>. If he errs to believe that the Torah said, after them, after them, he is not Simeon ben Azzai. As we have stated, I could think that if they tell you about right that it is left, and about left that it is right, you should listen to them? The verse says, “to go to the right or to the left;<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">A misquote of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 17:11" href="/Deuteronomy.17.11"><i>Deut.</i> 17:11</a>.</i>” that they should tell you about the right that it is right, and about the left that it is left<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">This is the opposite of the teachings of <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 154, <i>Cant. rabba</i> 1(18), which require one to follow the instructions of the rabbis even if they tell him to believe that left is right. Sound methodology would require one to follow the Talmud in preference to Midrashim. The <i>Sifry</i> text seems to be formulated as a polemic against the Yerushalmi.</i>. What about it<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since the first explanation of the Mishnah was found to be untenable, what would be a reasonable explanation?</i>? Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Hila: Because everywhere for an inadvertent sin one is not liable but for an intentional one is liable, and here even intentionally he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Any</i> action following the wrong teachings of the High Court has the status of unintended action even if it was intended. “Liable” and “not liable” here refer to criminal responsibility, not to obligations to offer sacrifices.</i>.
The colleagues in the name of Samuel. The Mishnah deals with an individual who complements the multitude<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">As explained at the end of the paragraph (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 2b" href="/Horayot.2b">Babli 2b</a> in the name of R. Jehudah). His problem with the prior explanation is that it does not fit the setting of the Mishnah. Since we are referring to sacrifices, the inadvertent sinner, while he is immune to prosecution, is obligated to bring a purification sacrifice; the intentional sinner, who can be punished, is barred from bringing a sacrifice. Therefore, if one compares the intentional to the unintentional sinner, the opposite of the argument of R. Hila should be formulated. (In contrast to the Babli, the Yerushalmi does not care for chronological consistency; Samuel of the first generation opposes R. Hila of the third.)</i>. But any individual who acted on his own is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Horayot 1:2" href="/Tosefta_Horayot.1.2">Tosephta 1:2</a>. If somebody acted in parallel with the ruling of the court but following his own interpretation of the biblical law, he is not liable for a purification sacrifice since in fact he is barred from offering one, and since he happened to act in parallel with the Court’s ruling neither is he prosecutable. Both interpretations of פטור are possible here. There is no reason to change the text which is confirmed by the two Yerushalmi texts and the Tosephta.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, even any single individual who acted on his own brings a female sheep or goat<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Since acting on a faulty interpretation is qualified as acting in error, which for an individual requires the offering of a female sheep or goat (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.1.8">Note 51</a>).</i>. It is difficult. In Samuel’s opinion, would any single individual be atoned by two sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Since, as is explained next, Samuel makes the Court’s offering dependent on whether a majority of the people acted on their instructions or not, it could be that after a number of individuals brought their own sacrifices it turns out that in the end a majority forces the Court to bring its own. But there cannot be more than one sacrifice for one infraction. Since the purification offering is eaten by the priests, it cannot be retroactively nullified.</i>? Rebbi Zeˋira in the name of Samuel: The individual is suspended<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The problem raised in the previous Note cannot occur. The individuals are prevented from bringing their own sacrifices until the situation is cleared.</i>. If a majority ate, the court brings. If a minority ate, each individual brings. For any ruling for which the court [bring] a bull, the individual does not bring a female sheep or goat<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 27:35.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Translation of the text of B, expanding the last sentence of the Leiden ms.</i> For any ruling for which the court bring a bull, the individual does not bring a female sheep; for any ruling for which the court does not bring a bull, the individual brings a female sheep or goat. Samuel explains the Mishnah by majority and minority. If a majority ate<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Referring to the standard example, that the Court allowed some forbidden fat (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.1.3">Note 14</a>) to be eaten.</i>, since the court does not bring a bull, an individual brings a female sheep or goat.
Rebbi Joḥanan explains the Mishnah by instruction from the Court. If the Court ruled to eliminate the entire body, since the Court has to bring a bull a private person does not have to bring a female sheep or goat. If they ruled to confirm part and to eliminate part, since the Court does not bring a bull a private person has to bring a female sheep or goat<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the text is confirmed by B, it cannot be accepted since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.3.1">Mishnah 3</a> explicitly states the opposite (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.1.6">Note 32</a>), and R. Johanan does not disagree with an anonymous Mishnah. Therefore, one has to switch the place of the statements “bring” and “do not bring”. He disagrees with Samuel in that he does not require that a majority of the people act upon the instructions of the Court. If a single person consciously commits a sin by acting upon their instruction, the individual is barred from bringing a sheep and the Court is required to bring a bull.</i>.
Samuel spoke about the Mishnah: “I still am saying, if a minority acted they are liable because the Court will not bring a bull for them. The verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:27" href="/Leviticus.4.27"><i>Lev</i>. 4:27</a>.</i>, <i>[from] the people of the Land.</i> Even [all of them] (part of them)<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The text is a <i>baraita</i> in <i>Sifra Wayyiqra 2, Parašah</i> 7(5), quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 2b" href="/Horayot.2b">Babli, 2b</a>. Usually, a prefix מ “from” is interpreted to mean “not all”. This is behind the reading of B. The reading of the ms., in brackets, is that of <i>Sifra</i> and the Babli; it means that without instruction from the Court, any number of the people, maybe all except the members of the Court, may be required to bring private purification offerings simultaneously. This would not be a case that <i>all of the congregation of Israel err</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:13" href="/Leviticus.4.13"><i>Lev.</i> 4:13</a>); this expression is reserved for pronouncements of the Court.</i>, even most of them.” Rebbi Joḥanan explains the Mishnah: I still am saying, if a minority acted without ruling they are liable because with instruction the Court will not bring a bull<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">A person acting on the instructions of the Court can never bring a purification offering since his action is not inadvertent. If the conditions for such an offering by the Court are not satisfied, no sacrifice at all is due or possible for the action.</i>. Samuel said, but they bring a female sheep or goat. Rebbi Joḥanan said, they do not bring a female sheep or goat<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">This is a repetition of their prior positions. For Samuel, a private offering is due if and only if there is no Court offering. For R. Joḥanan, no offering is possible for action on the instruction of the Court, independent of what the Court has or does not have to do.</i>. According to Samuel, who infers liability from liability, it is understandable. According to Rebbi Joḥanan, who infers liability from exemption<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Samuel is understandable; either the rules of the Court sacrifice or those of the private one do apply; never both together nor none of them. But might R. Johanan, who accepts a situation where both the individual and the Court are exempted from bringing an offering, have a situation where both apply simultaneously? The question is not answered.</i>? The statement of a <i>baraita</i><sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Sifra Wayyiqra 2, Parašah</i>7(7), quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 2a" href="/Horayot.2a">Babli, 2a</a>. The apostate wants to forget; even if he really forgot it was desired by him; he never qualifies for a purification offering, even if his transgression happens to be in a situation for which the Court would have to bring an offering if its conditions were fulfilled.</i> disagrees with Samuel. <i>Or his transgression in which he sinned was made known to him</i><sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:28" href="/Leviticus.4.28"><i>Lev.</i> 4:28</a>. Since he was informed that he failed to commit the sin, he cannot bring a purification sacrifice. His repentance for his sinful intent will be a private matter between him and God.</i>; this excludes the apostate. A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with Samuel, “<i>A person who would transgress; one would transgress; acting he would transgress</i>; these are restrictions<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The quotes are correct in the Yerushalmi text of the Babli <i>editio princeps</i>, but the first of the quotes in the Leiden ms. is a misquote, referring to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:2" href="/Leviticus.4.2"><i>Lev.</i>4:2</a> instead of 4:27. The basic text is in <i>Sifra Wayyiqra Parašah</i> 7(1), referred to in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 2b" href="/Horayot.2b">Babli 2b</a>, discussed in detail <i>Šabbat</i>93a.<br>Chapter 4 in <i>Lev.</i> treats the purification sacrifices for unintentional sin first by the High Priest (vv. 1–12), then the High Court (13–21), then a chief, identified in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.1">Mishnah 3:3</a> as a king (22–26), and finally by a commoner (27–35). V. 27 reads: <i>If one</i> <i>person of the populace transgresses inadvertently, by acting on one prohibitions of the Eternal, and feels guilt.</i> It is noted that the sentence seems to be unnecessarily wordy. Why does it not say simply, “if somebody inadvertently transgresses a prohibition of the Eternal”? The additional words must have a meaning; they describe restrictions. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 93a" href="/Shabbat.93a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>93a</a> one derives from the insistence that <i>one</i> person commit the sin that a violation of a commandment cannot be prosecuted if committed by two persons acting in common, so that no single person commits a punishable act but the combined result is a clear violation,. Such a violation cannot be atoned for by a purification sacrifice. It also is clear that only <i>acts</i> are punishable.<br>In the context here the additional terms are interpreted to mean that only a person acting on his own is required to offer a purification sacrifice; this excludes one who is told by a religious authority that his act is permitted.</i>: the person depending on himself is liable, but one dependent on the Court is not liable.” This disagrees with Samuel and cannot be confirmed<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">If the Court permitted certain intrinsically forbidden things and an individual acted on his own but did what they had allowed, then both the Court and the individual have to bring sacrifices for the same kind of action.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the Court gave an instruction; then they realized that they erred and reversed themselves<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Publicly.</i>; whether they brought their atonement or they did not bring their atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifice prescribed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:13-21" href="/Leviticus.4.13-21"><i>Lev.</i>4:13–21</a>.</i>, another person went and acted on their instructions<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">He followed the original instructions after the Court had reversed itself. R. Simeon holds that the responsibility remains the Court’s as long as not all of Israel were duly informed of the new ruling.</i>, Rebbi Simeon declares him not liable, but Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">One should read with the Babli and many independent Mishnah mss. “R. Eleazar” (ben Shamuaˋ), since the third generation R. Simeon cannot be quoted preceding the first generation R. Eliezer.</i> says, it is in doubt. What is the doubt? If he remained at home, he is liable; if he went overseas he is not liable. Rebbi Aqiba said, I agree in this case that he is closer to not being liable than being liable. Ben Azzai asked him, what is the difference between him and the one staying at home? For one who stays at home might be informed<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">It would be his duty to stay informed. The one who is far away is still depending on the earlier ruling of the Court; he is not liable for a sacrifice according to everybody. The difference between R. Aqiba and ben Azzai is that the latter requires a sacrifice from anyone who could have known of the reversal whereas the former requires it only from one who should have known.</i> but the other one could not be informed.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If the Court gave an instruction; then they realized that they erred,” etc. Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Our Mishnah, for example, if Simeon ben Azzai<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The paradigm of the know-all; he was ready to answer any question about religious law on the spot. The Babli knows of a number of famous rabbis who tried to imitate him but quickly were confronted with a question they could not answer. He never was ordained; therefore he could not have been part of the Court, but as an outsider he could have pointed out the Court’s error.</i> was sitting before them. Where do we hold? If he removed<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">In general, the <i>Piel</i> form סַלֵּק, from the root סלק “to raise, lift”, means “to remove (from office)”, comparable to German <i>entheben.</i> Here it must mean, “to silence the opposing party” either by a convincing argument of the single opponent, or by a formal judgment of the High Court.</i> them, their instruction would be invalid. If they removed him, his instruction would be invalid. But we hold in the case that each side stands by its answer. For him, their instruction is no instruction, for they did not remove him<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Since they could not convince him, he does not have to follow them against his better knowledge; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.1.7">Note 43</a>.</i>. For others it is an instruction, for he did not remove them. Does this not disagree with Rebbi Mana bar Tanḥum, since Rebbi Mana bar Tanḥum said, if a hundred came together, only if they instructed unanimously<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The ruling triggers the obligation of a purification sacrifice only if it was unanimous, including the opinions of the law students sitting before the 72 members of the Court. Mishnah 4 requires in addition that the president of the court be present and voting [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 4b" href="/Horayot.4b">Babli 4b</a>, <i>Sifra Wayyiqra 2, Parašah</i> 4(4).]</i>? One explains it, that he was not present<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">At the vote.</i>. Does this invalidate<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The text of B is more intelligible: If he was not present, does this invalidate the vote?</i>? He explains it following Rebbi, since Rebbi said, no one invalidates but the distinguished member of the Court (at Lydda) [only]<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The ms. text, בלוד, “at Lydda” makes no sense since the High Court must sit in the ashlar hall on the Temple Mount. One has to read with B בלבד “only”. The president of the Court is the only one for whom no substitute can be found.</i>. Since Rebbi Mana bar Tanḥum said, if a hundred came together, only if they instructed unanimously; is it the same in retraction or by majority? If it is obvious for you<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Read כַּד “if it is” for כַּת “group” in the text. The reading of B, צַייְתֵי “they obey” might be acceptable; since everywhere a majority opinion of religious authorities is to be followed, it is obvious that a retraction by a majority has to be followed. The original instruction also would have had to be followed if rendered by a majority of the Court; it is only the obligation of a sacrifice which is triggered by a unanimous vote.</i> by majority, what kind of majority? The majority of those who instructed or the majority of those remaining? How is this? If there were a hundred but ten of them had died. If you say, a majority of those who instructed, 51. If you say, a majority of those remaining, 46.
<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph also is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:1:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.1.2-10"><i>Gittin</i> 7:1</a>, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:1:3-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.1.3-4">Notes 10–21</a>. In the translation, the words in brackets follow the text of B. The ms. text here has quite a number of scribal errors. A parallel is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 12b" href="/Zevachim.12b">Babli, <i>Zebahim</i> 12b</a>.</i> If somebody had selected his purification sacrifice when he became a deaf-mute, or insane, or an apostate, or the Court rules that fat may be eaten, Rebbi Joḥanan said, his sacrifice of purification is pushed aside<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">This version is confirmed in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 11a" href="/Horayot.11a">Babli, 11a</a>.</i>, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, his sacrifice of purification is not pushed aside. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rebbi Aḥa switches traditions, to avoid that a word of Rebbi Joḥanan contradict his own word. For Rebbi Simeon bar Abba said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: One sprinkles the blood of a purification sacrifice or a reparation sacrifice for a person [terminally ill]<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">In the ms: “a cup”.</i>. The Rebbi of Caesarea said, Rebbi Ḥiyya and [Rebbi] Immi, [one] switches and one says like [this] Tanna.
He who switches has none of these difficulties<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The one difficulty pointed out in the previous paragraph that R. Johanan accepts the sacrifice on behalf of a dying person even though the sacrifice of a dead person is impossible. The second difficulty is that R. Johanan permits the writing and delivery of a divorce document to a wife whose husband became insane after he had ordered the document to be written.</i>. For him who says, his sacrifice of purification is not pushed aside<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">If the sacrifice becomes invalid, there is no problem. But since a purification sacrifice cannot be offered voluntarily, even according to him who said that the sacrifice remains valid, are the officiating priests not required to refuse the sacrifice after the Court ruled that the action for which it is offered was not forbidden?</i>, who would accept it from him? He has to wait until the Court retracts, or if he was a Cohen who served, sacrificed, and atoned<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">A layman can do nothing but wait whether the Court change its mind. But a Cohen can bring his own sacrifice and eat its meat for atonement if at the moment of the action it would have been sinful for anybody. Since he does not have to explain his reasons to a priest, he is not dependent on the Court.</i>. If he was Simeon ben Azzai<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">A layman who knows that the Court erred is not freed from an obligation to bring a sacrifice by the Court’s ruling; nevertheless he cannot find a priest who would accept it before the Court changes its mind.</i>, who would accept it from him? He has to wait until the Court retracts, for him who says, his sacrifice of purification is not pushed aside.
Are consequences of a ruling like the ruling? Do ruling within ruling combine<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of these expressions will be explained by examples in the text.</i>? How is that? If the public ate forbidden fat and designated their sacrifices. If you say that consequences of a ruling are like the ruling, the Court is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Most of the people ate forbidden fat (which makes the sinner subject to extirpation and therefore requires a sacrifice if inadvertent, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.4.1">Mishnah 3:7</a>) without a ruling of the Court, and they already had dedicated their sacrifices when the Court ruled that eating fat is permitted. When it rescinded its ruling, it turned out that in the meantime nobody had acted on their instruction. According to the opinion that the ruling of the Court pushed aside the dedicated sacrifices, the Court certainly has to bring their own sacrifice since they invalidated the private sacrifices. But if one holds that the private sacrifices were re-installed upon recission, then if the rule is that consequences of instructions are like instructions, the Court still is liable for a sacrifice for preventing the purification offerings to be brought in the meantime, even though this was not included or intended in the original ruling. Otherwise, the private offerings will now be brought and the Court’s ruling is eliminated without further consequences.</i>. If you say that consequences of a ruling are not like the ruling, the Court is not liable. The following should be obvious to you: the consequences of a ruling are like the ruling<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">This should not be so obvious since it implies that a purification sacrifice which for outside reasons could not be offered is reinstated when the outside reason disappears.</i>. Do ruling within ruling combine with each other? How is that? If the Court ruled that the fat in the right side kidney was permitted but that of the left side and its cover<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The fat in lumps outside the kidney which is forbidden together with the fat embedded in the kidney lobes.</i> was forbidden; then they reversed themselves and said the opposite. Most ate the first time, and most the second time. If you say that they combine, they are liable for one. But if you say that they do not combine, they are liable for two<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">If the Court followed one wrong decision with another wrong one on the same subject, it is an unresolved question whether they have to offer one or two sacrifices when they finally see their errors.</i>. Do two instructions about one kind of work<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">In B: “Two instructions about one kind of <i>sin</i>.” While the text as it stands is difficult, the text of B is impossible since, as explained in <i>Makkot</i> 3:11, while committing one sin one may commit any number of others at the same time.<br>The following example makes it clear that instead of “two instructions about one kind of work” one has to read “one instruction about two kinds of work.”</i> combine? How is that? If a majority ate and a majority slaughtered<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that one contemplates the case that the Court decided that one does not have to keep the Day of Atonement. This one instruction implicitly allows both work (slaughter) and eating on that day; both are sins punishable by extirpation.</i>. In Rebbi Meïr’s opinion they are once liable; in Rebbi Simeon’s opinion they are twice liable<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The difference between R. Meïr, the presumed author of the anonymous statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.1">Mishnah 3:3</a>, and R. Simeon is that R. Meïr holds that the sin determines the sacrifice whereas R. Simeon holds that the status of the sinner at the moment he becomes aware of his sin is determining. Since the Court issued one statement, they have to bring one sacrifice. Since the people became aware of two kinds of sins, two sacrifices are due. This shows that R. Simeon does not hold that the consequences of an instruction have the status of the instruction.</i>. If a minority ate the first time and a minority the second time. In Rebbi Meïr’s opinion they are liable; in Rebbi Simeon’s opinion they are not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">If the two majorities together form a majority, the conditions for the Court to be liable are satisfied; for R. Simeon the two minorities cannot be combined.</i>.
Rebbi Zeˋira said, the time span is between them<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">This refers back to the Mishnah, about a person who acted on the Court’s instruction after the Court reversed itself. It is stated in <i>Sifra Wayyiqra 2, Paršetah</i>7(3) that R. Simeon does not hold him liable (for a sacrifice) but R. Meïr does. It is explained that neither does R. Simeon free him forever nor R. Meïr hold him liable immediately, but R. Meïr holds him liable if he could have heard, and R. Simeon only if he had ample time to be informed. The reading of the ms., שֶׁיִּשְׁפַּע עַד, i. e., “until he had ample (time to be informed)” is preferable over that of B, “until he actually was informed.”</i>. Rebbi Meïr said, one gives him time until he may hear. Rebbi Simeon said, until he must have heard, and we have stated so: If the Court instructed in the upper market and a single person was in the lower market, or the Court on the ground floor and a single person on the upper floor, he is not liable until he actually heard.
In Rebbi Aqiba’s opinion it remains a doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to R. Aqiba’s statement in the Mishnah. Even though the position of one who could have known but did not inform himself looks as if he should not be liable, a doubt remains and in fact he has to bring a suspended reparation offering (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.1.4">Note 19</a>).</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, if he stands between two domains, between two domains of the Land of Israel and domains of the Land of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">The reading in B is: Between domains of the Land of Israel and domains outside the Land. In this version, “overseas” mentioned in the Mishnah is interpreted to mean, “outside the Land.” The text of the ms. is to be read to mean that a person living outside of urban centers in the Land of Israel has the same status as a city dweller on an overseas trip, since he is far from sources of information.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:6:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.6.2-7"><i>Pesahim</i> 7:6</a> (34c line 66), following the wording of B.</i> Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. For instructions they considered the settlement of the Land of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The definition of “majority” which would trigger the liability of the Court for a sacrifice for issuing false instruction counts only the Jewish population of the Land of Israel. Since the verse from <i>1Kings</i> is not quoted here (in contrast to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 3a" href="/Horayot.3a">Babli, 3a</a>), the definition of the Land of Israel is that given in <i>Ševi</i>ˋ<i>it</i> 6:1, Notes 31–51.</i>. For impurity they considered the majority of those coming to the Temple precinct<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 94b" href="/Pesachim.94b"><i>Babli Pesaḥim</i> 94b</a>. A private sacrifice may be presented in the Temple only by a person ritually pure. But the Passover lamb has the status of a public sacrifice; if most of the public are impure (of a kind which cannot be remedied by simple immersion in water), the sacrifice is slaughtered and eaten in impurity (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 9:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.9.2.6"><i>Nazir</i> 9:2 Note 66</a>.)</i>. How? Do they estimate every group or only the first group? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, when they are still outside they estimate themselves<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Temple courtyard was rather small, the Passover lambs were slaughtered in three groups. Making the count depending on the composition of the groups would lead to the paradoxical situation that a first group might be permitted to slaughter and eat the lamb in impurity while from a second group only the pure members are admitted and have to follow the rules of purity. Also, it is impossible to make the decision depending on “those in the Temple court” since only pure persons could enter the Temple precinct in the absence of a <i>prior</i> finding that most of Israel were impure.</i>. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: For appearance<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The assembly of all of Israel in a Sabbatical year (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 31:10-13" href="/Deuteronomy.31.10-13"><i>Deut.</i> 31:10–13</a>) could proceed in impurity if most of Israel in the domain of Solomon’s empire were impure.</i> they consider from Levo- Ḥamat to the brook of Egypt. Rebbi Tanḥuma in the name of Rebbi Ḥuna: The reason of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi is, <i>at that time Solomon celebrated the holiday, and all of Israel</i>, <sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 8:65" href="/I_Kings.8.65"><i>1K.</i> 8:65</a> (misquoted in B.)</i> etc.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the Court ruled to uproot an entire subject; if they said, the menstruating woman is not mentioned in the Torah, Sabbath is not mentioned in the Torah, idolatry is not mentioned in the Torah, they are not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Since anything written in the Torah is public knowledge and nobody would listen to them.</i>. If they ruled to eliminate part and to confirm part, they are liable. How is that? If they said, the menstruating woman is mentioned in the Torah but one who copulates with one who is watching a day to the next day is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">In rabbinic medical theory, the minimum time which must elapse between one menstrual period and the next is the seven days of the <i>niddah</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:19" href="/Leviticus.15.19"><i>Lev.</i>15:19</a>) followed by another 11 days. If a woman has a discharge on one of these 11 days, she is not classified as <i>niddah</i> but as <i>zavah,</i> whose rules are spelled out in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:25-30" href="/Leviticus.15.25-30"><i>Lev.</i>15:25–30</a>. Since the verse speaks of a discharge of <i>many days,</i> it is concluded that the full rules of <i>zavah</i> only apply after 3 days. For the first and second discharges in that 11 day period, the woman is called “watching one day to the next day”. For a day she is under the rules of <i>niddah</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:25" href="/Leviticus.15.25"><i>Lev.</i> 15:25</a>) and therefore forbidden to her husband. But since the verse uses the expression <i>all the days of the flow of her impurity shall be like the days of her menstruation,</i> one could think that she is impure only during the day and not during the following night, or that a discharge during the night does not make her impure. This is clearly a matter of rabbinic interpretation.</i>; Sabbath is mentioned in the Torah but one who brings from a private domain to a public domain is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">The pentateuchal root of the prohibition to carry from a private to the public domain is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 16:29" href="/Exodus.16.29"><i>Ex.</i> 16:29</a>, <i>nobody should go out from his place,</i> which is explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 17:22" href="/Jeremiah.17.22"><i>Jer.</i> 17:22</a> by <i>do not move a load from your houses.</i> Since as a matter of principle prophetic utterances should not be used as legal texts, the ruling of the Court could not be dismissed out of hand.</i>; idolatry is mentioned in the Torah but one who prostrates himself is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">This is more difficult to understand since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 17:3" href="/Deuteronomy.17.3"><i>Deut.</i> 17:3</a> clearly defines prostrating oneself in idolatry as a capital crime. Therefore, one has to agree with Maimonides’s Commentary that the Court changed the definition of “prostration”, e. g., ruling that kneeling down, bowing the head to the ground, is not punishable as long as one does not lie on the ground with outstretched hands and feet.</i>; these are liable for it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:13" href="/Leviticus.4.13"><i>Lev.</i> 4:13</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Braita d'Rabbi Yishmael 2" href="/Sifra,_Braita_d'Rabbi_Yishmael.2"><i>Sifra Wayyiqra 2, Parašah</i>4(7–8</a>).</i> <i>something was hidden</i>, something but not an entire subject.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If the Court ruled to uproot an entire subject,” etc. Rebbi Ḥizqiah said, “of a subject,” not the entire subject. Rebbi Hila said, “of the commandments”, not entire commandments<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:13" href="/Leviticus.4.13"><i>Lev.</i> 4:13</a>, R. Hizqiah reads דָּבָר וְנֶעֱלַם as וְנֶעֱלַם מִדָּבָר, presupposing a script which does not differentiate between regular and final <i>mem.</i> R. Hila’s comment is really unnecessary since מִכָּל־מִצְוֹת already means “of any commandments” but not entire commandments. In all situations, prefix <i>mem</i> is read as partitive, some but not all; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.2.3"><i>Nazir</i> 5:4 Note 105</a>.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">The following text also is found in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 5:1:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.5.1.2-5"><i>Sotah</i> 5:1</a>, explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.1.10">Notes 8–10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.1.8"><i>Nazir</i>5:1 Note 56</a>.</i> Is that written? As Rebbi Immi said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: For interpretation, one removes from the beginning of the paragraph to its end. Rebbi Ḥananiah in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah: Even a middle word. <i>You have to pour oil on it, it is a flour offering,</i> to include all flour offerings for pouring<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Wayyiqra 1 Pereq</i> 12 on <i>Lev.</i>2:6. The ms. text follows the argument of <i>Sifra</i> while B reproduces the text of <i>Sotah</i> and <i>Nazir.</i> The argument of <i>Sifra</i> has no connection with the theories of RR. Johanan and Jeremiah; it is a straightforward reading of the verse. Since it is stated that one has to pour oil on the bread crumbs <i>because it is a flour-offering,</i> it follows that a flour-offering requires pouring oil over it unless it be explicitly excluded as in the purification offering of v. 5:11.</i>.
But would you not have eliminated the entire notion of one who watches a day for the next day<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">The literal text of the Mishnah could be read as a hypothetical ruling that there be no restriction on relations between a man and a temporary <i>zavah.</i> But since the verse declares the one who watches a day for the next day as being under the rules of <i>niddah</i> for whom such relations are forbidden (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:19" href="/Leviticus.18.19"><i>Lev.</i> 18:19</a>), such a ruling would in effect eliminate all rules for the one who watches a day for the next day. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.3.1">Note 99</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 4a" href="/Horayot.4a">Babli 4a</a>.</i>? If they said, the night is permitted but the day is forbidden. Would you not have eliminated the entire notion of bending? Samuel bar Abba said, if they said, one cubit is permitted but two are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">It is not at all clear to what the <i>hapax</i>שחיחה refers. Since in the Mishnah the Sabbath is mentioned in second place, a reasonable reference would be to Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 1:1 which describes forbidden transactions on the Sabbath as, e. g., a person standing behind a window handing a parcel to another who is standing outside. While the distance by which the parcel is moved is irrelevant in practice since only the fact counts that it is transported across the border line between public and private domains, a ruling that a minimum distance be required for the transfer to be a violation of Sabbath law is thinkable.</i>. Would you not have eliminated the entire notion of prostrating? If they said, it is permitted to prostrate oneself but forbidden to sink down<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">While prostrating oneself before an idol is certainly subject to a biblical prohibition, there is no biblical definition of what constitutes prostrating. Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 19:18" href="/I_Kings.19.18"><i>1K.</i>19:18</a> shows that going down on one’s knees is forbidden worship, it is possible to imagine a ruling that going down onone’s knees is prosecutable but other forms of prostration are not.</i>. Would you not have eliminated the entire notion of taking out? Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac said, if they said, one dried fig is permitted but two dried figs are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the prohibitions of the Sabbath. While the prohibition of moving things from one place to another is only one of the 49 categories of forbidden actions, its rules in effect cover half of Tractate <i>Šabbat</i> and all of Tractate ˋ<i>Eruvin.</i> Moving minute quantities from domain to domain is not punishable. For example, transporting vegetable seeds in a volume less that of a dried fig is not punishable (Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 9:7). The long list of minimal quantities is traditional, not biblical. A wrong ruling in these matters does not abolish the principle that some minimal quantity is defined for everything.</i>. This follows him who said that bringing in and taking out are the same<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody agrees that “transporting” for the rules of the Sabbath comprises taking up, moving, and putting down. There is a discussion at the start of Tractate <i>Šabbat</i> (1:1, 2b l.11 ff.) whether the inclusion of putting down is scriptural or is a matter of indirect inference. If one accepts that “taking up” implies “putting down”, then the formulation of the Mishnah covers rulings both about taking up and putting down; but if the biblical status of “putting down” is different from “taking up”, the latter should have been mentioned. An answer is unnecessary since the consensus is that “taking up” implies “putting down”.</i>. But for him who said that bringing in and taking out are two notions, would you not have eliminated the entire notion of bringing in? Rebbi Yose said, not that they said that it was permitted to eat fat; they knew that it is forbidden to eat fat, but the Torah gave permission to the Court to instruct<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">While fat is not mentioned in the Mishnah, R. Yose explains how the Court might be liable for a false ruling concerning fat. The only fat (חֵלֶב) forbidden for consumption is (a) fat of domestic animals which for any sacrifice would be burned on the altar and (b) the fat in which the hip tendons are embedded. All other fat is permitted (שׁוּמָן). The exact definition of each category is a matter of rabbinic tradition and as such within the purview of the Court.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked, the [amount of] an olive today and of two olives tomorrow<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">He asks whether the entire discussion about abolishing an entire commandment or only a detail makes any sense. If they would permit eating one olive-sized piece of <i>ḥēlev</i> today, two tomorrow, etc., they might come to disestablish the entire commandment by a succession of steps, none of which can be classified as total negation of the commandment.</i>? What about a prophet and seducer<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">To whom the distinction between abolishing a commandment and modifying it also applies, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 4b" href="/Horayot.4b">Babli 4b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 11:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.11.6.2"><i>Sanhedrin</i>11:8, Note 112</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sanhedrin 14:4" href="/Tosefta_Sanhedrin.14.4">Tosephta <i>Sanhedrin</i>14:13</a>).</i>? I could think that if they said to you, do not put on phylacteries today, put them on tomorrow<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">This would be a situation in which the accredited prophet could eliminate a commandment in steps, similar to the Court ruling on fat. Since phylacteries are mentioned in connection with the study of Torah (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:8" href="/Deuteronomy.6.8"><i>Deut.</i> 6:8</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:18" href="/Deuteronomy.11.18">11:18</a>), wearing them is a daily biblical commandment.</i>, that you should listen to them. The verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">There is no such verse. The reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 13:6" href="/Deuteronomy.13.6"><i>Deut.</i> 13:6</a> where the reading is בָּ֑הּ לָלֶ֣כֶת.</i>, <i>to walk in them,</i> in all of them, not only in part of them; you would have eliminated the notion of that entire day. You can say that this is not elimination of the entire subject. And here it is not elimination of the entire subject<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Bun bar Hiyya’s question could have been asked about any example in the Mishnah, but the Mishnah makes a distinction between eliminating and modifying a commandment. It is true that a modification is only a modification even if its open-ended iteration could result in eliminating the commandment.</i>. Rebbi Mana understood it from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">It is not at all clear to what the <i>hapax</i>שחיחה refers. Since in the Mishnah the Sabbath is mentioned in second place, a reasonable reference would be to Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 1:1 which describes forbidden transactions on the Sabbath as, e. g., a person standing behind a window handing a parcel to another who is standing outside. While the distance by which the parcel is moved is irrelevant in practice since only the fact counts that it is transported across the border line between public and private domains, a ruling that a minimum distance be required for the transfer to be a violation of Sabbath law is thinkable.</i>; Samuel bar Abba said, if they said, one cubit is permitted but two are forbidden. You can say that this is not elimination of the entire subject. And here it is not elimination of the entire subject.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the Court ruled but one of them knew that it was in error and he told them, you are erring<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Then the ruling of the Court is not unanimous; no sacrifice is due; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.2.2">Note 36</a>.</i>, or that the distinguished member of the Court was not there<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">The ruling is not <i>ex cathedra,</i> <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.4.2">Note 71</a>.</i>, or that one of them was a proselyte, or a bastard, or a Gibeonite<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">While they can be civil judges, they are not eligible for the High Court as explained in the Halakhah.</i>, or a childless old man<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">He should not be member of a criminal court since he never raised children and as a consequence never learned to have a positive attitude towards misbehaving people.</i>, it is not liable since it is said here “congregation” and it is said there “congregation”<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">“Here” is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:13" href="/Leviticus.4.13"><i>Lev.</i> 4:13</a>; “there” is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 35:12" href="/Numbers.35.12"><i>Num.</i>35:12</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 35:24" href="/Numbers.35.24">24</a>,25 containing the rules of criminal courts.</i>. Since in the congregation mentioned there, all of them were worthy of ordination, also the congregation here all have to be worthy of ordination.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If the Court ruled but one of them knew that it was in error,” etc. The Mishnah is Rebbi’s, since Rebbi said, no one invalidates but the distinguished member of the Court (at Lydda) [only]<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The ms. text, בלוד, “at Lydda” makes no sense since the High Court must sit in the ashlar hall on the Temple Mount. One has to read with B בלבד “only”. The president of the Court is the only one for whom no substitute can be found.</i>. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:24" href="/Numbers.15.24"><i>Num.</i> 15:24</a>, detailing the rules governing the sacrifice of a goat if the Court unintentionally permitted idolatry. This is taken as biblical proof that the Court cannot rule in the absence of its president.</i>: <i>If from the eyes of the congregation,</i> from him who is appointed as eyes of the congregation. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 11:16" href="/Numbers.11.16"><i>Num.</i> 11:16</a>, the appointment of the 70 Elders, the paradigm for the High Court. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 4b" href="/Horayot.4b">Babli 4b</a></i>, <i>they shall stand there with you.</i> Just as you are neither proselyte, nor Gibeonite, nor a bastard<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">As son of a man and his aunt, Moses would have been a bastard if his parents had married after the promulgation of Torah laws.</i>. Proselytes are reasonable, but bastards? does the court appoints bastards? Rav Ḥuna said, when they breached the rules and appointed<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">The exclusion of proselytes, Gibeonites, and bastards is strongly recommended but a breach does not invalidate the appointment.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanania, Rebbi Mana. One said, as part of the Seventy; the other said, apart from the Seventy<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the exclusion is prescriptive; an appointment would be invalid.</i>. He who said, apart from the Seventy, is understandable. But he who said, as part of the Seventy, therefore not apart from the Seventy? Since he is not suitable for ordination, he is considered like a stone<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">If their vote cannot be counted, then automatically not all who are present are voting; therefore the false ruling will never trigger the obligation of a sacrifice.</i>.

Halakhah 5
