<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the Court ruled in error and the public acted in error, they have to bring a bull; intentionally but they acted in error, they bring a female sheep or goat; in error but they acted intentionally, they are not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">All purification sacrifices have a stated prerequisite, <i>viz</i>., that the sin to be expiated was committed unintentionally (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:2" href="/Leviticus.4.2"><i>Lev.</i> 4:2</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:13" href="/Leviticus.4.13">13</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:22" href="/Leviticus.4.22">22</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:27" href="/Leviticus.4.27">27</a>). If both Court and public acted in error, the conditions for a sacrifice by the Court are satisfied. If the Court intentionally gave a false ruling, their sin cannot be atoned by a sacrifice; the public are forced to bring individual sacrifices. If the Court ruled in error but the public, although realizing the error, intentionally followed the false ruling, the Court cannot bring a sacrifice since the public did not follow their intent, and the public is barred from any sacrifice since they did not act in error.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If the Court ruled in error,” etc. Does this not disagree with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish? Since Rebbi Immi said in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Our Mishnah, for example, if Simeon ben Azzai<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The paradigm of the know-all; he was ready to answer any question about religious law on the spot. The Babli knows of a number of famous rabbis who tried to imitate him but quickly were confronted with a question they could not answer. He never was ordained; therefore he could not have been part of the Court, but as an outsider he could have pointed out the Court’s error.</i> was sitting before them<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">He would immediately have pointed out the error; then one would be back at the situation of Mishnah 4; the case of Mishnah 5 never could arise.</i>. “Intentionally but they acted in error.” Is there intentional misdeed or error with respect of an instruction by the Court<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">It already was stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.1.1">Mishnah 1</a> that a person acting upon the instructions of the Court is never liable for a purification offering, irrespective of the quality of the Court’s ruling. Why should the individual be held liable?</i>? The colleagues in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: If not most of the public accepted it<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Then the main condition for a sacrifice of the Court is not fulfilled; automatically there is no valid ruling of the Court, only actions of individuals.</i>. Rebbi Zeˋira in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, if they rebelled against their instruction<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">A High Court without authority is no High Court; it cannot claim to be the subject of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:13" href="/Leviticus.4.13"><i>Lev.</i> 4:13</a>.</i>. What is the difference between them? If they first accepted and then rebelled. In the opinions of the colleagues, since they rebelled, they are not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Since at the moment a sacrifice would be due the conditions are not met, the Court is no longer liable.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Zeˋira, since at the first moment they accepted it, those are liable<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Since the authority of the Court is acknowledged, a later rejection does not change the fact of the Court’s false ruling, and the Court is liable.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the Court ruled and all the public or a majority acted on their instruction, they bring a bull; or in a matter of idolatry a bull and a goat<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">Sacrifices for unintended sins committed by the entire community are prescribed both in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:13-31" href="/Leviticus.4.13-31"><i>Lev.</i> 4:13–31</a> (a bull) and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:22-26" href="/Numbers.15.22-26"><i>Num.</i> 15:22–26</a> (a bull as elevation offering and a goat as purification offering). The verses in <i>Num.</i> are interpreted to refer to the sin of idolatry since that is the only sin by which in one action one violates <i>all</i> commandments (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:22" href="/Numbers.15.22"><i>Num.</i> 15:22</a>). Since the sacrifice for violating all commandments cannot be less than that for violating one commandment, it is logical that the sacrifice for idolatry must be more than the regular sacrifice <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:13-31" href="/Leviticus.4.13-31"><i>Lev.</i>4:13–31</a>. (<i>Sifry Num.</i> 111)</i>, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Jehudah says, the Twelve Tribes bring twelve bulls and in a matter of idolatry the Twelve Tribes bring twelve bulls and twelve goats<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">As discussed in the Halakhah, this is a problem of definition of עֵדָה and קָהָל.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, thirteen oxen, and in a matter of idolatry thirteen bulls and thirteen goats; a bull and a goat for each tribe; a bull and a goat for the Court.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If the Court ruled and all the public acted,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">The entire Halakhah is shortened from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:6:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.6.2-7"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 7:6</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 9:9-14" href="/Numbers.9.9-14"><i>Num.</i> 9:9–14</a> prescribes that individuals who were impure on Passover have to bring their Passover sacrifice a month later, on the Second Passover celebrated on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of the Second Month. It is concluded that if the entire people are impure, the Second Passover is impossible and everybody celebrates the (First) Passover in impurity in the Temple. The problem then arises which percentage of the people have to be impure so that they represent the entire people; just as here the question is, how many people do have to follow the erroneous ruling of the Court so that “all of Israel were in error” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:13" href="/Leviticus.4.13"><i>Lev.</i> 4:13</a>).</i> Who stated “a majority”? Rebbi Meïr, as it was stated: Either half of the tribes or half of each tribe, if only it be a majority<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that everywhere 50%+1 represent “all”; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 5b" href="/Horayot.5b">Babli 5b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, half of each tribe, but only a majority of entire tribes<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">The language is somewhat self-contradictory. He also requires that a majority of Israel follow the erroneous ruling but in addition he demands that in a majority of tribes a majority follow the ruling. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 5b" href="/Horayot.5b">Babli 5b</a>.</i>. One tribe drags all tribes<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">If one tribe has more members than all the others together, the action of one tribe triggers the obligation of all of them. He does not hold that the law about erroneous rulings of the High Court became moot with the exile of the Ten Tribes. Even later, when the tribe of Jehudah represented the overwhelming majority of Israel, a majority of the people can be considered a majority of all twelve tribes and the majority of Judeans triggers the obligation for all tribes.</i>. Rebbi Meïr says, all tribes are called “the public”<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">The purification sacrifice for an erroneous ruling by the Court has to be brought by “the public” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:14" href="/Leviticus.4.14"><i>Lev.</i> 4:14</a>). The difference of opinions in the Mishnah is traced to different interpretations of this notion. R. Meïr holds that only the entire people of Israel qualify as “public”; RR. Jehudah and Simeon consider each tribe as a separate public. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 5b" href="/Horayot.5b">Babli 5b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 80a" href="/Pesachim.80a"><i>Pesahim</i>80a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 15a" href="/Menachot.15a"><i>Menahot</i> 15a</a>).</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, each single tribe is called “public”. And Rebbi Simeon follows Rebbi Jehudah. Just as Rebbi Jehudah said, each single tribe is called “public”, so Rebbi Simeon says, each single tribe is called “public”. What is between them? Dragging. Rebbi Jehudah says, one tribe drags all tribes<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">If one tribe has more members than all the others together, the action of one tribe triggers the obligation of all of them. He does not hold that the law about erroneous rulings of the High Court became moot with the exile of the Ten Tribes. Even later, when the tribe of Jehudah represented the overwhelming majority of Israel, a majority of the people can be considered a majority of all twelve tribes and the majority of Judeans triggers the obligation for all tribes.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, one tribe does not drag all tribes<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore he requires a separate sacrifice for the people of Israel in their entirety.</i>. [Even though Rebbi Jehudah says, one tribe drags all tribes,]<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Missing in the ms., from B and the <i>Pesaḥim</i> text; required by the context.</i> he agrees that only if the ruling came from the ashlar hall<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Even though each tribe has to bring its own sacrifice, the ruling of a tribal High Court cannot trigger an obligation of any other tribe; only the Court sitting at the central sanctuary has this power.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, the reason of that Tanna: <i>From this place which the Eternal will choose</i><sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 17:10" href="/Deuteronomy.17.10"><i>Deut.</i> 17:10</a>.</i><i>.</i> Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Bejamin ben Levi: The verse supports him who said that each tribe is called “public”, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 35:11" href="/Genesis.35.11"><i>Gen.</i> 35:11</a>, said to Jacob after the birth of 11 sons. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 5b" href="/Horayot.5b">Babli 5b</a>.</i>: <i>A people and a public of peoples will come from you,</i> and Benjamin was not yet born. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, just as they differ here, so they differ about impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">The entire Halakhah is shortened from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:6:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.6.2-7"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 7:6</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 9:9-14" href="/Numbers.9.9-14"><i>Num.</i> 9:9–14</a> prescribes that individuals who were impure on Passover have to bring their Passover sacrifice a month later, on the Second Passover celebrated on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of the Second Month. It is concluded that if the entire people are impure, the Second Passover is impossible and everybody celebrates the (First) Passover in impurity in the Temple. The problem then arises which percentage of the people have to be impure so that they represent the entire people; just as here the question is, how many people do have to follow the erroneous ruling of the Court so that “all of Israel were in error” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:13" href="/Leviticus.4.13"><i>Lev.</i> 4:13</a>).</i>, as we have stated: If the public was half pure and half impure; pure [people] celebrate the first [Passover] and impure the second. Rebbi Jehudah said, the pure ones celebrate for themselves, and the impure ones celebrate for themselves<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Both offer their sacrifices in the Temple, in separate groups. For this to happen, the number of pure people in Jerusalem on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan must be <i>exactly</i> equal to the number of impure ones.</i>. They told him, there is no split Passover; either all celebrate in impurity or all celebrate in purity. Who is “they told him”? Following Rebbi Jehudah? As it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 2:2" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.2.2">Mishnah <i>Menahot</i> 2:2</a>.</i>: “If one of the loaves or one of the (leftovers) [orders]<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is from the ms.; the text in brackets is from B, the text in <i>Pesahim,</i> and all sources of the Mishnah; it is the only one which makes sense.<br>The Mishnah speaks of the two public cereal offerings which have to be baked, <i>viz.,</i> the weekly show-bread and the two leavened loaves presented at Pentecost. The 12 show-breads were presented in two rows, here called “orders” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 24:6" href="/Leviticus.24.6"><i>Lev.</i>24:6</a>).</i> became impure, Rebbi Jehudah said, both have to be brought to be burned<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">Outside the Temple precinct.</i> for a public offering cannot be split. But the Sages say, the impure in its impurity, and the pure shall be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">By the officiating priests.</i>” Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, who is “they told him”? The Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Since the opinions of R. Jehudah and his opponents in <i>Pesaḥim</i> are the opinions of his opponents and R. Jehudah in <i>Menahot,</i> both seem to contradict themselves. One has to conclude that they agree in principle and they only differ about the practical applications of their theory. In this sense, B reads: The Sages <i>acting</i> in the sense of R. Jehudah.</i> who argue like Rebbi Jehudah.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the Court ruled and seven tribes or a majority<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">Either seven tribes who form a majority of the tribes or a majority of the people of Israel irrespective of tribes.</i> acted on their saying they bring a bull and for idolatry they bring a bull and a goat, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Jehudah says, the seven tribes who sinned bring seven bulls, and the remaining tribes who did not sin bring because of them a bull each, for also those who did not sin each bring a bull because of the sinners. Rebbi Simeon says eight bulls<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in the preceding Halakhah, he disputes that innocent tribes should be dragged with the sinners into offering sacrifice.</i>, and for idolatry eight oxen and eight goats, a bull and a goat for each tribe and a bull and a goat for the Court.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If the Court ruled and seven tribes acted,” etc. It was stated: Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says in his<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">In the name of R. Meïr, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Horayot 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Horayot.1.5">Tosephta 1:7</a>; <i>Sifra Wayyiqra 2, Parašah</i> 4(17); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 3a" href="/Horayot.3a">Babli 3a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 5b" href="/Horayot.5b">5b</a>.</i> name, if six sinned and they are a majority. Therefore seven even though they do not form a majority are liable<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 45a" href="/Menachot.45a">Babli, <i>Menaḥot</i> 45a</a>, this is the conclusion of R. Johanan. Since in the next sentence R. Eleazar disagrees, one has to assume that the name originally was stated here also.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, he only said “six and they are a majority”. Therefore for five, even though they are a majority, they are not liable. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, a <i>baraita</i> said so: Half of the tribes on condition that they be most of the population. And similarly, half of the population on condition that they be most of the tribes. <sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">This does not belong here but at the end of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.1.2">Halakhah 2:1</a>, speaking of rulings by the High Priest.</i> Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, the question of the Anointed Priest, is it as from the High Court or a lower court?

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the Court of one of the tribes<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">The tribal High Court.</i> ruled and that tribe acted on their pronouncement, that tribe is liable but any other tribes are not liable, the words of Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">Since he holds that the expression קָהָל refers to each of the tribes.</i>. But the Sages say, they are only liable for a ruling by the High Court, as it is said, <i>if the entire congregation of Israel be in error, and something was hidden from the eyes of the congregation</i><sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:13" href="/Leviticus.4.13"><i>Lev</i>. 4:13</a>. The verse is misquoted; the masoretic text reads הַקָּהָל “the public” instead of הָעֵדָה “the congregation”, supporting R. Jehudah. In most Mishnah sources, only the first clause of the verse is copied.</i>, not the congregation of that tribe.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> "If the Court of one of the tribes ruled," etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">The Halakhah does not refer to Mishnah 8 but to Mishnaiot 6-7, to explain why R. Meïr requires only one bull, R. Jehudah 12, and R. Simeon 13.</i>Rebbi Meïr says, it is the Court's obligation. Rebbi Jehudah says, it is the public's obligation. Rebbi Simeon said, it is an obligation of the Court and an obligation of the public. What is Rebbi Meïr's reason? It is said here <i>from the eyes</i><sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 4:13.</i>, and it is said there <i>from the eyes</i><sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 15:24.</i>. Since <i>from the eyes</i> said there refers to the Court, here it also refers to the Court<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote"> This argument is difficult to explain. In Sifra Wayyiqra 2 Parasah 4(2), the expression עֲדַת יִשְׂרָאֵל used in Lev. 4:13 is explained as referring to the High Court, the selected group from Israel, based on v. 15 which makes it clear that the bull has to be presented by the Elders, the members of the High Court. Then R. Meïr's argument is to infer from Lev. 4:13 to Num. 15:24: Since the bull is the responsibility of the Court, the goat for idolatry also must be the responsibility of the Court. </i>. What is Rebbi Jehudah's reason? It is said here <i>from the eyes</i>, and it is said there <i>from the eyes</i>. Since <i>from the eyes</i> said there refers to the public, here it also refers to the public<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">His argument is straightforward. Since the entire paragraph Num. 15:22-26 speaks only about עֵדָה, without any mention of the Elders, it is addressed to the public. Then the use of parallel terms is taken to transfer the setting to Lev. 4: 13.</i>. What is Rebbi Simeon's reason? It is said here <i>from the eyes</i>, and it is said there <i>from the eyes</i>. Since <i>from the eyes</i> said there refers to the Court, also <i>from the eyes </i>here refers to the Court. Since <i>from the eyes</i> said there refers to the public, here it also refers to the public<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">He accepts arguing both from Lev. 4: 13 to Num. 15:24 and vice versa.</i>. For him who says, it is the Court's obligation, the Court has to bring<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">They have to pay for the bull from their own money and present it in the Temple. </i>. For him who says, it is the public's obligation, who brings<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">Who has to pay and who has to officiate?</i>? As we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">One imposes a tax and collects from everybody. In all other sources, Babli 3b, Menahot 52a; Tosephta Seqalim 2:6, the argument is between R. Jehudah and R. Simeon. This is the reasonable reading since for R. Meïr the Court pays from their own means.</i>, "one imposes and collects, the words of Rebbi Meïr; Rebbi Jehudah says, they are brought from the Temple tax". For him who says, it is the Court's obligation, the Court has to lay their hands on. For him who says, it is the public's obligation, who lays their hands on<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">Since obviously not every single Israelite can be called to lay his hands on the bull.</i>? As we have stated, three from every tribe, <sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">This follows R. Simeon in Sifra Wayyiqra 2 Pereq 6(2); R. Jehudah requires five.</i> led by the president of the Court, lay their hands on the head of the bull. "<i>Their hands</i>, the hands of each single one. <i>Their hands on the head of the bull</i>; the bull needs laying on of hands but the goats of idolatry do not need laying on of hands, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Simeon said, the bull needs laying on of hands by the Elders but the goats of idolatry do not need laying on of hands by the Elders; for Rebbi Simeon says, every public purification offering whose blood is brought inside<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">The only sacrifices whose blood is brought inside the sanctuary to be sprinkled on the incense altar are the purification offerings of the High Priest and the Community as well as the offerings of the Day of Atonement. The body of any such sacrifice must be burned outside the holy precinct (Lev. 6:23,16:27).</i> needs laying on of hands. <sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">Sifra Wayyiqra 2 Pereq 6(3); Tosephta Menahot 10:9; Babli Menahot 92a.</i>" One objected to Rebbi Jehudah, is it not written, <i>they presented the goats of the purification offering</i><sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote"> 2Chr. 29:23. As usual, the argument is from the part of the verse which was not quoted: <i>they presented the goats of the purification offering before the king and the public; they laid their hands on them.</i> The goats were offered by Josiah to atone for the idolatry of his father Ahas.</i>? Rebbi Hiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, it was a temporary ruling<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">A temporary deviation from Torah norms acceptable by prophetic instruction as long as it does not violate prohibitions. The absence of a bull and the presence of multiple goats both deviate from Torah prescriptions.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan asked: If one of the public died, can it be brought in his stead<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">This is a question for RR. Jehudah and Simeon. If a person dedicated an animal as a purification offering but died before it was sacrificed, the animal cannot be sacrificed without its owner nor can it be redeemed or used for any profane or holy purpose whatsoever. If the bull really is the obligation of the public and paid by the public’s money, it should become unusable if anybody who gave money for the sacrifice (Note 172) died before the ceremony was held. Practically, this would make the ceremony impossible.</i>? They answered, is it not written, <i>those who came from captivity</i><sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezra 8:35" href="/Ezra.8.35"><i>Ezra</i> 8:35</a>: <i>Those who came from captivity, from the diaspora, sacrificed elevation offerings to the God of Israel, twelve bulls for all of Israel, 96 goats, 77 sheep, purification goats twelve, all of it an elevation offering for the Eternal.</i> An elevation offering is completely burned; the meat of a purification offering is eaten by the priests. To call a purification offering an elevation offering is a contradiction in terms.</i>? Is a purification offering an elevation offering? But just as an elevation offering is not eaten, this purification offering was not eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 6a" href="/Horayot.6a">Babli 6a</a>. Since they brought 12 goats, R. Jehudah has Ezra’s authority for his position. This interpretation justifies the reading of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:24" href="/Numbers.15.24"><i>Num.</i> 15:24</a> by the Mishnah. That verse requires the congregation to bring <i>a bull as elevation offering and a goat as purification offering.</i> If a purification offering which may not be eaten can be called an elevation offering, it is possible to identify this bull with the one prescribed in <i>Lev.</i>4:13. V. 24 requires the congregation (i. e., its Elders) to proffer the sacrifices but v. 25 requires the Cohen to conduct the entire ceremony. Both the opinions that the Elders do the laying on of their hands as also that the Cohen has to do it have biblical support.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, they brought it for idolatry; Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Jeremiah, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, it was a temporary ruling<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">A temporary deviation from Torah norms acceptable by prophetic instruction as long as it does not violate prohibitions. The absence of a bull and the presence of multiple goats both deviate from Torah prescriptions.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah (learned) [did not say]<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">The text in the Babli shows that one has to read למר i. e. לֹא אָמַר.</i> so but the bull needs laying on of hands by the Elders while the goats of idolatry do not need laying on of hands by the Elders. By whom? Rebbi Jeremiah wanted to say, by Aaron and his sons. Rebbi Yose told him, Rebbi Ḥiyya also stated, <i>he shall put his hands on, they shall put their hands on</i><sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:21" href="/Leviticus.16.21"><i>Lev.</i> 16:21</a> prescribes that Aaron has to lay his hand on the live goat. Since the entire service of the Day of Atonement is by the unaided High Priest, the mention of the name seems to be superfluous; it could as well have said “he has to lay his hands on.” It is concluded (next Note) that this is the only case in which the High Priest is required to lay his hands on. In parallel, one may read <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:15" href="/Leviticus.4.15"><i>Lev.</i> 4:15</a> where the Elders of the congregation are required to lay their hands on the bull, that they are not required to lay their hands on the goat.</i>, to include the goats of idolatry for laying on hands but not by the Elders. Rebbi Yose (learned) [did not say]<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">The text in the Babli shows that one has to read למר i. e. לֹא אָמַר.</i> so but the living [goat] needs laying on of hands by Aaron, but the goats of idolatry do not need laying on of hands by Aaron. It is written so, “<i>Aaron shall lean with both his hands on the living goat’s head;</i> the living [goat] needs laying on of hands by Aaron, but he goats of idolatry do not need laying on of hands by Aaron.<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Acharei Mot" href="/Sifra,_Acharei_Mot"><i>Sifra Ahare Mot Parašah</i> 4(4</a>).</i>” What does Rebbi Jeremiah do with this? He explains it, for a common priest<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">Since neither the High Priest nor the Elders are empowered but <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:25" href="/Numbers.15.25"><i>Num.</i> 15:25</a> requires the participation of a common priest, all biblical requirements are satisfied by having the common priest do the entire ceremony.</i>.
Rebbi Zeˋira in the name of Rav Hamnuna: Following Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">The following <i>baraita</i> can be understood only following R. Meïr who declares the bull to be the exclusive responsibility of the Court.</i>. There, it was stated: If the Court ruled and the public acted<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Wayyiqra 2 Paršetah</i> 4(10). The Court is not liable as long as the public did not act on their instructions.</i>. If a member of the Court died, they are not liable. If a member of the public died, they are liable<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">If a member of the Court died, the bull becomes a purification sacrifice whose part-owner had died; it cannot be brought nor sacrificed. While the sacrifice is brought for the benefit of the public, the public has no monetary interest in the bull following Rebbi Meïr. For him, all the public does is trigger the obligation.</i>. Rebbi Meïr told them, if he<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">If a member of the Court acted on his own faulty ruling, the bull cannot relieve him of the obligation for a private purification sacrifice since the bull only is intended to shield those who acted on instructions of the Court. He himself does not depend on the Court and still is liable (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:8:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.8.5">Note 191</a>).</i> relieves others of their liability, not so much more for himself? They told him, he can relieve others from their liability since they have where to hang on; he cannot relieve himself of liability since he has nothing to hang on.
Rebbi Zeˋira in the name of Rav Ḥisdai. There, it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 5a" href="/Horayot.5a">Babli 5a</a>, <i>Sifra Wayyiqra 2 Paršetah</i>4(12).</i>: “If the Court ruled, and they themselves acted, and they realized what they ruled about. If they erred in what they ruled, would they be liable? The verse says, <i>if the sin became known</i><sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 4:14. V. 13 makes it clear that the actions of the people trigger the obligation of the bull, not the actions of the court when it is not followed by the people.</i><i>,</i> not that the sinners became known.” Anyway you take it<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to another situation which is described at the end. The court ruled, they were followed by the people, they realized their error but know they cannot decide which paragraph of the law they misinterpreted. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 5a" href="/Horayot.5a">Babli 5a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 18b" href="/Shevuot.18b"><i>Ševuot</i>18b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 19a" href="/Keritot.19a"><i>Keritut</i> 19a</a>, R. Eliezer is quoted to hold that if one is not sure of the exact category of the sin committed it does not matter as long as all of them require a sacrifice. R. Joshua holds that a purification sacrifice is possible only if the legal definition of the transgression is known, as in all cases the verse requires that the sin be known (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:14" href="/Leviticus.4.14"><i>Lev.</i> 4:14</a> for the court, v. 23 for the prince, v. 28 for a private person; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.1.5">Note 22</a>.) From the text here it seems that the Yerushalmi tradition switches the names.</i>, if about fat [<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">Text of B, missing in the ms. The text must be supposed also for the ms. since otherwise the reference to R. Joshua is unmotivated.</i> they ruled they are liable, if about Sabbath they ruled they are liable. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, this follows Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">Who in the Yerushalmi version prohibits the Court from offering the bull if they cannot define exactly which commandment had been breached.</i>; it does not follow Rebbi Joshua. Rebbi Yose said, but] it follows Rebbi Joshua if they ruled and they did not know whether about idolatry or about any other commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">The argument in Note 191 is valid only if the different infractions all carry the same penalty. But if there is a question about which sacrifice to offer, no sacrifice is possible. Purification (and reparation) offerings cannot be brought as voluntary offerings since in contrast to these all voluntary offerings need gifts of flour and wine. Therefore one could not bring both kinds of sacrifice stipulating that the inappropriate one should be considered as voluntary.</i>. If about idolatry by a bull, if about any other commandment a goat<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">Clearly, one has to switch the positions of “bull” and “goat”.</i>. Since it is in doubt whether a bull or [a bull and<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">Text of B, more correct since the bull for unspecified sins is a purification offering and that for idolatry an elevation offering.</i>] a goat, it is a difference in sacrifice and he<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">The Court.</i> is not liable.

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the Anointed Priest<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This is the expression used in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:1-12" href="/Leviticus.4.1-12"><i>Lev.</i>4:1–12</a> for the High Priest. Since the verse refers here to “the Anointed Priest” while in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:10" href="/Leviticus.21.10"><i>Lev.</i> 21:10</a> he is called “the High Priest”, it is inferred (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.1">Mishnah 3:4</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:7:2-8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.7.2-8.1"><i>Megillah</i> 1:9</a>) that High Priests of the Second Temple who were not anointed were not entitled to this ceremony. The Anointed has to confess his sin while leaning with his hands on the bull’s head. The remainder of the ceremony, as also that of the Court’s bull, may be performed by a common priest. <i>Sifra Wayyiqra 2 Parashah</i> 3(6).</i> ruled for himself in error and acted in error, he brings a bull<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">As described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:1-12" href="/Leviticus.4.1-12"><i>Lev.</i> 4:1–12</a>, his private purification offering.</i>. If in error but he acted intentionally, or intentionally and he acted in error, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">He cannot bring a purification offering unless it was an unintentional act. If he ruled wrongly but then did not act on his ruling because he did not trust his judgment, no sacrifice is due or possible.</i> since the ruling of the Anointed Priest for himself is like the ruling of the Court for the public.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If the Anointed Priest ruled,” etc. <i>A person; if the Anointed Priest.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:2" href="/Leviticus.4.2"><i>Lev.</i> 4:2</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:3" href="/Leviticus.4.3">3</a>.</i>” This makes the Anointed like a private person<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:2" href="/Leviticus.4.2">v. 2</a>, the High Priest is mentioned as “a person”; only in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:3" href="/Leviticus.4.3">v. 3</a> as Anointed. It is concluded that the Anointed Priest follows the rules of private persons unless there is an indication to the contrary. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 7b" href="/Horayot.7b">Babli 7b</a>, <i>Sifra Wayyiqra 2 Paršeta</i> 2(1).</i>. Since the private person is not liable if he ate<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Taking eating forbidden fat as standard example of a forbidden action.</i> on the Court’s ruling, also neither is this one liable if he ate on the Court’s ruling. Since the private person is liable if he ate without a ruling, this one also should be liable if he ate without a ruling<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The question is raised why does the Mishnah require a sacrifice only if the Anointed Priest first gives an inadvertent wrong instruction and then acts on it without realizing his error? This mixes required features of the purification offerings both of the Court (only after issuing a prescript, not acting on it themselves) and the private person (not instructing anybody but acting).</i>; the verse says, <i>for the fault of the people.</i> As the people<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This argument identifies “the people” as the Court.</i> are liable only if they issued a ruling, this one also is liable only if he issued a ruling. There are Tannaïm who state that <i>the people</i> are the Court. Since the people<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This argument identifies “the people” as the Court.</i> are liable if they instructed and others acted, this one also should be liable if he ruled and others acted. The verse says, <i>as he sinned.</i> He brings for what he sinned but he does not bring for what others sinned. There are Tannaïm<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 7a" href="/Horayot.7a">Babli 7a</a>, <i>Sifra Wayyiqra 2 Pereq</i>2(1,4).</i> who state that <i>the people</i> are the public. Since the people are liable if others ruled and they acted, this one also should be liable if others ruled and he acted. The verse says, <i>as he sinned.</i> He brings for what he sinned but he does not bring for what others sinned<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Including the Court. Since the High Priest is mentioned before the Court, his purification offering cannot be dependent on the Court’s ruling.</i>. Rebbi Jacob<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In B: R. Jacob bar Aha. It is impossible to decide which reading is preferable or whether the same person is meant in both sources.</i> in the name of Rebbi Eleazar. Only if he is competent to argue about practice<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This shows that the rules are applicable to the High Priests of the Second Temple, who were invested but not anointed. The High Priests of the First Temple were supposed to be the guardians of the Law. The Hasmonean kings and the later High Priests mostly had no claim to learning.</i>. Otherwise, would we say the incompetents give instructions? The anointed who ate following the Court’s prescript is not liable; following another Anointed’s prescript he is liable. Following the Court’s prescript he is not liable, since the prescript of others is nothing<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows B. The text of the ms., “since the prescript of others is by all their prescripts” does not make any sense.</i> compared to their prescripts<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.1.2">Note 10</a>, the High Priests cannot be made dependent on the prescripts of others. The High Priest is “not dependent on the Court” <i>ex officio.</i> But as explained earlier, he cannot bring his purification offering unless his action was based on his own ruling.</i>. Following another Anointed’s prescript he is liable, on condition that he instructed similarly<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">It is not required that the High Priest have original thoughts when acting; he is presumed to follow (correct) precedent.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he ruled for himself and acted for himself, it is atoned for himself. If he ruled with the public and acted with the public, it is atoned for him with the public<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">As shown in the preceding paragraph, his sacrifice is reserved for inadvertent sins peculiar to himself. Even if he concurred with an erroneous ruling by the Court, a separate offering would be inappropriate. This differs from the ritual of the Day of Atonement where the High Priest has to atone for himself (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:6" href="/Leviticus.16.6"><i>Lev.</i> 16:6</a>) before he can officiate for the other priests (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:11" href="/Leviticus.16.11">v. 11</a>) and the people (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:15" href="/Leviticus.16.15">v. 15</a>). But here he is not acting on behalf of others.</i>. As the Court is not liable unless they instruct to void part and confirm part, so is the Anointed<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">All rules established in Chapter 1 for the Court’s bull apply to the High Priest’s bull. In the verse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:20" href="/Leviticus.4.20"><i>Lev.</i> 4:20</a>) it is formulated the other way: All rules of the High Priest’s bull apply to the Court’s.</i>. Also for idolatry they are not liable unless they instruct to void part and confirm part<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The rules concerning general infractions are transferred to idolatry by an argument similar to Chapter 1, Note 167.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he ruled for himself,” etc. The Court ruled, he ruled following them, and then changed. It is obvious that for him it is the equivalent of uprooting the whole<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">R. Moses Margolis (<i>Pene Moshe</i> and <i>Mar’eh Happanim</i>), the author of the only complete Commentary to <i>Horaiot</i>, explains that the Court and the High Priest originally issued consistent rulings. Then the High Priest changed his mind, and issued a new ruling on the same subject so that the two inconsistent prohibitions together amount to a complete removal of a biblical prohibition which according to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.3.1">Mishnah 1:3</a> prevents atonement by a sacrifice. It is difficult to find this in the language of the paragraph since it says clearly that the High Priest changed his mind, i. e., he permits what the Court forbade and the Court forbids what the High Priest forbade; neither of the parties uproots the entire commandment.<br>Therefore, it seems that at first the High Priest followed the High Court’s ruling, as stated in the text, but then reversed himself and opposed the High Court’s ruling. Then irrespective of the topic of the dispute, the High Court uprooted <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 17:8-13" href="/Deuteronomy.17.8-13"><i>Deut.</i> 17:8<i>–</i>13</a>. If the Court stays with its pronouncement, the High Priest is barred from rehabilitating himself by a sacrifice.</i>. But if he ruled first, the Court then ruled following him, and afterwards they reversed themselves. Nevertheless is it for him the equivalent of uprooting the whole<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">If he continues to adhere to his own ruling after the Court reversed itself.</i>, or because he ruled first, but his ruling was overturned by their ruling, for him it should not be the equivalent of uprooting the whole<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Since he first ruled by scriptural authorization. The question remains unanswered.</i>? If the Court ruled and he ruled following them, it is obvious that he is not liable if he ate<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Forbidden fat which the Court had permitted; the standard example of a sin atonable by a sacrifice.</i> according to the ruling of the Court<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Since he follows the public, his action in performing the rites for the Court’s offering also atones for him as noted in the Mishnah.</i>. But after the Court reversed itself, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Temple is adjacent to the Court’s seat, he will know immediately of the Court’s decision and, therefore, his later actions are not covered by the Court’s offering (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:2:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.2.2-8">Halakhah 1:2</a>). The text in the Babli lacks the last word; this text has to be read as a question: “But after the Court reversed itself? Rebbi Yose said, is that not the Mishnah? …”, i. e., the answer to the question is obvious. There is no material difference in the meaning of the two texts.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, is that not the Mishnah? “If he ruled for himself [and acted for himself]<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Addition from B.</i>, it is atoned for himself.” Because he ruled for himself and acted for himself. “But if he ruled with the public and acted with the public, it is atoned for him with the public.”<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">B here has an additional text:<br>Rebbi Mana said, what you want to prove from the first part [of the Mishnah] is stated in the later part, “if he ruled with the public and acted with the public, it is atoned for him with the public.”<br>In this text, the quote of the same statement preceding the statement of R. Mana (II, student of R. Yose and R. Jonah) should be omitted.</i> Because he ruled with the public and acted with the public, but “if he ruled for himself [and acted for himself]<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Addition from B.</i>, it is atoned for himself.”

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> They<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The High Court.</i> are liable only for forgetting a topic<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">There must be an element of oblivion in their ruling; either forgetting a certain law or that it applies in the given situation.</i> with action in error; the same applies to the Anointed. Also for idolatry<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:8:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.8.2-5">Halakhah 1:8</a> that the special offerings required for the sin of idolatry follow the rules of the bull required for all other infractions.</i> only for forgetting a topic with action in error.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “They are liable only for forgetting,” etc. Rebbi Zeˋira in the name of Rav Jeremiah. The Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The spelling in B is a scribal error.</i> is Rebbi Meïr’s or has inferences like Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to make sense of this statement. The version of B is straightforward: “Which R. Meïr?” Meaning, which statement of R. Meïr fits the Mishnah? The answer to this question is given by R. Yose. Since the Mishnah refers to the Court’s, not to the public’s monetary responsibility, it must accept R. Meïr’s position which, being formulated as anonymous doctrine, becomes practice.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, Rebbi Meïr who said, it is an obligation of the Court. <sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.1">Halakhah 1:8, Notes 185–188</a>. (There, R. Zeˋira reports in the name of a Sage whose identity is not clear.)</i> Rebbi Mana said, following Rebbi Meïr. There, it was stated: If the Court ruled and the public acted. If a member of the Court died, they are not liable. If a member of the public died, they are liable. Rebbi Meïr told them, if he relieves others of their liability, not so much more for himself? They told him, he can relieve others of their liability since they have where to hang on; he cannot relieve himself of liability since he has nothing to hang on.
Rebbi Jeremiah asked before Rebbi Zeˋira: Maybe following Rebbi? If<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">There is a lacuna in the text which must be filled by the text of B:<br>[Does it follow Rebbi, for Rebbi said,] the Anointed refers to the case of action in error, but for the rabbis, it refers to forgetting a topic.<br>In contrast to the Mishnah, which for the High Priest as for the Court requires both an element of oblivion and acting in error, Rebbi points out that for the Court, the forgetting is the Court’s but the action in error is the public’s. Rebbi compares the status of the High Priest to that of the Court. Since for general errors the Mishnah states that the same rules apply to the High Priest as to the Court, the argument must be about the sin of idolatry where the High Priest is not mentioned. The rabbis note that the High Priest does not have the status of the Court vis-a-vis the public; his rulings are private, not public actions. Therefore, he cannot be held responsible unless he act. They do not deny that an element of oblivion is required to trigger the obligation of the purification sacrifice.</i> the Anointed, error in action it is. But if the rabbis, oblivion of a topic it is. Rebbi Huna<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The reading “R. Huna”, a student of R. Jeremiah, is preferable to the reading of B: “R. Hiyya”, the teacher of R. Zeˋira.</i> said, it was needed for the rabbis. That you should not say, the Anointed and idolatry: Rebbi said, in acting in error; the rabbis said, in forgetting a topic. He said [ ]<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The text of the ms., “what for easy what” seems corrupt. It is better to follow the reading of B: “He said to them, why? Because … “. The Anointed is not mentioned in the last sentence of the Mishnah, but this does not mean that the rule given there does not apply to him.</i>, because we did not state about the Anointed. But in the former<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>, in the last sentence about idolatry the High Priest is not mentioned but everybody agrees that it also applies to him; so the parallel sentence in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.3.1">Mishnah 3</a> also must apply to him. The Mishnah strictly follows the rabbis, not Rebbi.</i>, we did not state about the Anointed but the Anointed is included, and here, even though we did not state about the Anointed, the Anointed is included.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> They<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The High Court.</i> are liable only for something which when intentional is punishable by extirpation<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">These are enumerated in the first Chapter of Tractate <i>Keritut.</i></i> and when unintentional by a purification sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">This excludes violations of <i>sancta</i> which require a different kind of sacrifice as described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5" href="/Leviticus.5"><i>Lev.</i> 5</a>. Cf. Mishnah 5. There remain 31 topics for which High Priest or Court may be required to sacrifice a bull.</i>. The same applies to the Anointed. Also for idolatry<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:8:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.8.2-5">Halakhah 1:8</a> that the special offerings required for the sin of idolatry follow the rules of the bull required for all other infractions.</i> only for something which when intentional is punishable by extirpation and when unintentional by a purification sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The text of the ms., “what for easy what” seems corrupt. It is better to follow the reading of B: “He said to them, why? Because … “. The Anointed is not mentioned in the last sentence of the Mishnah, but this does not mean that the rule given there does not apply to him.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “They are only liable for something which when intentional is punishable by extirpation,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 4:15:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.4.15.4"><i>Yebamot</i> 4:15, Notes 219–227</a>.</i><i>The commandments of the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Since the introduction to the rules of purification sacrifices, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:2" href="/Leviticus.4.2"><i>Lev.</i> 4:2</a>, seems to prescribe such a sacrifice for <i>all the Commandments of the Eternal,</i> it needs an argument why in all four cases (the High Priest, the High Court, Prince, and commoner) simple transgressions are excluded.</i><i>.</i> I could think that this also includes those who eat abominations and crawling things. <sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.6.2">Halakhah 1:8, Notes 145</a> ff. Here also one compares the transgressions for which the Court has to bring a bull to the sin of idolatry for which a bull and a goat are required.</i> It says here <i>from the eyes</i> and it says there <i>from the eyes.</i> Just as <i>from the eyes</i> mentioned there means something which is punished by extirpation in case of intentional sin<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The punishment for idolatry in the absence of witnesses or warning.</i> and needs a purification sacrifice for an inadvertent sin, so <i>from the eyes</i> mentioned here means something which is punished by extirpation in case of intentional sin and needs a purification sacrifice for an inadvertent sin. Or since <i>from the eyes</i> mentioned there means something that can lead to the death penalty, so <i>from the eyes</i> mentioned here should mean something that can lead to the death penalty<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The punishment for idolatry if there are witnesses for warning and deed.</i>? <sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">This is a wrong quote from <i>Yebamot</i>(Note 220). The father’s wife who is not the mother is forbidden in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:1" href="/Deuteronomy.23.1"><i>Deut.</i> 23:1</a> (in addition to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:8" href="/Leviticus.18.8"><i>Lev.</i> 18:8</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 20:11" href="/Leviticus.20.11">20:11</a>). The bastard is mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:3" href="/Deuteronomy.23.3"><i>Deut.</i> 23:3</a>; it is inferred that the bastard is a child from a forbidden union exemplified by the father’s wife.<br>One may assume that the intended quote from <i>Yebamot</i> was the answer given there to the question: “R. Yose ben Ḥanina said, from the place from which idolatry was singled out to teach about all who are subject to extirpation (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:31" href="/Numbers.15.31"><i>Num.</i>15:31</a>), there was no mention of anything but extirpation (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:30-31" href="/Numbers.15.30-31"><i>Num.</i> 15:30–31</a>). But the death penalty is written elsewhere (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 20:2" href="/Leviticus.20.2"><i>Lev.</i>20:2</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 17:5" href="/Deuteronomy.17.5"><i>Deut.</i> 17:5</a>).” (Note 226).</i> Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, from a place where all incest prohibitions were taken as one set [of sins] causing extirpation, the (married woman) [father’s wife] came out to tell you about the bastard. <sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 8a" href="/Horayot.8a">Babli 8a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 9a" href="/Yevamot.9a"><i>Yebamot</i> 9a</a>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:14" href="/Leviticus.4.14"><i>Lev.</i> 4:14</a>, speaking of the Court’s sacrifice, it is said, <i>and the sin became known concerning which (</i>עָלֶיהָ<i>) they had sinned.</i> In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:18" href="/Leviticus.18.18"><i>Lev.</i> 18:18</a> one reads: <i>Do not marry a woman in addition to her sister, to make them co-wives, to uncover her nakedness over her (</i>עָלֶיהָ<i>) during her lifetime. Lev.</i>18:29 is a blanket warning that all sexual crimes mentioned in the Chapter are punishable by extirpation. The wife’s sister is not mentioned in the list of punishments in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 20" href="/Leviticus.20"><i>Lev.</i> 20</a>. Therefore this is a reference only to divine extirpation; even with witnesses and due warnings no death penalty is involved (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 7:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.7.5.3"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 7:5, Note 78</a>).</i> Rebbi said <i>over which, over her.</i> Since <i>over her</i> mentioned there refers to something which is punished by extirpation in case of intentional sin and needs a purification sacrifice for an inadvertent sin, so <i>over which</i> mentioned here means something for which one is punished by extirpation in case of intentional sin and needs a purification sacrifice for an inadvertent sin. Why does Rebbi not infer from <i>from the eyes, from the eyes</i>? <sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Text found only in B. Since the argument of R. Zeˋira is identical to that of R. Mana (student of R. Zeˋira’s student), the text is suspect. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:3:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.3.2-3">Halakhah 3</a> it was established that Rebbi requires the High Priest to bring a sacrifice for an erroneous action even without an element of oblivion. If Rebbi would accept transfer of the argument of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.8">Halakhah 1:8, Notes 165ff</a>. to the problem here, he would have to hold that the same applies to the Court. But since forgetting is mentioned in the verse speaking of the Court, this would disprove his thesis. Therefore, Rebbi cannot accept the application of the argument of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:8:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.8.2-5">Halakhah 1:8</a> to the problem of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.4.1">Mishnah 2:4</a>.</i>[Rebbi Zeˋira said: If Rebbi would infer from <i>from the eyes, from the eyes,</i> then <i>from the eyes</i> mentioned there for the Anointed refers to action in error; also <i>from the eyes</i> mentioned here would have to refer to action in error. Therefore, Rebbi will not infer from <i>from the eyes, from the eyes.</i>] Rebbi Yose said: If Rebbi would infer from <i>from the eyes, from the eyes,</i> then since <i>from the eyes</i> mentioned there means something that can lead to the death penalty, so <i>from the eyes</i> mentioned here means something that can lead to the death penalty<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">He accepts the question (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.4.2">Note 43</a>) as valid.</i>. Therefore, Rebbi does not infer from <i>from the eyes, from the eyes.</i> (Does Rebbi not have a book) [Will Rebbi not in the end have]<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is from the ms., the one in brackets from B. It seems that the ms. text is a scribal error.</i> to infer <i>from the eyes, from the eyes</i>? <sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Text of the ms. Since Rebbi only denies that for the High Priest’s offering an element of oblivion is necessary, for all others he accepts the reasoning of <i>Sifra Wayyiqra 2 Parašah</i> 4(6) that from a comparison with the rules for unintentional idolatry it is established that both oblivion and unintentional action are needed. The connection is established by <i>from the eyes, from the eyes.</i></i> For if it is not so, from where would we have the rules for the individual, the chief, and the Anointed? Not from <i>from the eyes, from the eyes</i>? <sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Text of B. But in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:3:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.3.2-3">Halakhah 3</a>, there is no reference to inferences from idolatry.</i> For if it is not so, from where would we have “unless they ruled to eliminate part and to confirm part”? Not from <i>from the eyes, from the eyes</i>? For if it is not so, from where would we have [the difference between] a ruling of the High Court and a ruling of a lower court<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that the court mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4" href="/Leviticus.4"><i>Lev.</i> 4</a> is the High Court is proven from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:24" href="/Numbers.15.24"><i>Num.</i> 15:24</a>. The connection is established by <i>from the eyes, from the eyes.</i></i>? Rebbi Ḥanania<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">This is the correct attribution, not R. Hanina as in B.<br><i>53 Sifra Wayyiqra 2 Parašah</i> 3(7). The reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:5" href="/Leviticus.4.5"><i>Lev.</i> 4:5</a>: <i>The Anointed Priest shall take of the bull’s blood and bring it to the Tent of Meeting.</i> “To bring it” could have been expressed as וְהֵבִיאוֹ, but it is given as וְהֵבִיא אֹתוֹ. The choice of a separate word for “it” is read as emphasizing that precisely <i>this</i> blood is brought into the sanctuary, not blood of similar sacrifices. The one sacrifice similar to the bull of the High Priest and the one of the Court which follows the same rule (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:16" href="/Leviticus.4.16"><i>Lev.</i> 4:16</a>, where the language is: <i>of the bull’s blood</i>) is the bull offered for inadvertent idolatry. The latter is called “the singular commandment” both because it requires a rite of atonement different from all other commandments as also it is the only sin which in one act violates all commandments (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.6.1">Halakhah 1:6, Note 135</a>).</i> said before Rebbi Mana: Would not Rebbi in the end have to infer from <i>from the eyes, from the eyes</i>? He told him, what do you want from Rebbi? Rebbi follows his own opinion, since Rebbi said, the Anointed refers to action in error. Intentional error is only written for the Court<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Text found only in B. Since the argument of R. Zeˋira is identical to that of R. Mana (student of R. Zeˋira’s student), the text is suspect. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:3:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.3.2-3">Halakhah 3</a> it was established that Rebbi requires the High Priest to bring a sacrifice for an erroneous action even without an element of oblivion. If Rebbi would accept transfer of the argument of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.8">Halakhah 1:8, Notes 165ff</a>. to the problem here, he would have to hold that the same applies to the Court. But since forgetting is mentioned in the verse speaking of the Court, this would disprove his thesis. Therefore, Rebbi cannot accept the application of the argument of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:8:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.8.2-5">Halakhah 1:8</a> to the problem of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.4.1">Mishnah 2:4</a>.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i>It.</i> It specifies about his sacrifice that for the singular commandment its blood should not be brought into the Sanctuary. Does this follow Rebbi, as Rebbi said, the Anointed refers to action in error; if it would follow the rabbis, there must be an instance of forgetting. (Rav) [Rebbi]<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is from the ms., the (correct) one in brackets from B. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.3.3">Note 34</a>.</i> Huna said, it was needed for the rabbis. Lest you say, because there is extirpation<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">For idolatry which cannot be prosecuted in court for lack of eye witnesses or due warning.</i> its blood should be brought into the Sanctuary. Therefore, it was necessary to say <i>it,</i> it specifies his sacrifice that for the singular commandment its blood should not be brought into the Sanctuary.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> They are not liable for a positive commandment or a prohibition in the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">At least not for any incorrect ruling regarding impurity in the Temple or of sacrifices. A private person who unintentionally violates any of those rules is required to offer a sacrifice, as described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5" href="/Leviticus.5"><i>Lev.</i> 5</a>, whose value depends on the wealth of the offerer. The sacrifices prescribed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4" href="/Leviticus.4"><i>Lev.</i> 4</a> for the High Priest, the Court, the Prince, and private persons, all are of fixed value. It will be argued that both the sacrifice of the Court as also “suspended” sacrifices, those offered for suspected sins, are possible only under circumstances in which the certainly sinning individual would be liable for a fixed-value offering.</i>; one does not bring a suspended reparation sacrifice for a positive commandment or a prohibition in the Temple. But they are liable for a positive commandment or a prohibition about the menstruating woman<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">That means any sin which if committed unintentionally requires a fixed-value offering. The Mishnah does not refer to the standard example, eating prohibited fat, since there no positive commandment is involved.</i>; one brings a suspended reparation sacrifice for a positive commandment or a prohibition about the menstruating woman. What is the positive commandment about the menstruating woman? Separate from the menstruating woman<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">As will be explained in the Halakhah.</i>. The prohibition, do not copulate with a menstruating woman<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">A rather weaker statement than <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:19" href="/Leviticus.18.19"><i>Lev.</i>18:19</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “They are not liable for a positive commandment or a prohibition in the Temple,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph also is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.4.2">Halakhah 2:4</a> in <i>Ševuot</i> (<b>ש</b>).</i> Cahana said, is it impossible that there was knowledge at the beginning and knowledge at the end and oblivion in between<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">He questions why it should be impossible to bring a suspended sacrifice for violating the Temple’s sanctity by impurity. In the introduction to the rules of the variable-value reparation offering (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:3" href="/Leviticus.5.3"><i>Lev.</i> 5:3</a>), one of the cases for such an offering is described as <i>or if he touch a human’s impurity, any impurity that make him impure, and it was hidden from him, and then he knew and was guilty.</i> It is clear that causing damage by impurity is subject to a sacrifice only if there was knowledge of the impurity interrupted by a period of unawareness. Why should it be impossible that this condition be satisfied for one entering the Temple while impure?</i>? Rebbi Samuel ben Eudaimon objected before Rebbi Mana: Could that be for the teachers<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah is formulated for the Court, why can it not be read as addressing only the teachers of the law but not all impure individuals? But for issuing a ruling a suspended reparation sacrifice is impossible; therefore, the Mishnah refers not only to the Court but to everybody.</i>? He told him, we have a problem with those entering and you bring us teachers? What about it? Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac said, <i>commandments, commandments.</i> Since <i>commandments</i> mentioned there are about fixed-value [offerings], here also about fixed-value [offerings]<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The introduction to the rules of the suspended reparation sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:17" href="/Leviticus.5.17"><i>Lev.</i> 5:17</a>) reads: <i>But if a person sin, violating one of the commandments of the Eternal that are prohibitions; if he is unsure whether he be guilty and have to carry his sin.</i> The introduction to the rules of the fixed-rate purification sacrifices of an individual reads: <i>But if a person of the people of the Land sin inadvertently, violating one of the commandments of the Eternal that are prohibitions, and is guilty.</i> The parallel language implies parallel rules; suspended sacrifices are possible only for suspected sins whose atonement would be covered by the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4" href="/Leviticus.4"><i>Lev.</i> 4</a>, not <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5" href="/Leviticus.5"><i>Lev.</i> 5</a>. A different argument with identical result is in<i>Sifra Wayyiqra 2, Parašah</i> 12(10).</i>.
But one never is liable for any positive commandment in the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Since the rules for purification sacrifices clearly say that they are for inadvertent violations of prohibitions. <i>Sifra Wayyiqra 2 Paršeta</i> 1(6).</i>! Rebbi Mattaniah said, we only came to state matters similar to the matter<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The only positive commandments which can lead to the liability for a purification sacrifice are special obligations to take steps to avoid certain prohibitions. The only examples are impurity in the Temple and relations with a menstruating woman; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.5.4">Note 70</a>.</i>. How is that? If he entered the Temple impure, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">There are many verses forbidding entry into the holy precinct to impure people, each one for a specific impurity. For impurity of the dead, the sufferer from skin disease, and the sufferer from gonorrhea <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:2" href="/Numbers.5.2"><i>Num.</i> 5:2</a> (taking together <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:20" href="/Numbers.19.20"><i>Num.</i>19:20</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:46" href="/Leviticus.13.46"><i>Lev.</i> 13:46</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:15" href="/Leviticus.15.15">15:15</a>); for the woman after childbirth <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:4" href="/Leviticus.12.4"><i>Lev.</i> 12:4</a>. The general prohibition covering all impurities is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:31" href="/Leviticus.15.31"><i>Lev.</i> 15:31</a> as noted in the next paragraph.</i>. If he entered pure but became impure, if he leaves on a long path he is liable, on a short one not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 14b" href="/Shevuot.14b">Babli <i>Ševuot</i> 14b</a>. There and in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 3:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.3.5.3"><i>Nazir</i>3:5 (Note 68</a>) it is stated that the person noticing his impurity must leave in less time than is needed to prostrate himself.</i>. Similarly, if he was having sex with an impure woman, he is liable. If he was having sex with a pure one and she said to him, I became impure, if he leaves on a long path he is liable, on a short one not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 18a" href="/Shevuot.18a">Babli, <i>Ševuot</i> 18a</a>, last line in the Wilna ed., objects to a time limit in this case while agreeing with the practice as explained in the next paragraph.</i>. What is his shortcut? He shall cool down<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">He must remain immobilized until his erection has disappeared.</i>.
“What is the positive commandment about the menstruating woman?” Rebbi Abin said, <i>Keep the Children of Israel away from their impurities</i><sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:31" href="/Leviticus.15.31"><i>Lev.</i> 15:31</a>. The verse continues: <i>Lest they die in their impurities when they defile My abode which is in their midst.</i> This is the positive commandment not to defile the Temple. The verse concludes the chapters on impurities created by the human body (childbirth, skin diseases, male and female venereal diseases, menstruation, and sexual relations with a menstruating woman). Therefore it also is the positive commandment regarding the menstruating woman and is interpreted to forbid sexual relations with a woman close to the expected onset of her menses. The question about the woman experiencing a discharge during sex must refer to an unexpected event. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 18b" href="/Shevuot.18b">Babli <i>Ševuot</i> 18b</a>, most of the paragraph.</i>. Rebbi Jonathan sent to ask Rebbi Simeon ben Rebbi Yose bar Lakonia, from where a warning for one having sex with an impure woman? He wanted to throw a stone after him; he told him, you are asking me something that children recite every day in the synagogue<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Serving as elementary school under the system of compulsory elementary education instituted by Joshua ben Gamla.</i>: <i>To a woman in the separation of</i><i>her impurity you shall not come near to uncover her nakedness</i><sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:19" href="/Leviticus.18.19"><i>Lev</i>. 18:19</a>.</i>. He answered him, that is not my problem. My only problem is rather “if he was having sex with an impure woman, he is liable. If he was having sex with a pure one and she said to him, I became impure,” if he separates immediately, is he liable<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">As the Babli explains, interrupting the coition during an erection is pleasurable for the male and therefore forbidden under the circumstances. The end of the erection must precede the separation.</i>? He told him, I and you have the same problem. Let us go out and learn. They went out and heard the voice of a Tanna who stated following Ḥiskiah: <i>If lying a man will lie with her</i><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:24" href="/Leviticus.15.24"><i>Lev.</i> 15:24</a>.</i>. Not only that if he was having sex with an impure woman, he is liable. If he was having sex with a pure one and she said to him, I became impure, if he separates immediately, is he liable? The verse says, <i>her secretion shall be</i><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:24" href="/Leviticus.15.24"><i>Lev.</i> 15:24</a>.</i>, even if her secretion starts<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The rabbinic expression for the onset on the menses is פִּרֵס נִידָּה, “breaking through”.</i> with him. What should he do? Rav Hoshaia, Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel, he shall cool down. If he did not cool down? Rebbi Yose said, for him I am reading <i>do not come near</i><sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:19" href="/Leviticus.18.19"><i>Lev</i>. 18:19</a>.</i> as “do not separate”. Closeness is separation. Rav Ḥuna in the name of Rav Abba: <i>Those who say, be close to yourself, do not touch me for I sanctified you</i><sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 65:5" href="/Isaiah.65.5"><i>Is.</i> 65:5</a>. Also in the Babli the verse is quoted in support of the interpretation of the root קרב as “to separate”.</i>. Rebbi Zeˋira said, he should imagine that a sword is cutting into his flesh. Is everybody Rebbi Zeˋira? Rebbi Tanḥuma in the name of Rav Ḥuna: He shall press his fingertips on the wall, then he will cool down<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 18a" href="/Shevuot.18a">Babli <i>Ševuot</i> 18a</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">An explanation of the difficult verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 49:24" href="/Genesis.49.24"><i>Gen.</i> 49:24</a> as describing Joseph’s reaction to the advances of Potiphar’s wife, as appendix on the difficulties of eliminating an unwanted erection. <i>Gen.</i>98(24).</i> It is written: <i>His bow was sitting immobile.</i> Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman: his bow was spanned and relaxed. Rebbi Abun said, his semen spread out and came out from under his fingernails, <i>the arms of his hands were excited.</i> Rav Ḥuna in the name of Rav Mattanah, he lifted his eyes, saw the picture<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Greek εἰκόνιον. It would not be impossible to vocalize אֵיקוֹנִיֹן.
