In B: “The picture of our father Jacob; immediately he cooled down.”</i> of his father, and cooled down immediately, <i>from the hands of the noble Jacob.</i> Rebbi Abin said, he also saw the picture of Rachel, <i>from there the shepherd, the rock of Israel.</i>

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> They are not liable for hearing a sound, or expression of the lips, or the impurity of the Temple and its <i>sancta</i><sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the preceding Halakhah, the Court does not bring a sacrifice for a false ruling in a case subject to a sacrifice which depends on the wealth of the person. These are enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1-4" href="/Leviticus.5.1-4"><i>Lev.</i> 5:1–4</a>; the Mishnah uses the biblical expressions to characterize the different categories.<br>“Hearing of a voice” refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1" href="/Leviticus.5.1"><i>Lev.</i>5:1</a>: <i>If a person sin, for he heard the sound of an imprecation when he is a witness, or saw, or knew; if he does not tell he has to bear his iniquity.</i> If a person is asked by another to testify in his case before the court; he refuses and assents to an oath to the effect that he does not know about the case, if that was a lie he is subject to the variable reparation offering for swearing falsely.<br>“Expression of the lips” (v.4) refers to an oath made by a person on his own initiative but not kept since he forgot about his own oath. This also subjects the maker to the same obligation.<br>Improper handling of impurity because of oblivion is mentioned in vv. 2–3.</i>. The same applies to the Prince<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">He is identified with the king.</i>, the words of Rebbi Yose the Galilean. Rebbi Aqiba says, the Prince is liable for all of them except the hearing of a sound since the king does not judge, nor may one judge him; he does not testify, nor may one testify against him<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.2.3.1">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 2:3</a>. He extends the rules created for the non-Davidic kings to all kings.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “They are not liable for hearing the sound of an imprecation,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the reason of Rebbi Yose the Galilean is, <i>if he is poor and cannot afford it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:21" href="/Leviticus.14.21"><i>Lev.</i> 14:21</a>. This is a wrong quote since it refers to the sacrifice of the healed sufferer from skin disease. The expression used in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5" href="/Leviticus.5"><i>Lev.</i> 5</a> is וְאִם־לֹ֨א תַגִּ֣יעַ יָדוֹ֮ “if it is out of his reach” for the poor person and וְאִם־לֹא֩ תַשִּׂ֨יג יָד֜וֹ “if he cannot afford” for the poorest.</i>. Somebody who is apt to fall into poverty; this excludes the Anointed<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">He is not mentioned in our Mishnah text, but Mishnah 8 states that the High Priest is exempt according to everybody; only for the king does R. Aqiba disagree; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 9a" href="/Horayot.9a">Babli 9a</a>. According to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Horayot 1:7-8" href="/Tosefta_Horayot.1.7-8">Tosephta 1:10</a>, the king is exempted only for disregarding a request for testimony and the High Priest for violations of impurity (since his diadem is a permanent atonement for imperfect sacrifices, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 28:38" href="/Exodus.28.38"><i>Ex.</i> 28:38</a>.)<br>The High Priest is required (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:10" href="/Leviticus.21.10"><i>Lev.</i>21:10</a>) to be the richest priest; if he is not, the other priests have to make him so. R. Joseph David Sinzheim (<i>Yad David</i> on <i>Horaiot</i>) notes that the High Priest had the choice always to officiate at the burning of incense. Any other priest was given only a once in a lifetime occasion for this (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.2.3.1">Mishnah <i>Yoma</i> 2:4</a>) since presenting the incense made the presenter rich (explicit in the Babli, implicit in the Yerushalmi, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.2.3.2"><i>Yoma</i> Halakhah 2:4</a>, 40a 12). The king naturally has taxing powers.<br>Since king and High Priest are never able to bring a sacrifice according to the rules of the poor (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:7-10" href="/Leviticus.5.7-10"><i>Lev.</i> 5:7–10</a>) or the very poor (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:11-13" href="/Leviticus.5.11-13">vv. 11–13</a>), they are prohibited from ever bringing a sacrifice depending on the offerer’s wealth.</i> who is not apt to fall into poverty. [<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Text of B. It seems that this text presupposes a Mishnah mentioning only the Anointed; no such Mishnah is known.</i> They objected: There is the prince who is not apt to fall into poverty.] Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, <i>it shall be if he becomes guilty of any of these</i><sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:5" href="/Leviticus.5.5"><i>Lev.</i> 5:5</a>.</i>. He who can be liable for all of them is liable for part of them; but one who cannot be liable for all of them is not liable for part of them<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Since the king is exempt from testimony and the High Priest for violations of impurity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.7.1">Note 84</a>), neither of them is qualified to bring a sacrifice for all cases enumerated in vv. 1–4; they are not under the rules of vv. 6–7.</i>. Rebbi Isaac asked: Then he should not become impure by skin disease since he is not apt (easily and then) [to fall]<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The text in brackets, from B, is the only one making sense; the text of the ms., in parentheses, seems to be a scribal error.</i> into poverty or the deepest of poverty<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Since the verse quoted at the start of the Halakhah refers to the poor sufferer healed from skin disease. But there is no verse requiring that the sufferer from skin disease be able to bring all possible sacrifices; the question does not deserve an answer.</i>. Rav Hoshaia asked: Then a woman should not be liable for entering the Temple. Does the woman not bring<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Since a woman cannot be a formal witness in court, she cannot be the subject of an imprecation forcing here to testify. But the question is moot since women after childbirth are ordered in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:6-8" href="/Leviticus.12.6-8"><i>Lev.</i> 12:6–8</a> to bring a sacrifice after being impure.</i>? Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The reason of Rebbi Aqiba, <i>this is the offering of Aaron and his sons</i><sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:13" href="/Leviticus.6.13"><i>Lev.</i> 6:13</a>, the daily flour offering of the High Priest, identical in quantity to the variable sacrifice of the very poor. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 9a" href="/Horayot.9a">Babli 9a</a>.</i>. This one he brings; he does not bring another tenth of an <i>ephah.</i> Rebbi Zeˋira asked before Rebbi Yasa<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">This is the correct attribution, against the text of B.</i> may he not bring a voluntary offering? He told him, yes. He does not bring an obligatory one; he may bring a voluntary one.
So is the Mishnah, <sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that he does not judge is irrelevant for our topic (and certainly not true for Davidic kings.)</i>“since the king does not testify, nor may one testify against him.”

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> For all commandments of the Torah where one is liable to extirpation for willful infraction and a purification sacrifice for unintentional infraction, the individual brings a sheep or a she-goat<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:28" href="/Leviticus.4.28"><i>Lev</i>. 4:28</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:32" href="/Leviticus.4.32">32</a>.</i>, the Prince a he-goat<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:23" href="/Leviticus.4.23"><i>Lev</i>. 4:23</a></i>, and the Anointed or the Court bring a bull<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.6.1">Mishnaiot 1:6</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.1.1">2:1</a>.</i>. For idolatry the individual, the Prince, and the Anointed bring a she-goat, the Court bring a bull as elevation offering and a goat as purification sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:22-25" href="/Numbers.15.22-25"><i>Num.</i> 15:22–25</a>.</i>.<br>For a suspended reparation sacrifice the individual and the Prince are liable but the Anointed and the Court are not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 5.</i>. For a certain reparation sacrifice the individual, and the Prince, and the Anointed are liable but the Court is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">The reparation sacrifices for robberies or defrauding (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:20-26" href="/Leviticus.5.20-26"><i>Lev</i>. 5:20–26</a>), larceny of <i>sancta</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:14-16" href="/Leviticus.5.14-16"><i>Lev.</i> 5:14–16</a>), the semi-manumitted slave girl (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:20-22" href="/Leviticus.19.20-22"><i>Lev</i>. 19:20–22</a>), the <i>nazir</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:12" href="/Numbers.6.12"><i>Num.</i> 6:12</a>), and the healed sufferer from skin disease (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:1-32" href="/Leviticus.14.1-32"><i>Lev</i>. 14:1–32</a>). Since no extirpation is involved, the Court is not liable for a sacrifice in case they rule wrongly in one of these matters.</i>. For hearing a sound, or expression of the lips, or the impurity of the Temple and its <i>sancta,</i> the Court is not liable, the individual, and the Prince, and the Anointed are liable<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 6.</i> but the Anointed is not liable for the impurity of the Temple and its <i>sancta,</i> the words of Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">He is not mentioned in our Mishnah text, but Mishnah 8 states that the High Priest is exempt according to everybody; only for the king does R. Aqiba disagree; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 9a" href="/Horayot.9a">Babli 9a</a>. According to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Horayot 1:7-8" href="/Tosefta_Horayot.1.7-8">Tosephta 1:10</a>, the king is exempted only for disregarding a request for testimony and the High Priest for violations of impurity (since his diadem is a permanent atonement for imperfect sacrifices, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 28:38" href="/Exodus.28.38"><i>Ex.</i> 28:38</a>.)<br>The High Priest is required (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:10" href="/Leviticus.21.10"><i>Lev.</i>21:10</a>) to be the richest priest; if he is not, the other priests have to make him so. R. Joseph David Sinzheim (<i>Yad David</i> on <i>Horaiot</i>) notes that the High Priest had the choice always to officiate at the burning of incense. Any other priest was given only a once in a lifetime occasion for this (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.2.3.1">Mishnah <i>Yoma</i> 2:4</a>) since presenting the incense made the presenter rich (explicit in the Babli, implicit in the Yerushalmi, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.2.3.2"><i>Yoma</i> Halakhah 2:4</a>, 40a 12). The king naturally has taxing powers.<br>Since king and High Priest are never able to bring a sacrifice according to the rules of the poor (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:7-10" href="/Leviticus.5.7-10"><i>Lev.</i> 5:7–10</a>) or the very poor (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:11-13" href="/Leviticus.5.11-13">vv. 11–13</a>), they are prohibited from ever bringing a sacrifice depending on the offerer’s wealth.</i>. What do they bring? A variable sacrifice. Rebbi Eliezer says, the Prince brings a goat<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">This is qualified in the Halakhah.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “For all commandments of the Torah,” etc. <b>Halakhah 8:</b>“For a suspended sacrifice the individual and the Prince are liable,” etc. <i>A person,</i> to include the Prince<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:17" href="/Leviticus.5.17"><i>Lev</i>. 5:17</a>, the introduction to the rules for the suspended reparation sacrifice.</i>. Should it include the Anointed? “<i>And sinned inadvertently.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:15" href="/Leviticus.5.15"><i>Lev.</i> 5:15</a>. There are two problems with this quote: The first that it is a misquote, it reads וְחָֽטְאָה֙ בִּשְׁגָגָ֔ה not בְחָֽטְאָה֙ בִּשְׁגָגָ֔ה. This is easily explainable since in talmudic times under the influence of Greek every ב sounded like <i>v</i>. The serious problem is that the quote is from the paragraph detailing the rules of the fixed reparation sacrifice for larceny committed with <i>sancta.</i> It seems that the quote from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:17" href="/Leviticus.5.17"><i>Lev.</i> 5:17</a> refers to the full text וְאִם־נֶ֙פֶשׁ֙ <i>in addition, if a person . .</i> which in <i>Sifra Wayyiqra 2 Parašah</i> 12(1) is explained as meaning that the rules of the suspended reparation sacrifice, vv. 17–19 are an appendix to the rules of the reparation sacrifice for larceny involving <i>sancta,</i> vv. 14–16.</i>” Any depending on acting inadvertently. This excludes the Anointed who is not depending on acting inadvertently<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">He is liable for a sacrifice only if there is an element of ruling falsely, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>.</i>. But following Rebbi who said, the Anointed is depending on acting inadvertently<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:3:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.3.2-3">Halakhah 3</a>. Rebbi declares him liable for a bull and a goat without an element of ruling falsely in case the subject was idolatry, not in any other case. This permits to formulate the preceding argument so it remains valid even for Rebbi.</i>? One dependent on acting inadvertently in any situation. This excludes the Anointed who is not dependent on acting inadvertently in any situation. <i>A person,</i> to include the Prince and the Anointed<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:20" href="/Leviticus.5.20"><i>Lev.</i> 5:20</a>, the introduction to the rules for the reparation sacrifice for monetary offenses.</i>. Here you say, to include the prince, and there you say, to include the Anointed? <i>Like the purification sacrifices is the reparation sacrifice</i><sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:7" href="/Leviticus.7.7"><i>Lev.</i> 7:7</a>.</i>. Just as the purification sacrifice atones and wipes clean, also the reparation sacrifice atones and wipes clean. This excludes the suspended reparation sacrifice which atones but leaves a residue<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">If at the end it becomes clear that a sin had be committed which qualifies for a purification offering, the suspended offering did not take its place, and a second sacrifice is due. Therefore the rules for the suspended sacrifice are separate from those of other reparation sacrifices.</i>.
So is the Mishnah: “but the Anointed is not liable for the impurity of the Temple and its <i>sancta, everybody’s opinion, and neither is the Prince for hearing a sound,</i> the words of Rebbi Simeon.<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 9a" href="/Horayot.9a">Babli 9a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Horayot 1:7-8" href="/Tosefta_Horayot.1.7-8">Tosephta 1:10</a>. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 9b" href="/Horayot.9b">Babli 9b</a> points out that there are three levels of variable sacrifices and the argument of Note 84 excludes only the sacrifice of the very poor for the High Priest. In R. Simeon’s opinion, the High Priest is still liable at least for a poor man’s sacrifice for disregarding a summons to testify.</i>” Rebbi Joḥanan said, <i>and the Sanctuary he shall not leave, nor desecrate.</i> Therefore, if he left, he would not desecrate<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:12" href="/Leviticus.21.12"><i>Lev.</i> 21:12</a>. The argument seems to be: If the High Priest does not leave the Sanctuary, he has no occasion to desecrate it. Therefore the verse is read as: <i>and the Sanctuary he shall not leave; he will not desecrate.</i> The implication would be that the High Priest not only is exempt from bringing a sacrifice (which is a dubious distinction since it denies him a means of atonement) but his infraction of the Sanctuary’s purity does not need atonement.</i>. Rebbi Ashian<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">A student of R. Jonah’s. The reading of B, R. Joshia, referring to an Amora preceding R. Jonah by two generations, is impossible.</i>, Rebbi Jonah: Rebbi Abun bar Cahana found a difficulty. Is it not written, <i>a widow, or a divorcee, or a desecrated, a harlot, these he shall not marry,</i> therefore if he married he would not desecrate<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:13" href="/Leviticus.21.13"><i>Lev.</i> 21:13</a>. The next verse gives the reason for the prohibition: So he may not desecrate his descendants. Since the child of a Cohen from a woman forbidden to him by the special rules of the priesthood is desecrated, R. Johanan’s interpretation of v. 12 is shown to be unacceptable.</i>? What about it? <sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">A slightly different version of the following is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 9b" href="/Horayot.9b">Babli, 9b</a>.</i> Ḥizqiah said, <i>this person would be extirpated from the community</i><sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:19" href="/Numbers.19.19"><i>Num.</i> 19:19</a>. The entire Chapter deals with the preservation of the purity of the Sanctuary (<i>Sifry Num.</i> 129).</i><i>.</i> One whose sacrifice is identical to that of the community. This excludes the Anointed whose sacrifice is not equal to that of the community<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">By his office he is excluded from being one of the community. His sacrifice is either a bull or nothing; the sacrifice of a member of the community is the variable offering (a female sheep or goat, or two pigeons, or flour.)</i>. They objected, is not also the Prince’s sacrifice not equal to that of the community<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">It always is a goat.</i>? It is equal on the day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">On that day, the High Priest brings three sacrifices (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16" href="/Leviticus.16"><i>Lev.</i> 16</a>) cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.2.1">Note 16</a>. The first one for himself and his family; the second for his fellow priests and their families, and the third a double offering for the people. There the king (unless he is a usurping High Priest and king) is included with the people.</i>. But his brothers the priests are not equal on the day of Atonement! They are equal on the other days of the year<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">The lesser priests are subject to the rules of the variable value sacrifice.</i>. Rebbi Yudan bar Shalom said, they are equal in that the blood is given outside<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">On the Day of Atonement, only the blood of the first and third sacrifices are brought inside the Temple to purify the incense altar; the blood of the second sacrifice, the atonement of the priests, is sprinkled on the large outside alter like any other sacrifice. Similarly, the blood of the prince’s purification sacrifice is treated like that of a commoner, to be sprinkled on the outside altar.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Eliezer said this only because of his extirpation<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">Of all the sins calling for a variable sacrifice, only infractions of the purity of the sanctuary or of sacrifices are punished by extirpation. Therefore R. Eliezer lets him bring the fixed-value sacrifice prescribed for the Prince for all other cases of possible extirpation.<br>The entire paragraph has an inconclusive parallel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 9b" href="/Horayot.9b">Babli 9b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Hoshaia asked: If so, then even for a fixed [sacrifice]<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">If the argument of R. Johanan were correct, R. Eliezer also should require that the prince bring a he-goat, not a she-goat, as the fixed-value sacrifice required for inadvertent idolatry, against Mishnah 7.</i>? Rebbi Jonah said, Rebbi Hoshaia is of the opinion that he is uprooted from the entire paragraph; but Rebbi Eliezer treats him like a rich commoner<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">R. Hoshaia’s argument is unacceptable since only R. Yose the Galilean denies the Prince any variable-value sacrifice; we follow R. Aqiba who is granting him a sacrifice for any sin but freeing him from any obligation regarding a summons to testify.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, if Rebbi Eliezer treats him like a rich commoner, then also for hearing a sound, or expression of the lips<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the preceding Halakhah, the Court does not bring a sacrifice for a false ruling in a case subject to a sacrifice which depends on the wealth of the person. These are enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1-4" href="/Leviticus.5.1-4"><i>Lev.</i> 5:1–4</a>; the Mishnah uses the biblical expressions to characterize the different categories.<br>“Hearing of a voice” refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1" href="/Leviticus.5.1"><i>Lev.</i>5:1</a>: <i>If a person sin, for he heard the sound of an imprecation when he is a witness, or saw, or knew; if he does not tell he has to bear his iniquity.</i> If a person is asked by another to testify in his case before the court; he refuses and assents to an oath to the effect that he does not know about the case, if that was a lie he is subject to the variable reparation offering for swearing falsely.<br>“Expression of the lips” (v.4) refers to an oath made by a person on his own initiative but not kept since he forgot about his own oath. This also subjects the maker to the same obligation.<br>Improper handling of impurity because of oblivion is mentioned in vv. 2–3.</i>, as it was stated: Rebbi (Eleazar) [Eliezer] and the Sages did not differ about hearing a sound, or expression of the lips, that he does not bring a male goat but a she-goat<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">In fact only for expression of the lips.</i>. About what did they differ? About impurity of the Sanctuary and its <i>sancta,</i> where Rebbi Eliezer says since he is subject to extirpation why should he not bring a male goat instead of a she-goat<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Since the king cannot become poor, he should not be under the rules of variable-value sacrifices.</i>? They objected: Is there not the Anointed in the case of idolatry where he does not bring a male goat but a she-goat<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">Not only the Anointed but also the Prince are included in the rules for the individual inadvertently committing idolatry. Since there are no exceptions for the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15" href="/Numbers.15"><i>Num.</i> 15</a>, the argument made for R. Eliezer does not hold. In addition,; the she-goat of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5" href="/Leviticus.5"><i>Lev.</i> 5</a> is a reparation sacrifice but his he-goat of <i>Lev.</i> 43 a purification sacrifice.</i>.

Chapter 3



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> An Anointed Priest who sinned and then was removed from his anointed status, or a Prince who sinned and then was removed from his exalted status. The Anointed Priest brings a bull and the Prince brings a goat<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since at the moment of the sin they became obligated for the sacrifices, a later change of status has no influence. The difference between ecclesiastical and political offices will become clear in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.1.1">Mishnah 2</a>.</i>.<br>An Anointed Priest who was removed from his anointed status and then sinned, or a Prince who was removed from his exalted status and then sinned: The Anointed Priest brings a bull and the Prince is like a commoner<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Again the moment of the sin determines the kind of sacrifice. Priestly offices follow the rule that “one rises in holiness; one does not descend;” the former Anointed Priest remains in his status. He cannot officiate as Anointed Priest since another one holds the office; neither can he officiate as common priest since he may not descend in rank. The same is true for an unanointed High Priest. But a politician who loses his rank is free to run for any other office; his sacrifice is a female goat or sheep like everybody else’s.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “An Anointed Priest who sinned,” etc. <b>Halakhah 2:</b>“An Anointed Priest who was removed from his anointed status,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">A slightly enlarged version of a paragraph in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 2:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.2.1.3"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 2:1, Notes 14</a> ff. The paragraph is missing in B, as in all similar cases it was added by later editors of the Babli from the Yerushalmi. Only the few added verses are indicated here. The additions are printed in a different typeface. In a few places, the text here is slightly shortened.</i> Rebbi Eleazar said, if a High Priest sinned, one whips him but does not remove him from his elevated status. Rebbi Mana said, it is written: <i>For the crown of his God’s ointment is on him, I am the Eternal;</i> if one could compare it, just as I am in My Greatness, so Aaron is in his greatness. Rebbi Abun said, it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:8" href="/Leviticus.21.8"><i>Lev</i>. 21:8</a>.</i>: <i>Holy shall he be for you;</i> if one could compare it, I am in My Sanctity, so Aaron is in his sanctity. Rebbi Ḥanina the scribe, Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: if an Anointed Priest sinned, one whips him in a court of three [judges]. If you would say in a court of 23, his elevation would be his degradation. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, if a patriarch sinned, one whips him in a court of three [judges]. Does one return him? Rebbi Ḥaggai said, better if one did return him, he would kill them. Rebbi Jehudah the Prince heard this and became angry. He sent Goths to catch Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. He fled to Magdala, some say to Kefar Ḥiţţim. Rebbi Joḥanan went to the assembly hall; Rebbi Jehudah the Prince also went to the assembly hall. He said to him, why is the master not telling us words of instruction? He started clapping with one hand. He asked, does one clap with one? He answered him, no, but without ben Laqish there is nothing. He said, no. He told him, I shall free him. He said to him, in Magdala. He told him, I and you will go out to meet him. Rebbi Joḥanan sent to Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, prepare for yourself some words of instruction since the Patriarch will go out to meet you. He went out to meet them and said, your example is similar to that of your Creator. For when the Merciful went to liberate Israel from Egypt, He sent neither messenger nor angel but He went Himself, as is written: <i>I shall pass through the Land of Egypt in that night, not only</i> He but all His Court. It is not written in this context “when Power went” but <i>when Powers went</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 7:23" href="/II_Samuel.7.23"><i>2S</i>. 7:23</a>.</i>. They asked him, why did you say these things? He told them, what are you thinking? That for fear of you I would refrain from the teachings of the Merciful? As Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac said, <i>No my sons, because the reputation is not good,</i> etc., <i>you remove the Eternal’s people</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 2:24" href="/I_Samuel.2.24"><i>1S</i>. 2:24</a>. This quote is the gist of the entire sermon, missing in <i>Sanhedrin.</i></i>, one removes him.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If they sinned before being appointed; when afterwards they were appointed, they remain commoners<sup class="footnote-marker">1,</sup><i class="footnote">Since at the moment of the sin they became obligated for the sacrifices, a later change of status has no influence. The difference between ecclesiastical and political offices will become clear in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.1.1">Mishnah 2</a>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">For the purposes of this sacrifice.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, if it became known to them before they were appointed, they are obligated; if after they were appointed they are not liable. Who is the Prince? This is the king, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:22" href="/Leviticus.4.22"><i>Lev.</i> 4:22</a>.</i>, <i>if he transgressed one of the commandments of the Eternal, his God;</i> a Prince who has none above him but the Eternal, his God. And who is the Anointed? This is one anointed with the anointing oil, not one clothed in multiple garb<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Making the anointing oil was commanded personally to Moses (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:25" href="/Exodus.30.25"><i>Ex.</i>30:25</a>). All High Priests up to the time of king Josiah were anointed with it. Since that time, the oil was no longer available; it cannot be reconstituted. The later High Priests were inducted into their office by investiture with the High Priest’s garments.</i>.<br>The only difference between the priest anointed with the anointing oil and the one clothed in multiple garb is the bull brought for all commandments<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The rules about the High Priest’s purification sacrifice explained in Chapter 2 became obsolete with the destruction of the First Temple and could be restored to validity only if a dig on the Temple Mount would recover the flask containing the original oil. The High Priests of the Second Temple had the status of commoners in this respect.</i>. And the only difference between an officiating High Priest and a deposed one is the bull of the Day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Which has to be acquired by the High Priest with his own money together with a goat (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:3" href="/Leviticus.16.3"><i>Lev.</i> 16:3</a>).</i> and the tenth of an <i>ephah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The personal daily offering of the High Priest, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:12-16" href="/Leviticus.6.12-16"><i>Lev.</i> 6:12–16</a>, of about 3.84 l of fine flour.</i>.<br>Both are equal in the office of the day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">If the acting High Priest becomes impure or otherwise incapacitated, a former High Priest can replace him without special dedication. No common priest can perform any of the prescribed acts of the Day of Atonement.</i>, commanded about the virgin<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:13" href="/Leviticus.21.13"><i>Lev.</i> 21:13</a>. This applies only if the High Priest marries while High Priest. If he married a widow while a common priest, he still may be elevated to High Priest.</i>, and prohibited for a widow<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:14" href="/Leviticus.21.14"><i>Lev</i>. 21:14</a>.</i>, and do not defile themselves for close relatives<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:11" href="/Leviticus.21.11"><i>Lev</i>. 21:11</a>.</i>, and may not let their hair grow<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:10" href="/Leviticus.21.10"><i>Lev</i>. 21:10</a>.</i> or rend their garments<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:10" href="/Leviticus.21.10"><i>Lev</i>. 21:10</a>. These are forbidden as mourning rites.</i>, and let the homicide return<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 35:25" href="/Numbers.35.25"><i>Num.</i> 35:25</a> (where anointing is mentioned), 32 (where anointing is not mentioned).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If they sinned before being appointed,” etc. The colleagues say that the reason of Rebbi Simeon is because greatness atones<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">As will become clear later (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.3">Note 29</a>), it is not the high office which atones but the appointment to high office. This is the Babli’s interpretation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 13:1" href="/I_Samuel.13.1"><i>1S.</i> 13:1</a>, that Saul was 1 year old when he became king; his sins were remitted and he was innocent like a one year old baby (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 22b" href="/Yoma.22b"><i>Yoma</i> 22b</a>).<br>The colleagues are Rav Hanania and Rav Oshia, two Babylonians of the third generation of Amoraim who lived in Galilee but never held office there.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, because his sin and his knowledge are not equal<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">This is the only opinion mentioned in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 3a" href="/Horayot.3a">Babli, 3a</a>. Since the status of the individual at the moment of the sin determines the appropriate sacrifice, if later his status changes he is prevented from sacrificing.</i>. What is the difference<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Are there practical differences depending on which doctrine one chooses? Since only R. Simeon’s opinion is discussed, it seems that the Yerushalmi accepts his as practice, as far as these rules have practical applications.</i> between them? Referring to the beginning of the Chapter: “An Anointed Priest who sinned and then was removed from his anointed status, or a Prince who sinned and then was removed from his exalted status. The Anointed Priest brings a bull and the Prince brings a goat;” if there is any doubt whether they sinned. He who said, because greatness atones; just as it atones for the certain [sin] so it atones for the doubt. He who said. because his sin and his knowledge are not equal; his sin and his knowledge are not equal<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">There is no practical difference between rulings based on the colleagues’ or R. Yose’s opinions.</i>. If they sinned before they were appointed and after they were appointed they breached, for him who said, because greatness atones, greatness atoned for the first, but he is liable for the second and the third<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The first case occurred before he was appointed, the second while he was in office, the third after he was removed from office. According to the colleagues, the induction into exalted office cancels the previous sins; once he entered office no further benefits accrue; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.3">Note 29</a>.</i>. For him who said, because his sin and his knowledge are not equal<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">No detail is given since the answer is complicated; the problem is taken up again in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.11">Note 64</a>. There is no sacrifice possible if the status of the sinner has changed between the date of the sin and the realization that it happened. The only problem is that of a sin committed in stage 1 which becomes known in stage 3; whether or not a sacrifice is possible depends on the difference between R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:2:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.2.2-8">1:2</a>, cf. there <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.13">Note 74</a>; cf. also the following <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.11">Note 61</a>.</i>. If they sinned regarding hearing a sound, or expression of the lips, or the impurity of the Temple and its <i>sancta,</i> for him who said, because his sin and his knowledge are not equal, here his sin and his knowledge are equal<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">A sacrifice of variable value is required in all three cases; for R. Simeon under the restrictions of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.7.1">Chapter 2, Note 84</a>.</i>. For him who said, because greatness atones, even in this case greatness atones<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The complications discussed in Chapter 2 regarding the variable sacrifice become irrelevant by the elevation of a person to exalted status.</i>.
Rebbi Mattania said: Greatness cannot atone (until his greatness becomes known to him) [unless his error became known to him.]<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is from L, the one in brackets is from B. It seems that the latter is the correct one. Since it was established in the preceding paragraph that in the opinion of the colleagues it is the act of elevation which wipes off earlier guilt, only those transgressions which are known to the person being elevated are being atoned for. The argument is also acceptable to R. Yose since once the transgression became known to the person, it determines the sacrifice which he is obligated to bring. If then his status changes, he might be prevented from ever bringing this sacrifice.</i> If he ate half the volume of an olive before he was appointed and half the volume of an olive after he was appointed, even in one oblivion he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">While the consumption of any forbidden food is sinful, the transgression is prosecutable, or if committed inadvertently requires a sacrifice, only if the amount consumed was at least the volume of an olive (except it one ate a complete being, such as an ant.) All transgressions committed during one period of oblivion are added together, on condition that their accumulation result in one and the same sacrifice. But if his appointment changes the nature of the sacrifice, the partial transgressions cannot be added.</i>. A doubt of half the volume of an olive before he was appointed and a doubt of half the volume of an olive after he was appointed: he brings a suspended reparation offering<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Since the appointment does not change the nature of the required suspended reparation sacrifice (except that the Anointed Priest is not liable for possible infractions relating to the purity of the Temple and its <i>sancta.</i>)</i>. Do we find anything where for the actual offense he is not liable but for a doubt he is liable? We do not find this! If he ate the volume of two olives, the true nature of one of them became known to him, the other remained for him in doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">This now refers to anybody and is not subject to varying purification sacrifices. If he ate two different foods, for one it became known to him that it was forbidden, the other is only suspected of not being kosher. Then for the forbidden food he is liable to bring a purification sacrifice, for the suspected one a suspended reparation sacrifice. Bringing a suspended reparation sacrifice implies an obligation to bring a purification sacrifice if it should become clear that a sin had actually been committed.</i>. Rebbi Jacob the Southerner asked before Rebbi Yose: As you take it, if he ate forbidden fat, it atoned. [If he ate permitted fat, it atoned.]<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The text in brackets is from B; it makes for a smoother reading but one could argue that it is redundant.<br>R. Jacob’s argument is that a second purification sacrifice (and possibly the suspended reparation sacrifice) should be unnecessary (and therefore impossible) since if the two kinds of food were both known to be not kosher and they were eaten in one period of oblivion, one purification sacrifice would be prescribed. If the second food turned out to be kosher, no sacrifice would be needed.</i> Rebbi Yose said, anything which convinced him to bring a suspended reparation offering, the knowledge of the doubt establishes his transgression<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The previous argument is rejected. The obligation of sacrifices is fixed at the moment the person realizes the sinfulness of his actions. Since at that moment, the status of the second food remained in doubt, it required a suspended reparation sacrifice. But a suspended reparation sacrifice carries with it the implicit obligation of a purification sacrifice in case the situation could be cleared up.</i>. So we find a case where for the actual offense he is not liable but for a doubt he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">In case of a certain violation, one sacrifice would have been due. Because of the doubt, two or three are due now. The previous statement is disproved.</i>.
One ate the volume of an olive before he was appointed and the volume of an olive after he was appointed. If it was in one oblivion, he is liable only for one [sacrifice]<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The moment of realization of the transgression determines the kind of sacrifice required. Since the Anointed and the king are forbidden to offer the sacrifice of a commoner and vice-versa, only one sacrifice is possible.</i>. If the volume of an olive was in doubt before he was appointed, and the volume of an olive was in doubt after he was appointed, in one oblivion he is liable only for one, in two forgettings he is liable for two [sacrifices.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Since a suspended reparation sacrifice is authorized for everybody, it can be offered both by a commoner and by an Anointed Priest or a king. But it was established earlier that elevation starts new obligations of sacrifices. Therefore, obligations of suspended reparation sacrifices before and after elevation cannot be combined.</i>]
One ate the volume of three olives but was of the opinion that he ate only two. {He selected a sacrifice following Rebbi Joḥanan.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Text of L.</i>} [If he selected one sacrifice, this atones.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Text of B. The two texts have the same meaning; B’s is more easily understood. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 71b" href="/Shabbat.71b">Babli, <i>Šabbat</i> 71b</a>, switches the attributions between RR. Johanan and Simeon ben Laqish.<br>For R. Simeon ben Laqish, the dedication of the sacrifice to atone for two infractions requires a new sacrifice for the third. For R. Joḥanan, the one sacrifice automatically is valid for the third also.</i>] For Rebbi Joḥanan said, if part of the sin was atoned for, all of the sin was atoned for. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, if part of the sin was atoned for, not all of the sin was atoned for.
One ate five times the volume of an olive; he separately realized a doubt about each one. Afterwards it became known to him (in court) [as a certainty.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is from L, the one in brackets from B. Since the testimony as to the occurrence of a sinful act by a single witness in court is sufficient to obligate the perpetrator for a sacrifice (even though a single witness is not admissible in any criminal procedure and may be contradicted by an oath in civil proceedings) the text in parentheses has to be preferred as <i>lectio difficilior</i> while the meaning for the English reader is more easily understood from the text of B.</i>] Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, the knowledge about his doubt determines his kind of transgression. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the knowledge about his doubt does not determine his kind of transgression<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The problem discussed here has no direct connection with change of status; it applies as well to a commoner who progressively becomes aware of multiple transgressions of the same kind; <i>Ševuot</i>2:1 (33d l. 10) <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 18b" href="/Keritot.18b">Babli <i>Keritut</i> 18b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 19b" href="/Shevuot.19b"><i>Ševuot</i>19b</a>. The Babli finds here a tannaitic controversy. It was stated that the awareness of a transgression determines the obligation of a purification sacrifice, but the obligation of a suspended reparation sacrifice may cover separate incidents. The question then arises what are the obligations if the doubts about a single suspended reparation sacrifice are resolved on different occasions? (In the Babli, R. Simeon ben Laqish’s opinion is attributed to Rebbi, that of R. Johanan to Rebbi’s teachers R. Yose ben R. Jehudah and R. Eleazar ben R. Simeon.)<br>In the example, the doubt is whether he ate permitted or prohibited fat.</i>. Rebbi Yose bar Abun in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish agrees that for the Anointed Priest the knowledge about his doubt does not determine his kind of transgression<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The paragraph is referred to in <i>Ševuot</i>2:1.<br>Since the Anointed Priest is barred from bringing a reparation sacrifice, the knowledge of the doubt has no influence on his status.</i>. What is the reason? <i>Like purification offering, like reparation offering</i><sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:7" href="/Leviticus.7.7"><i>Lev.</i> 7:7</a>. The verse appears in a different context, i. e., that the technicalities of purification and reparation sacrifices are identical [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Tzav, Chapter 9 1" href="/Sifra,_Tzav,_Chapter_9.1"><i>Sifra Ṣaw Pereq</i> 9(1</a>)]. In Maimonides’s opinion, the quote here is an allusion, not a proof.</i>. The knowledge about his doubt determines his kind of transgression for one who brings a suspended reparation offering. The knowledge about his doubt does not determine his kind of transgression for one who does not bring a suspended reparation offering.
The argument of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish seems to be inverted. There he says, the knowledge about his doubt determines his kind of transgression. But here he says, the knowledge about his doubt does not determine his kind of transgression. There, his reparation offering determines it. Here what do you have<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon ben Laqish is not inconsistent. Here, he holds that the moment which determines one’s obligation for a reparation offering also determines the conditions for a future purification offering. But in <i>Ševuot</i> 2:1, the doubt arises about impurity of the Sanctuary which is not subject to a suspended reparation offering. There is no sacrifice which could define future obligations.</i>?
The argument of Rebbi Joḥanan seems to be inverted. There, he said, if part of the sin was atoned, all of the sin was atoned. And here he says so? Rebbi Joḥanan said this only for the last realization which does not require any sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph also appears in <i>Ševuot</i>2:1; its original place is here. In the case of three olives he holds that the supplementary in- formation about the third olive is irrelevant since the purification sacrifice for the first two also covers the third. In the case of the volume of five olives, the supplementary information that he ate forbidden fat triggers the obligation of a purification sacrifice which did not exist before; it is relevant.</i>.
Everybody agrees that if the first one was still in existence it is pushed aside<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of three volumes of olive size R. Johanan asserts that if a sacrifice was brought for the first two before the person was informed of the third, no additional sacrifice is needed. But if the animal was only dedicated to atone for two and before it was slaughtered its owner was informed of the third, it is asserted that the dedication becomes invalid and a new animal is needed even according to R. Johanan. (Therefore, a careful person will formally dedicate his animal at the last possible moment, just before entering the Temple precinct. An intention to use the animal, short of formal dedication, is no obstacle to using it for another purpose.)</i>. What should be done with it? Rebbi Yose said, it is suspended for atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">It should be left to graze until it develops a bodily defect or becomes too old to serve as sacrifice, then be sold and its proceeds used to buy the new purification sacrifice.<br>While both sources here read “R. Yose”, the only acceptable reading is that of L in the next paragraph, “R. Yasa”, of the generation of R. Zeˋira’s teachers. R. Yose was the student of R. Zeˋira’s student R. Jeremiah.</i>. Rebbi Zeˋira said, where neither itself nor its blood are usable it dies immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">He objects that the animal now has the status of a purification sacrifice whose owner’s sin was atoned for by another animal, which has to be left to die (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 2:2" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.2.2">Mishnah <i>Temurah</i> 2:2</a>) since it can neither be redeemed, nor used, nor allowed to produce young which would perpetuate its impossible situation.</i>.
If he ate five times the volume of an olive; the doubt became known to him before he was appointed (and in court) [as the certainty]<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is from L, the one in brackets from B. Since the testimony as to the occurrence of a sinful act by a single witness in court is sufficient to obligate the perpetrator for a sacrifice (even though a single witness is not admissible in any criminal procedure and may be contradicted by an oath in civil proceedings) the text in parentheses has to be preferred as <i>lectio difficilior</i> while the meaning for the English reader is more easily understood from the text of B.</i> after he was removed<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">One now applies the preceding discussion to the Mishnah, following R. Simeon who insists that the status of the person at the moment at which he receives the information determines his liability.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish he is not liable; in the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">In this interpretation, since according to R. Simeon ben Laqish the obligation of a suspended sacrifice implies that of the corresponding purification sacrifice, the prior obligation which was eliminated by the appointment is not re-instituted by removal from office. For R. Joḥanan the status of the suspended sacrifice is irrelevant for the purification offering. Since after removal the person is again under the rules of a commoner, his obligation is not changed.</i>. Did Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish state to free from liability, not rather to insist on liability<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Since the original statement of R. Simeon ben Laqish referred to a case where he is more restrictive than R. Joḥanan, it is inadmissible to quote him in support of a more lenient position. In the case considered there is unanimity that for a transgression committed as a commoner, which could not have been atoned for while one was elevated, the original obligation of a purification sacrifice of a female sheep or goat is re-instituted.</i>? But it must be as follows: He ate five times the volume of an olive. The doubt became known to him before he was appointed; the certainty after he was appointed. Following the opinion of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish who said, the knowledge about his doubt determines his kind of transgression, he must say<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The construction in L is rather awkward; that of B is more smooth, but this probably indicate that the text of B is babylonized.</i> that one is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Since the obligation preceded the elevation it cannot be removed following R. Simeon.</i>. Following the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan who said, the knowledge about his doubt does not determine his kind of transgression, he must say that one is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Since the obligation of a purification sacrifice became known when the person was prohibited from offering a commoner’s sacrifice, following R. Simeon the person is prevented from offering any sacrifice.</i>. Everybody agrees that if the first one was still in existence it is pushed aside<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of three volumes of olive size R. Johanan asserts that if a sacrifice was brought for the first two before the person was informed of the third, no additional sacrifice is needed. But if the animal was only dedicated to atone for two and before it was slaughtered its owner was informed of the third, it is asserted that the dedication becomes invalid and a new animal is needed even according to R. Johanan. (Therefore, a careful person will formally dedicate his animal at the last possible moment, just before entering the Temple precinct. An intention to use the animal, short of formal dedication, is no obstacle to using it for another purpose.)</i>. What should be done with it? Rebbi Yasa said, it is suspended for atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">It should be left to graze until it develops a bodily defect or becomes too old to serve as sacrifice, then be sold and its proceeds used to buy the new purification sacrifice.<br>While both sources here read “R. Yose”, the only acceptable reading is that of L in the next paragraph, “R. Yasa”, of the generation of R. Zeˋira’s teachers. R. Yose was the student of R. Zeˋira’s student R. Jeremiah.</i>. Rebbi Zeˋira said, if neither itself not its blood are usable, it dies immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">He objects that the animal now has the status of a purification sacrifice whose owner’s sin was atoned for by another animal, which has to be left to die (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 2:2" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.2.2">Mishnah <i>Temurah</i> 2:2</a>) since it can neither be redeemed, nor used, nor allowed to produce young which would perpetuate its impossible situation.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">A similar problem is treated in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 11a" href="/Horayot.11a">Babli, 11a</a>.</i> If one ate half the volume of an olive before he was appointed, half the volume of an olive after he was appointed, half the volume of an olive after he was removed from office. Since [no]<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Text of B, necessary in the text. Since no full olive-sized piece of forbidden food was eaten while the person was in his privileged appointed status, the previous argument that the new obligation of sacrifices invalidates the old one does not apply. The question is raised whether the two unrelated episodes of a commoner’s obligation can be added. In the Babli, the question remains undecided in principle.</i> obligation of a sacrifice came in between, do they combine? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 71b" href="/Shabbat.71b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 71b</a>.</i>: Before him were three [olive sized pieces]<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Text of B.</i>. He ate the first but did not realize it; the second (and the third)<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Text of L. It is preferable to delete it since if the second and third olives are eaten under similar circumstances, the remaining text of the statement becomes redundant.</i> while he was oblivious of the first. He was informed of the first but was not informed of the second. [He ate] the third while oblivious of the second. Afterwards he was informed about all of them. Rebbi Joḥanan said, he is liable for the first and the second but not liable for the third<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">When he became obligated for a purification sacrifice for the first piece, he did not know of his obligation for the second. Following R. Simeon, the obligation for the first piece and the future one for the second are incompatible. But if the information about the second and the third reaches him after the third had been eaten, one sacrifice covers both of them.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, the second depends on his intention. If he wishes, he atones for it with the first; if he wishes, he atones for it with the third<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">He disputes R. Johanan’s interpretation of R. Simeon’s position. Since the first and second pieces were eaten in ignorance, even if the information reached the perpetrator piecemeal, one sacrifice still may cover both. The argument for using one and the same sacrifice for the second and third pieces is the same as that given by R. Joḥanan.</i>. The colleagues compare it to four half-olives<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">He ate pieces 1,2,3,4 in a situation when while eating piece <i>i</i> he was informed about the forbidden character of piece <i>i</i>–2 but not yet about piece <i>i</i>–1. (If one of these numbers be 0 or negative, the relative information is void.) Then one sacrifice covers pieces 1 and 2 which were eaten in ignorance. But since at the time he was eating piece 3 he already was informed about piece 1, the sacrifice for 1 and 2 cannot be applied to 3. Since he was informed about 2 but not 3 when he ate 4, the last two pieces can be atoned for by one sacrifice.</i>. If he was intelligent, he brings one sacrifice; otherwise, he brings two sacrifices. How is that? He brings one for the first and the second together; one for the third and fourth together. (Rebbi Yose compares it to full olive sizes. If he was intelligent, he brings two sacrifices; otherwise, he brings three sacrifices. How is that? He brings one for the first and the second together, and one for the third and fourth together.)<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">This text, missing in B, is an intrusion of the later text into the current discussion; it should be disregarded.</i> If he brought for the middle ones, he is not liable for the first and the fourth<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The intelligent person will dedicate his sacrifice for pieces 2 and 3. Then 1 is a single half-olive which does not qualify for a sacrifice. The same holds true for 4. Since the information about 1 was available when 4 was consumed, 1 and 4 do not combine.</i>. Rebbi Yose compares it to full olive sizes. If he was intelligent, he brings two sacrifices; otherwise, he brings three sacrifices. How is that? He brings one for the first and the second together, and one for the third and fourth together. If he brought for the middle ones, he is liable for the first separately and for the fourth separately<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The situation contemplated by R. Yose is parallel to that considered by the colleagues but this time each of the pieces has the full volume of an olive. Then each piece itself qualifies for a sacrifice. The second best solution of the colleagues now becomes the best for R. Yose; their best now is the second best.</i>.
Rebbi Isaac asked: Is it the same with the eating of half loaves<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">פְּרַס is the technical term for half a loaf of bread. (The punctuation פְּרָסִים is the ms.’s. The term is derived either from Hebrew פרס “to break bread” or Latin <i>pars</i> “part, share, portion” of food). The time needed to eat half a loaf serves as definition of the time needed for a minimal meal. For example, any person entering an infected house becomes impure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:46" href="/Leviticus.14.46"><i>Lev.</i> 14:46</a>) but his garments also become a source of original impurity only if he stays there for a meal, i. e., the time needed to eat half a loaf (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:47" href="/Leviticus.14.47">v. 47</a>). <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Negaim 7:9-10" href="/Tosefta_Negaim.7.9-10">Tosephta <i>Negaˋim</i> 7:10</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.2.1">Mishnah <i>Eruvin</i> 8:2</a>) defines the loaf in question as baked from wheat flour in the volume of a third of a <i>qab.</i> <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 5:1:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.5.1.11">Halakhah <i>Terumot</i> 5:3 (Note 44</a>) estimates a <i>modius,</i> 4 <i>qab,</i> as the volume of 96 eggs. This makes a פְּרַס a piece of bread baked from the volume of 4 eggs of wheat flour. The sources give no indication of a translation of this definition into terms of time.<br>Snacks eaten at times separated by more that the time needed to eat a <i>peras</i> must be counted as separate meals. Therefore, eating forbidden food triggers the obligation of a purification sacrifice only if the volume of an entire olive was eaten in the time needed to eat a <i>peras</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Keritot 3:3" href="/Mishnah_Keritot.3.3">Mishnah <i>Keritut</i> 3:3</a>).</i>? Rebbi Yose said, this question of Rebbi Isaac is nothing. (Is it written) [Does]<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is from L, the one in brackets from B. It seems that the scribe of L read וכ׳ instead of וכי and unthinkingly interpreted כ׳ as the common abbreviation of כְּתִיב.</i> the matter depend on eating half loaves? If one ate the volume of half an olive during the time allotted for this half loaf and another volume of half an olive during the time allotted for that half loaf, is that anything<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.11">Note 56</a>, no atonable sin was committed.</i>? If one ate the volume of several olives during times allotted for several half-loaves in one oblivion, he is liable only for one [sacrifice]<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">As explained before, if an atonable sin was committed, any number of sins corresponding to the same definition committed during one spell of oblivion are atoned for by one sacrifice.</i>. The rabbis of Caesarea say, instead of comparing it to kinds of fat, why not compare it to [the laws of] Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">This remark does not fit in here; it shows that the text was taken from <i>Šabbat</i> 1:1 (2b line 32 ff.) where R. Yose’s (the Amora’s) remark here is put in the mouth of R. Yudan (the Amora) to explain the opinion of R. Yose (the Tanna) about violations of Sabbath prohibitions. It is forbidden to transport goods from one place to another on the Sabbath. “Transporting” means taking up the goods, moving them from one domain to another, and unloading. If any of the three actions be missing, no prosecutable sin was committed. In addition, for each kind of goods the Mishnah specifies minimal amounts. If less than the amount specified was transported while the person was oblivious of the Sabbath, no purification offering is required. On the other hand, if any number of transports were executed within one period of oblivion, only one sacrifice is needed. R. Yose the Tanna then specifies that repeated transports of less than a minimal amount add up to an atonable sin only if the different pieces were transported between the same domains [<i>Šabbat</i> 1:1 (2b line 22), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 80a" href="/Shabbat.80a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 80a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 55b" href="/Bava_Batra.55b"><i>Bava batra</i> 55b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 17a" href="/Keritot.17a"><i>Keritut</i> 17a</a>.] This is compared to the rules specifying purification sacrifices for eating pieces of forbidden fat. The rabbis of Caesarea object that there is no proof that the rules of the Sabbath be identical to the rules for other biblical prohibitions, but the rules detailed for transporting are paralleled by rules for other kinds of activities on the Sabbath. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 13:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.13.1.1">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 13:1</a> states that weaving is forbidden and the threshold for an action requiring a purification sacrifice is weaving two threads. For the majority, weaving two threads in one oblivion triggers the obligation of a sacrifice, for R. Yose only if the two rows were added to the same piece of cloth.</i>? If he wove a single thread for one cloth and a single thread for another cloth, is that anything? If he wove several threads for several pieces of cloth in one oblivion, he is liable only for one [sacrifice]. Was there not an obligation for a sacrifice in the meantime, and you say that they are counted together<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Now one returns to the problem posed in the preceding paragraph, of the person who ate three half-olive sized pieces of forbidden fat while he was in different states for possible purification sacrifices. Is it possible to decide between R. Joḥanan on one side and R. Yose (the Amora) and the colleagues on the other? For the latter, it should make sense to combine the two pieces eaten while the person was a commoner. This is rejected, since the two states of commoner are separated by an interval in which the purification offering had to be different, everybody will agree that the three half sized pieces do not add up to one full sized piece.</i>? Rebbi Abun said, there it is about the obligation of a sacrifice; but here [a change]<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Text of B, forgotten by the scribe of L.</i> of sacrifice.
If one ate the volume of an olive before he was appointed, the volume of an olive after he was appointed, and the volume of an olive after he was removed from office. In the opinion of the colleagues who say, because high office atones<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">As will become clear later (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.3">Note 29</a>), it is not the high office which atones but the appointment to high office. This is the Babli’s interpretation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 13:1" href="/I_Samuel.13.1"><i>1S.</i> 13:1</a>, that Saul was 1 year old when he became king; his sins were remitted and he was innocent like a one year old baby (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 22b" href="/Yoma.22b"><i>Yoma</i> 22b</a>).<br>The colleagues are Rav Hanania and Rav Oshia, two Babylonians of the third generation of Amoraim who lived in Galilee but never held office there.</i>, the office atoned for the first and he is liable for the second and the third<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">As explained at the start of the chapter, it is not the high office but the appointment to high office which atones; the occupant of a high office is responsible for his actions like everybody else.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Yose who said, because his sin and his knowledge are not equal<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">This is the only opinion mentioned in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 3a" href="/Horayot.3a">Babli, 3a</a>. Since the status of the individual at the moment of the sin determines the appropriate sacrifice, if later his status changes he is prevented from sacrificing.</i>, he is liable for the first and the second, but not liable for the third<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to make sense of this statement. If the information became known in stage three, which seems to be the hypothesis, there can be no sacrifice for unresolved sins committed in stage 2. The only problem would be a sin committed in stage 1, for which a sacrifice in stage 3 was ruled out by R. Abun in the preceding paragraph. The author of this paragraph seems to disagree with R. Abun.</i>. He ate the size of an olive. If it is in doubt whether he ate before he was appointed or after he was appointed<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">In the first case, a purification sacrifice is needed but not in the second, both for the colleagues and R. Yose.</i>, or whether it was before he converted or after he converted<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The Gentile is not required to observe biblical commandments except the Seven Noahide commandments. In no case is a purification sacrifice possible for an unconverted Gentile.</i>, or whether it was before he had grown two pubic hairs or after he had grown two pubic hairs<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is from L, the one in brackets from B. It seems that the scribe of L read וכ׳ instead of וכי and unthinkingly interpreted כ׳ as the common abbreviation of כְּתִיב.</i>, he brings a suspended reparation sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">This means that a reparation sacrifice is required not only in case the criminality of the act is in question but even if the possibility of a purification sacrifice is in doubt.</i>.
If he ate a full olive sized piece<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Kosher food, which only is forbidden on the Day of Atonement.</i> but there is doubt whether he ate it on the Day of Atonement or before the Day of Atonement. The atonement atones for the doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.1.4">Chapter 1, Note 19</a>.</i>. Whether he ate it on the Day of Atonement or after the Day of Atonement. The colleagues say, atonement atones for the doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">If the food was eaten in the twilight at the end of the Day of Atonement and the next day already had started, no sin was committed (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:32" href="/Leviticus.23.32"><i>Lev</i>. 23:32</a>). If it still was the day of Atonement, doctrine is that in the absence of a scapegoat the end of the day provides the Atonement [<i>Yoma</i> 8:8 45c l. 15; <i>Ševuot</i> 1:9 33c l. 3, lacunary <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 10:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.10.1.9"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 10:1 Note 34</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Yoma (Lieberman) 4:17" href="/Tosefta_Yoma_(Lieberman).4.17">Tosefta <i>Kippurim</i> 4:17</a>].</i>. Rebbi Mattaniah said, the atonement atones only for doubt of the kinds of blood<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">R. Mattaniah holds that the Day of Atonement only eliminates a suspended reparation sacrifice which certainly was due. But in the case where the doubt arises whether it was the Day of Atonement or not, there is no prior obligation and, therefore, it cannot be eliminated.</i>. A Mishnah supports the colleagues: “Sabbath and the Day of Atonement and he worked in twilight.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Keritot 4:2" href="/Mishnah_Keritot.4.2">Mishnah <i>Keritut</i> 4:2</a>. The Mishnah deals with a case which is impossible in our computed calendar, where the Day of Atonement was either Friday or Sunday and somebody was doing some forbidden work during the twilight between the days. R. Joshua, the overriding authority, holds that in this case no suspended sacrifice is due since one may assume that part of the work was done on the day of Atonement; this part of an unintended sin then is eliminated, leaving an incomplete work for the Sabbath which needs no sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.11">Note 60</a>). This shows that the colleagues are correct in extending the power of the Day of Atonement.</i>” As you take it, if it was the Day of Atonement, it atoned. If it was weekday, it is permitted. But did we not state “he ate”<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The questioner thinks that the food in question was forbidden fat. In that case, the Day of Atonement cannot eliminate the obligation of a suspended sacrifice for the following night. The answer is that in contrast to the cases considered earlier, one supposes that kosher food was eaten.</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, eating permitted [food].
If he ate five times the volume of an olive; the doubt became known to him before he was appointed, and (in court)<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.3">Note 30</a>. It seems that one should read “its certainty”.</i> after he was appointed. In the opinion of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish he is liable; in the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Simeon ben Laqish holds that his status at the moment of the first notification determines his obligation for sacrifices (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.6">Note 43</a>), he became obligated for a purification offering. If now he is not a commoner, he is obligated for an exalted person’s purification offering. For R. Johanan who disagrees (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.7">Note 44</a>), the knowledge of the doubt does not determine the obligation when he is informed of the certainty. Following R. Simeon, action and information referred to different status; no sacrifice is possible.</i>. Did Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish state to insist on liability, not rather to free from liability<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reverse of the question asked in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.6">Note 41</a>; in any case the argument is inconclusive since the exact position of R. Simeon ben Laqish is unknown.</i>? But it must be as follows: He ate five times the volume of an olive<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Before he was appointed.</i>. The doubt became known to him after he was appointed; (in court)<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.3">Note 30</a>. It seems that one should read “its certainty”.</i> after he was removed. Following the opinion of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish who said, if the sin was partially atoned for it was not totally atoned, he must say<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The paragraph is referred to in <i>Ševuot</i>2:1.<br>Since the Anointed Priest is barred from bringing a reparation sacrifice, the knowledge of the doubt has no influence on his status.</i> that he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">One does not argue directly from R. Simeon ben Laqiah’s statement but from his argument, based on the principle stated, in the case of three pieces when he realized only that he had eaten two (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.1">Note 19</a>). R. Simeon ben Laqish holds that the moment of first information determines his status for sacrifices. Since he was informed of a transgression as a commoner when he was exalted and therefore prevented of bringing a sacrifice, had he then been informed of the certainty, the additional information reaching him after he reverted to commoner status cannot change the situation. R. Johanan, who in the situation of Note 29 extends the validity of the sacrifice, will allow the information reaching him as a commoner to determine the sacrifice due for an act committed as a commoner.</i>.
