Following the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan who said, if the sin was partially atoned for it was totally atoned, he must say that one is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">While he ate the five pieces, he was oblivious either of the fact that these were forbidden fat or of the law that certain kinds of fat are forbidden. Then he was informed of the forbidden character of these pieces one by one and immediately after each information dedicated an animal but did not sacrifice yet.</i>. Everybody agrees that if the first one was still in existence it is pushed aside<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of three volumes of olive size R. Johanan asserts that if a sacrifice was brought for the first two before the person was informed of the third, no additional sacrifice is needed. But if the animal was only dedicated to atone for two and before it was slaughtered its owner was informed of the third, it is asserted that the dedication becomes invalid and a new animal is needed even according to R. Johanan. (Therefore, a careful person will formally dedicate his animal at the last possible moment, just before entering the Temple precinct. An intention to use the animal, short of formal dedication, is no obstacle to using it for another purpose.)</i>. What should be done with it? Rebbi Yasa said, it is suspended for atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">It should be left to graze until it develops a bodily defect or becomes too old to serve as sacrifice, then be sold and its proceeds used to buy the new purification sacrifice.<br>While both sources here read “R. Yose”, the only acceptable reading is that of L in the next paragraph, “R. Yasa”, of the generation of R. Zeˋira’s teachers. R. Yose was the student of R. Zeˋira’s student R. Jeremiah.</i>. Rebbi Zeˋira said, if neither it nor its blood are usable it dies immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">He objects that the animal now has the status of a purification sacrifice whose owner’s sin was atoned for by another animal, which has to be left to die (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 2:2" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.2.2">Mishnah <i>Temurah</i> 2:2</a>) since it can neither be redeemed, nor used, nor allowed to produce young which would perpetuate its impossible situation.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan agrees that if the first one was still in existence it is pushed aside. What should be done with it? Rebbi Yasa said, it is suspended for atonement. Rebbi Zeˋira said, if neither it nor its blood are usable it dies immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this paragraph is superfluous text. The scribe copied the preceding text with the wrong start and then copied again the (almost) correct text in the next paragraph.</i>.
Does Rebbi Joḥanan agree that if the first one was still in existence it is pushed aside<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose ben R. Abun will show that the often repeated statement that “everybody agrees” is false; R. Johanan disagrees; the explanation given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.4">Note 36</a> has to be amended.</i>? What should be done with it? Rebbi Yasa said, it is suspended for atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">These two sentences are copied from the previous statement; they are copied to point out that R. Zeˋira’s statement is disregarded since it would lead to a complication in R. Simeon ben Laqish’s statement.</i>. Therefore Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun discussed it. If he ate five times the volume of an olive<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">While he ate the five pieces, he was oblivious either of the fact that these were forbidden fat or of the law that certain kinds of fat are forbidden. Then he was informed of the forbidden character of these pieces one by one and immediately after each information dedicated an animal but did not sacrifice yet.</i>; the first became known to him and he brought a sacrifice. The second, and he brought a sacrifice. The third, and he offered a sacrifice. The fourth, and he offered a sacrifice. The fifth, and he offered a sacrifice. Rebbi Joḥanan said, his sin is atoned for by the first which precedes the eating of all of them; the remainder shall fall to voluntary offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is the same as in the case of three pieces discussed earlier; one constructs a case for five only because for R. Joḥanan in the case of three only one animal was needed. One could have done with four pieces.<br>For R. Joḥanan, the validity of the dedication of the first animal can be extended to cover all five pieces. The other four animals cannot be used, but dedicated animals cannot become undedicated. They are sent to graze until they either develop a defect which makes them unfit for the altar or they exceed the age limit for sacrificial animals (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Parah 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Parah.1.1">Mishnah <i>Parah</i> 1:1</a>) when they can be sold and the money used for voluntary elevation offerings.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, his sin is atoned for by the last which follows the eating of all of them; the others shall be set aside<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Since for him dedications cannot be extended, only the last animal can legitimately be sacrificed. The others have to be sent to graze.</i>. Rav Ḥisda and Rav Hamnuna. Rav Ḥisda like Rebbi Joḥanan; Rav Hamnuna like Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. Rav Ḥisda remarked to Rav Hamnuna, there is a Mishnah which seems to support you and disagrees with me<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Keritot 4:2" href="/Mishnah_Keritot.4.2">Mishnah <i>Keritut</i> 4:2</a>.</i>: “If there was awareness in between, just as he brings a purification sacrifice for each single one, so he brings a suspended reparation sacrifice for each single one<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Since temporary oblivion is a prerequisite for the possibility of a purification sacrifice, sins committed when there was an interval of awareness between them cannot be atoned for by one and the same sacrifice. This rule is extended to suspended sacrifices. Since in the case in question the selection of new sacrifices was in response to information, one should read the Mishnah as forbidding the extension of the meaning of dedications.</i>.” If he had stated the reparation sacrifice and stopped<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">The formulation of the Mishnah and the explanation given in the preceding Note are all wrong. The verses introducing purification sacrifices emphasize that these atone only for <i>unintentional</i> sins. The requirement of <i>oblivion</i> is only mentioned for the suspended reparation sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:17" href="/Leviticus.5.17"><i>Lev</i>. 5:17</a>). The Mishnah should have mentioned suspended sacrifices first. Since purification sacrifices were mentioned first, the Mishnah cannot be read as referring to the effect of information after the fact, only to information reaching the person between two intrinsically forbidden acts.</i>! Rebbi Ḥinena said, even so, for all eventualities you may say so<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Hisda’s objection is well taken. The formulation of the Mishnah is elliptic. One should read it as follows: “Just as he brings a purification sacrifice for each single one if there was awareness <i>of certainty</i> in between, so he brings a suspended reparation sacrifice for each single one if there was awareness <i>of doubt</i> in between.”</i>.
[It is written]<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">From B, missing in L.</i>: <i>If the Prince sin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:22" href="/Leviticus.4.22"><i>Lev</i>. 4:22</a>. The sermon is mentioned in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 10" href="/Horayot.10">Babli 10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 7:5" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).7.5">Tosephta <i>Bava qamma</i> 7:5</a>, <i>Sifra Hova (Wayyiqra II) Parašah</i> 5(1). It is standard homiletics to derive the conjunction אֲשֶׁר from the root אשר “to be fortunate.”</i> Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai said, fortunate [is the generation]<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">From B, missing in L.</i> whose Prince brings a purification sacrifice. He brings it for his inadvertent sin, not so much more for his intentional one<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">There is no formal atonement for intentional sin. If the Prince is aware of his unintentional missteps, he will be careful to avoid intentional ones.</i>? If its Prince brings, not so much more the commoner?
“The Prince.” I could think a tribal chieftain like Naḥshon; the verse says, “<i>if he transgressed one of the commandments of the Eternal, his God</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:22" href="/Leviticus.4.22"><i>Lev.</i> 4:22</a>.</i>; and further it says, <i>that he may learn to fear the Eternal, his God</i><sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 17:19" href="/Deuteronomy.17.19"><i>Deut</i>. 17:19</a>. This is justification for the short statement in the Mishnah. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 11" href="/Horayot.11">Babli 11a/b</a>, <i>Sifra Ḥova (Wayyiqra II) Parašah</i>5(1).</i>. “His God, [his God]” for an equal cut. Since “his God” mentioned there refers to a Prince over whom there is only [the Eternal]<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">From B, missing in L.</i> his God, so also “his God” mentioned here refers to a Prince over whom there is only [the Eternal]<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">From B, missing in L.</i> his God<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is the same as in the case of three pieces discussed earlier; one constructs a case for five only because for R. Joḥanan in the case of three only one animal was needed. One could have done with four pieces.<br>For R. Joḥanan, the validity of the dedication of the first animal can be extended to cover all five pieces. The other four animals cannot be used, but dedicated animals cannot become undedicated. They are sent to graze until they either develop a defect which makes them unfit for the altar or they exceed the age limit for sacrificial animals (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Parah 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Parah.1.1">Mishnah <i>Parah</i> 1:1</a>) when they can be sold and the money used for voluntary elevation offerings.</i>.
[It is written:]<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">From B, missing in L.</i> <i>There are just people</i><sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 8:14" href="/Ecclesiastes.8.14"><i>Eccl</i>. 8:14</a>. Since the parallel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 10b" href="/Horayot.10b">Babli, 10b</a>, also has only a short quote, it seems that the extensive quote of the verse in B is secondary. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kohelet Rabbah 8:15" href="/Kohelet_Rabbah.8.15"><i>Eccl. rabbati</i> 8(15</a>), wording of B.</i> <i>[to whom happens what should happen to evildoers and there are evildoers to whom happens what should happen to just people.]</i><sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">From B, missing in L.</i> It is fortunate for just people if to them happens in this world what should happen to evildoers; woe to evildoers if to them happens in this world what should happen to just people<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">This is a continuation of Rabban Johanan ben Zakkai’s homily about אשר, אשרי. The Just who are poor and unhappy in this world have already been punished for their sins and will go to the World to Come for unlimited eternal bliss. (The Babli somewhat disagrees.) The evildoers who have received the reward of their good deeds in this world will go to the World to Come for unmitigated pain.</i>.
A king of Israel and a king of Jehudah are both equal, neither of them is greater than the other. What is the reason? <i>The king of Israel and [Josaphat]</i><sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">From B, missing in L.</i> <i>the king of Jehudah [were sitting]</i><sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">From B, missing in L.</i> <i>in the threshing floor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 22:10" href="/I_Kings.22.10"><i>1K</i>. 22:10</a>.</i>. As in a threshing floor<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">They sat together in a circle (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 4:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.4.8.1">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 4:9</a>) so that none had any advantage over the other. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 5a" href="/Chullin.5a">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 5a</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Vayikra Rabbah 11:8" href="/Vayikra_Rabbah.11.8"><i>Lev. rabba</i> 11(8</a>); <i>Eccl. rabbati</i> 1(30)).</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, but only up to Jehu ben Nimshi. What is the reason? <i>Your descendants in the fourth generation will sit on the throne of Israel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 15:12" href="/II_Kings.15.12"><i>2K</i>. 15:12</a>. Jeroboam became king with prophetic sanction; Baˋsha at least had prophetic acknowledgment; Omri was appointed by popular acclaim, and Jehu by prophetic anointment. All permanent kings of Israel from Jeroboam I to Jeroboam II’s son had religious sanction. After that the kings of Israel with only one exception murdered their precedessors.</i>. After that they were taking it by robbery.
“And who is the Anointed? This is one anointed with the anointing oil,” etc. Rav Huna said, the entire six months during which David was in flight before his son Absalom, his sins would have been atoned for by a female goat for a commoner<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">The same statement also is found in <i>Roš Haššanah</i> 1:1 (56b l. 49). A king who is not in control of his government does not have the status of king [Cf <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 2:4:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.2.4.2-5"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 2:4</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:21" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.21">Note 108</a>)].</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">This text is part of a longer text found in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 8:3:5-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.8.3.5-7"><i>Soṭah</i> 8:3, Notes 69–92</a> (L. Ginzberg, <i>Yerushalmi Fragments from the Genizah</i>, New York 1909, p. 214), and, what seems to be the original source, <i>Šeqalim</i> 6:1, 49c l. 52 ff., Babli <i>editio princeps</i> 9d l. 21. Only the biblical quotes and major deviations from the <i>Sotah</i> text are noted here.</i> It was stated: Rebbi Jehudah bar Ilai says: The anointing oil made by Moses on the Mountain<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sotah</i> and <i>Šeqalim</i>: “in the desert.” The place is not mentioned in B.</i> was from beginning to end a series of miracles since there were only twelve <i>log</i> to start with, as it was said: <i>and olive oil one hin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:24" href="/Exodus.30.24"><i>Ex</i>. 30:24</a>.</i>. It would not have been enough to rub the wooden planks with it; so much more since the fire swallows, the kettle swallows, wood absorbs! From it the Tabernacle and all its vessels were anointed, the altar and all its vessels, the candelabra and all its vessels, the wash basin and its base. From it Aaron the High Priest and his sons were anointed all of the seven days of induction; from it all high priests and kings were anointed. A king who is first needs anointing; a king who is a king’s son does not need anointing, for it is said: <i>Do anoint him</i>, <i>for this one is it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 16:12" href="/I_Samuel.16.12"><i>1S</i>. 16:12</a>.</i>, this one needs anointing, but his son does not need anointing. But a High Priest who is the son of a High Priest needs anointing even for ten generations. Nevertheless, it is there for the future, as it was said: <i>a holy anointing oil will this be for Me</i>, <i>for all your generations</i><sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:31" href="/Exodus.30.31"><i>Ex</i>. 30:31</a>.</i>.
One anoints kings only at a spring, as it was said: <i>Let Solomon, my son, ride on my mule and take him down to the Giḥon</i>; <i>there Ṣadoq the priest and Nathan the prophet shall anoint him as king over Israel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 1:33-34" href="/I_Kings.1.33-34"><i>1K</i>. 1:33–34</a>.</i>. One anoints kings only because of disputes. Why was Solomon anointed? Because of the dispute of Adoniahu, Joash because of Athaliah, Jehu because of Joram. Is it not written, <i>do anoint him, for this one is it</i>, this one needs anointing, but the kings of Israel do not need anointing? <sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">In all other sources, this follows the quote about the sons of Josiah. If Josiah had buried the holy oil together with the ark, how could his son have been anointed?</i> But did not Josiah hide it? That means that they anointed with balsamum. Joaḥaz because of his brother Joakin who was two years his elder. One anoints kings only from a horn. Saul and Jehu were anointed from a can because their kingdom was temporary; David and Solomon were anointed from a horn because their kingdom was permanent. One does not anoint priests as kings. Rebbi Jehudah Antordiya said, because of <i>the scepter shall not be removed from Jehudah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 49:10" href="/Genesis.49.10"><i>Gen</i>. 49:10</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, because of <i>he shall have many days of his kingdom, he and his sons in the midst of Israel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 17:20" href="/Deuteronomy.17.20"><i>Deut</i>. 17:20</a>.</i>. What is written after that? <i>The levitic Cohanim should not</i><sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 18:1" href="/Deuteronomy.18.1"><i>Deut</i>. 18:1</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, Joḥanan is Joaḥaz. But is it not written: <i>The first born Joḥanan</i><sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Chronicles 3:15" href="/I_Chronicles.3.15"><i>1Chr</i>. 3:15</a>.</i>, the first in kingdom. <sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">There the introduction is missing, that R. Joḥanan identified Sedekiah and Shallum as one and the same person. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Horayot 11b" href="/Horayot.11b">Babli 11b</a> this and the following are a tannaitic statement.</i><i>The third Sedekiah</i>, <i>the fourth Shallum</i>? <i>Sedekiah</i>, because he accepted the judgment on himself, <i>Shallum</i>, because in his days the dynasty of David was completed. <sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">In the <i>Šeqalim</i> text of the Babli <i>editio princeps</i> the statement is attributed to R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i> His name was neither Shallum nor Sedekiah but Mattaniah. That is what is written: <i>The king of Babylon made his uncle Mattaniah king in his stead</i><sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 24:17" href="/II_Kings.24.17"><i>2K</i>. 24:17</a>.</i> etc.
<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the last sentence of the Mishnah, which continues with discussion of Mishnah 4 (= <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.10.1">Mishnah <i>Megillah</i> 1:12</a>).</i>“The anointed with the anointing oil,” in the first Temple. “The one clothed in multiple garb,” in the later Temple. It follows what Rebbi Ina<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 21b" href="/Yoma.21b">Babli (<i>Yoma</i> 21b</a>) and the Horaiot text in the Babli, as well as the parallels in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Makkot 2:6:2-7:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Makkot.2.6.2-7.4"><i>Makkot</i> 2:7</a> (explained in Notes 125–130), <i>Taˋaniot</i> 2:1 (65a l. 60): R. Samuel bar Ainia. Since the latter name appears as that of a student of R. Aḥa several times in different Tractates but “R. Ina” only here, the reading of B is preferable.</i> said in the name of Rebbi Aḥa: In five things was the later Temple deficient compared with the first. What is the reason? <i>Go to the mountain</i>, <i>bring wood</i>, etc. <i>I should be honored</i> is missing a <sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Haggai 1:8" href="/Haggai.1.8"><i>Hag</i>. 1:8</a>. אכבד is the <i>Ketib</i>, אכבדה the <i>Qere</i>. In the Alexandrian system of numeration by letters, ה׳ is 5.</i>ה. These are the five things in which the later Temple was deficient compared with the first. And these are it: The fire<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">The fire on the outer altar in the first Temple was of divine origin (<a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 7:1" href="/II_Chronicles.7.1"><i>2Chr.</i> 7:1</a>), but not that of the second Temple.</i>, the ark, Urim and Tummim<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">The oracle whose nature was unknown in later times.</i>, the (holy) oil [of anointing and the holy spirit.]<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Text of B; a necessary addition since the text of L mentions only 4 items. The list in the Babli is slightly different. The holy spirit is that of prophecy.</i>
It was stated: The Anointed Priest brings a bull, the one clothed in multiple garb does not bring a bull. This disagrees with Rebbi Meïr. What is Rebbi Meïr’s reason? <i>The Anointed</i>. Why does the verse say <i>priest</i>? To exclude the one clothed in multiple garb. What is the rabbis’ reason? <i>The anointed</i>. I could think that this is the king. Why does the verse say, <i>priest</i>? To include the one clothed in multiple garb. Here you say, to exclude the one clothed in multiple garb. But there you say, to include the one clothed in multiple garb. Rebbi Hila said, each inference refers to its meaning. If it had said <i>the Anointed</i> but not <i>priest</i>, I would have said, he brings a bull for forgetting a topic, but for acting in error he brings a goat. Therefore it is necessary that it mention <i>priest</i>. But if it had mentioned <i>priest</i> but not <i>the Anointed</i>, I would have said, this refers to the king. If you would say by a bull, preceding the paragraph about the king, assuming that for forgetting a topic he brings a bull but for acting in error he brings a goat. Therefore it is necessary that it mention <i>the Anointed</i> and that it mention <i>priest</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">The text and the following paragraphs up to the quote from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Eduyot 5:6" href="/Mishnah_Eduyot.5.6"><i>Idiut</i> 5:6</a> is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:10:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.10.2-9"><i>Megillah</i> 1:12</a>. The secondary character of the text here is shown by the thorough corruption of the present paragraph compared to the parallel text in <i>Megillah</i> and partially <i>Sifra Ḥovah (Wayyiqra 2) Paršeta</i> 2(6). One might conjecture that the editor of B neither did want to rearrange the text nor print it in disorder. The text of <i>Megillah</i> is readily understandable; it also explains the mutilated text here. The additional text is given in a different typeface.<br>תַּנִּי. כֹּהֵן הַמָּשִׁיחַ מֵבִיא פָר. אֵין הַמְרוּבֶּה בְגָדִים מֵבִיא פָּר. וּדְלֹא כְרִבִּי מֵאִיר, <b>דְּרִבִּי מֵאִיר אָמַר. הַמְרוּבָּה בְגָדִים מֵבִיא פָר.</b> מַה טַעֲמֵיהּ דְּרַבִּי מֵאִיר, מָשִׁים. וּמַה תַלְמוּד לוֹמַר כֹּהֵן, <b>לְרַבּוֹת אֶת הַמְרוּבֶּה בְגָדִים.</b> מַה טַעֲמוֹן דְּרַבָּנָן. מָשִׁים. יָכוֹל זֶה הַמֶּלֶךְ. <b>תַּלְמוּד לוֹמַר כּהֵן. אי כהן יָכוֹל אַף מַרוּבָּה בגָדִים. תַּלְמוּד לוֹמַר מָשִׁיחַ. אוֹ יָכוֹל שֶׁאֲנִי מַרְבֶּה אַף מְשוּחַ מִלְחָמָה. תַּלְמוּד לוֹמַר מָשִׁיחַ, שֶׁאֵין עַל גַבָּיו מָשִׁיחַ, מִחְלְפָה שִׁיטתּוֹן דְרַבָּנָן, הָכָא כְתִיב מָשִׁיחַ וְהָכָא כְתִיב מָשִׁיחַ, הָכָא אִינוּן אֶמְרִין.</b> לְרַבּוֹת מְרוּבֶּה בְגָדִים. <b>וְהָכָא אִינוּן אָמְרִין.</b> לְהוֹצִיא אֶת הַמְרוּבֶּה בְגָדִים, אָמַר רַבִּי אִילָא. כָּל־מִדְרַשׁ וּמִדְרַשׁ בְּעִנְיָנוֹ. <b>תַּמָּן כָּל־הַפָּרָשָׁה אֲמוּרָה בְאַהֲרן. לְאֵי־זֶה דָבָר נֶאֱמַר כֹּהֵן,</b> לְרַבּוֹת אֶת הַמְרוּבֶּה בְגָדִים. <b>בְּרָם הָכָא אֵין הַפָּרָשָׁה אֲמוּרָה בְאַהֲרן.</b> אִילוּ נֶאֱמַר מָשִׁיחַ וְלֹא נֶאֱמַר כֹּהֵן. הָיִיתִי אוֹמֵר, לְעוֹלָם עַל הֶעֶלֶם דָּבָר מֵבִיא פָר וְעַל שְׁנְגַת מַעֲשֶׂה מֵבִיא טָעִיר. הַוֵי צוֹרֶךְ הוּא שֶׁיֹּאמַר כֹּהֵן. או אִלוּ נֶאֱמַר כֹּהֵן וְלֹא נֶאֱמַר מָשִׁיחַ, הָיִיתִי אוֹמֵר. זֶה הַמֶּלֶךְ. אִין תּאמַר, כְּבָר קָדְמָה פָרָשַׁת הַמֶּלֶךְ. <b>הָיִיתִי אוֹמֵר.</b> עַל הֶעֶלֶם דָּבָר מֵבִיא פָר וְעַל שִׁנְגַת מַעֲשֶׂה מֵבִיא שָׂעִיר. הַנִי צוֹרֶךְ הוּא שֶׁיֹּאמַר מָשִׁיחַ וְצוֹרֶךְ הוּא שֶׁיֹּאמַר כֹּהֵן.<br>It was stated: The Anointed Priest brings a bull, the one clothed in multiple garb does not bring a bull. This disagrees with Rebbi Meir, for Rebbi Mei"r said, the one clothed in multiple garb brings a bull<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">The definite article used in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:3" href="/Leviticus.4.3">Lev. 4:3</a>, the priest, would alone have sufficed to characterize the High Priest, biblically distinguished from all others.</i>. What is Rebbi Meir's reason? The Anointed. Why does the verse say priest? To add the one clothed in multiple garb<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Horayot 2:3" href="/Tosefta_Horayot.2.3">Tosephta 2:3</a>.</i>. What is the rabbis' reason? The anointed. I could think that this is the king. The verse says, priest. If priest, I could think the one clothed in multiple garb. The verse says, anointed<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">The double restriction, the priest (the High Priest), anointed, makes it clear that only an anointed high priest is meant. The rabbinic disagreement implies that no High Priest of Second Temple times ever brought a purification sacrifice for himself.</i>. Then I could think that I am adding also the one anointed for war<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">The one mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:3" href="/Deuteronomy.20.3">Deut. 20:3</a> charged with addressing the army. He also is called the priest (Sotah Chapter 8) and bound by all restrictions imposed on the High Priest in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21" href="/Leviticus.21">Lev. 21: 10-15</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Horayot 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Horayot.2.1">Tosephta 2:1</a>).</i>. The verse says, Anointed; one who has no anointed person over him. The argument of the rabbis seems inverted. Here<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:15" href="/Leviticus.6.15">Lev. 6: 15</a>, on the daily flour sacrifice of the High Priest.</i> is written anointed and there is written anointed. Here they say, to include the one clothed in multiple garb<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 4 mentions the daily offering of a tenth of a ephah as duty of the High Priest clothed in multiple garb [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Tzav, Chapter 5 1" href="/Sifra,_Tzav,_Chapter_5.1">Sifra Saw Pereq 5(1</a>)].</i>. But here<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">In the Chapter on purification sacrifices.</i> they say, to exclude the one clothed in multiple garb. Rebbi Hila said, each inference refers to its meaning. There the entire paragraph is said for Aaron. Why is said priest? To include the one clothed in multiple garb<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Aaron and his successors are mentioned in v. 13. In v. 15, the mention of "the priest, anointed from his descendants in his stead" does not seem to require a mention of anointing as a definition.</i>. But here the paragraph does not mention Aaron. If it had said the Anointed but not priest, I would have said, he brings a bull for forgetting a topic, but for acting in error he brings a goat<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:3:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.3.2-3">Chapter 2:3</a>, The High Priest may offer a bull only for his forgetting a topic in religious law. One could argue that for simple acting in error, he should bring a commoner's sacrifice (or, since a male is mentioned, the goat characterized earlier as sacrifice for inadvertent idolatry.) The specific mention of priest bars him from a commoner's sacrifice.</i>. Therefore it is necessary that it would mention priest. But if it had mentioned priest but not the Anointed, I would have said, this refers to the king<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Since Cohen may simply mean "public servant" (<a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 8:18" href="/II_Samuel.8.18">2S. 8: 18</a>).</i>. If you would say already this<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">The unintelligible בפר in the text here is a plausible misreading for כבר.</i> precedes the paragraph about the king<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Which is only the third In the Chapter. The argument is parallel to that mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:26" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.26">Note 131</a>.</i>, I would have said that for forgetting a topic he brings a bull but for acting in error he brings a goat. Therefore it is necessary that it mention the Anointed and that it mention priest.</i>
Rebbi Joḥanan said, if he transgressed and offered his tenth of an <i>ephah</i> it is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Which is only the third in the Chapter. The argument is parallel to that mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:26" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.26">Note 131</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">From here on there is a parallel in <i>Yoma</i> 1:1 (38c l. 72 ff.).</i>“One arranges for another Cohen as his replacement, maybe a disqualification of his will happen.<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 1:1:1-27" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.1.1.1-27">Mishnah <i>Yoma</i> 1:1</a>. Since the entire service of the Day of Atonement is valid only if conducted by the High Priest, a replacement must be available in case the High Priest becomes impure or otherwise incapacitated. The High Priest undergoes a week of preparation for the service, to train for a very crowded program. The question then arises whether the designated backup also has to undergo the same training, possibly at the same place.</i>” How? Does one leave them alone together? Rebbi Ḥaggai said, by Moses<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Yoma</i> “because of”, a scribal error. “By Moses” was a preferred expression of R. Ḥaggai’s.</i>! If one would leave them alone together, he would kill him! <i>Him</i><sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:12" href="/Leviticus.6.12"><i>Lev</i>. 6:12</a>; the offering of the High Priest starting with the day he is anointed for his office. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Tzav" href="/Sifra,_Tzav"><i>Sifra Saw</i> <i>Parašah</i> 3(3</a>). The singular indicates that only one High Priest can be appointed at one time. This implies that the reserve appointee for the day of Atonement cannot have the status of High Priest unless he actually is needed.</i>. One anoints one, one does not anoint two. Rebbi Joḥanan said, because of rivalry<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees and holds that while the two could not have been anointed on the same day, they could have been anointed on different days. The rule that the back-up Cohen has lower status is practical, not biblical, as is the entire institution of the back-up.</i>.
If one was incapacitated and the other officiated. The first has all the sanctity of the High Priesthood on him; the second one is qualified neither as High Priest nor as common priest<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">As the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 12b" href="/Yoma.12b">Babli explains (<i>Yoma</i> 12b</a>), “one increases in sanctity but never decreases” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 3:3:15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.3.3.15"><i>Bikkurim</i> 3:3, Note 57</a>; <i>Yoma</i> 3:8 41a l. 10, <i>Megillah</i> 1:12 72a l. 47, <i>Ševuot</i> 1:8, 33b l.13). Since the service of the Day of Atonement is valid only if performed by the High Priest, the substitute becomes a temporary High Priest. He cannot act as a High Priest if the actual High Priest’s temporary disability is removed and he is permanently barred from acting as a common priest. As the Babli points out, if the High Priest dies, the substitute automatically becomes his successor.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, if he transgressed and officiated, his officiating is (invalid) [valid]<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is that of L, the one in brackets that of B as well as the parallels in <i>Megillah</i> and <i>Yoma</i> and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 13a" href="/Yoma.13a">Babli (<i>Yoma</i> 13a</a>). The text of L cannot be correct since it is held in general that a doubt about the legitimacy of officiating in the Temple does not invalidate the offering (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.1.4"><i>Terumot</i> 8:1, Note 26</a>).</i>. Whose officiating? <sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">The case is told not only in the two parallels in <i>Megillah</i> and <i>Yoma</i>, but also in abbreviated form in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 12b" href="/Yoma.12b">Babli, <i>Yoma</i> 12b</a> where, however, the ruling is not the king’s (necessarily of the Herodian dynasty) but “the rabbis’.” There is no reason to doubt the historicity of the Yerushalmi version.</i> Let us hear from the following: It happened to Ben Illem from Sepphoris<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">This translation follows B and the parallels. The text of L, “in Sepphoris”, is impossible.</i> that the High Priest experienced an emission of semen on the Day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">The High Priest is taken to live in the Temple, and therefore deprived of sexual activity, for seven days preceding the Day of Atonement. In the night of the Day of Atonement he is deprived of sleep (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 1:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.1.7.1"><i>Yoma</i> Mishnah 1:7</a>) to avoid the danger of him having an involuntary emission. If he has one anyhow, he is disqualified for the entire day even if he immediately purifies himself in a <i>miqweh</i> since the disqualification by temporary impurity is not removed by the removal of the impurity until the following sundown (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:7" href="/Leviticus.22.7"><i>Lev</i>. 22:7</a>; cf. <i>Maˋaser Šeni</i> 3:2, Notes 21–22.)</i>; Ben Illem entered and officiated in his stead. He went out and asked the king: “The bull and the ram which are brought today, from whose property are they offered? From his or from the High Priest’s?<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">In addition to the two parallels there is a short version in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 47a" href="/Yoma.47a">Babli, <i>Yoma</i> 47a</a>. There, the names are Ismael and Joseph. The passages are discussed in detail by Grätz, <i>Geschichte der Juden</i> vol. 3/2<sup>4</sup> <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.1">Note 19</a>/II. Josephus transscribes קִמְחִית בֶּן as τοῦ Kαμύδου.</i>” The king understood what he was asking and answered him, “is it not enough for you that you served once before Him Who spoke and the world was created?” Ben Illem understood that he was removed from the High Priesthood.
The remainder of the text (to line three of the next paragraph) is missing in B without indication of a lacuna.<br> <sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">In addition to the two parallels there is a short version in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 47a" href="/Yoma.47a">Babli, <i>Yoma</i> 47a</a>. There, the names are Ismael and Joseph. The passages are discussed in detail by Grätz, <i>Geschichte der Juden</i> vol. 3/2<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup> <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.1">Note 19</a>/II. Josephus transscribes קִמְחִית בֶּן as τοῦ Kαμύδου.</i> It happened that Simeon ben Qimḥit went out for a walk with the <sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">The text is not clear here. The text of L is also found in the parallel in <i>Yoma</i>. The text of B, “with the Arab (Nabatean) king” is also the text of <i>Megillah</i>. In the Babli, “with an Arab on the Day of Atonement”. (For some reason, the Russian censor of the Wilna Babli changed “Arab” into “a nobleman”.) The confusion comes from the similarity if ערב “evening” and ערבי “Arab.” The explicit reference to sundown should argue for the version of L. If the Arab was not converted to Judaism, the High Priest would have had to leave the holy precinct on the Day of Atonement, a most unlikely happening before he had finished all his duties.<br>The version of the text presupposes that the king had immersed himself in a <i>miqweh</i> so he could enter the restricted area on the Temple Mount. Nevertheless, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Chagigah 2:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Chagigah.2.7.1">Mishnah <i>Hagigah</i> 2:7</a>) “The garments of the vulgar are severely impure for Pharisees; the garments of Pharisees are severely impure for those eating heave; the garments of those eating heave are severely impure for those sacrificing.” The severe impurity of מִדְרָס referred to here is the impurity of seats or beds used by a sufferer from gonorrhea or a menstruating woman, which makes anyone touching it impure and requires immersion in water and waiting until sundown. Since the king could not sacrifice on the Day of Atonement, he could not have immersed himself with the intention which would make him co-pure with the High Priest. Since the incident happened at sundown, the High Priest was automatically disqualified for the next 24 hours.<br>While a living Gentile is not under the rules of biblical impurity, rabbinically every Gentile is impure and this impurity cannot be removed by immersion in water (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 36b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.36b">Babli <i>Avodah zarah</i> 36b</a>).</i> king on the Day of Atonement at sundown and a drop of spittle squirted on his garment and defiled him. His brother Jehudah entered and officiated in his stead. On that day their mother saw two of her sons as High Priests. Qimḥit had seven sons; all of them served as High Priests<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Under the Herodian kings, when the High Priesthood was conferred and removed at the whim of the king.</i>. They sent and asked Qimḥit, what good deeds are in your hand? She told them, there should come over me if the beams of the roof of my house ever saw the hair on my head or the seam of my undershirt<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">It is indecent for a married woman to be seen in public with uncovered hair. She was clothed at home as she was for the street and never undressed except in the dark. The Babli notes that many women follow this custom.</i>. They said, all flours are flour but Qimḥit’s flour is fine flour<sup class="footnote-marker">159a</sup><i class="footnote">A pun on the name of <i>Qimhit</i> “flour lady”.</i>. They recited about her the verse<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 45:14" href="/Psalms.45.14"><i>Ps</i>. 45:14</a>.</i>: <i>All the honor of the king’s daughter is inside</i>; <i>gold settings her garments</i><sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">According to Rashi’s commentary in <i>Yoma</i>, the argument means that the reward of a woman who behaves with dignity in her home is that her son will be High Priest whose garment is adorned with golden settings for precious stones. (Cf. <i>Tanḥuma Wayyišlaḥ</i> 6, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 3" href="/Numbers.3"><i>Bemidbar</i> 3</a>.)</i>.
I could think that the one anointed for war<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">The one mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:3" href="/Deuteronomy.20.3"><i>Deut</i>. 20:3</a> charged with addressing the army. He also is called <i>the</i> priest (<i>Soṭah</i> Chapter 8) and bound by all restrictions imposed on the High Priest in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:10-15" href="/Leviticus.21.10-15"><i>Lev</i>. 21:10–15</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Horayot 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Horayot.2.1">Tosephta 2:1</a>).</i> should (not)<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">Text of L, missing in the two parallels and contradicted by the following text.</i> bring his tenth of an <i>ephah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 4 mentions the daily offering of a tenth of a <i>ephah</i> as duty of the High Priest clothed in multiple garb [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Tzav, Chapter 5 1" href="/Sifra,_Tzav,_Chapter_5.1"><i>Sifra Ṣaw Pereq</i> 5(1</a>)].</i>. The verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:15" href="/Leviticus.6.15"><i>Lev</i>. 6:15</a>, on the daily flour sacrifice of the High Priest.</i>, <i>in his stead, of his sons</i>. One whose son will stand in his stead brings a tenth of an <i>ephah</i>. But one whose sons will not stand in his stead does not bring a tenth of an <i>ephah</i>. From where the anointed’s for war son will not stand in his stead? The verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 29:30" href="/Exodus.29.30"><i>Ex</i>. 29:30</a>. As often, the proof is from the part of the verse not quoted: <i>Seven days the priest shall wear them who of his sons will stand in his stead to officiate in the Sanctuary</i>. The only hereditary office in Divine Service is that of the High Priest. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 72b-73a" href="/Yoma.72b-73a">Babli <i>Yoma</i> 72b/73a</a>.</i>, <i>seven days shall the priest wear them</i>, etc. If one officiates in the Tent of Meeting, his son will stand in his stead. But one who does not officiate in the Tent of Meeting, his son will not stand in his stead. From where that he can be appointed as High Priest<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Anointed for War is under the restrictions valid for the High Priest one has to ascertain that his office be subordinate, not coordinate, to the High Priesthood and that an appointment to High Priesthood does not violate the rule that one may not reduce the holiness of one’s position (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:30" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.30">Note 151</a>).</i>? [As is written,] <sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Chronicles 9:20" href="/I_Chronicles.9.20"><i>1Chr</i>. 9:20</a>. The leader of the priests is the High Priest. Phineas was appointed Anointed for War by Moses, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 31:6" href="/Numbers.31.6"><i>Num</i>. 31:6</a>.</i><i>Phineas the son of Eleazar was leader over them</i>;<i>in earlier times the Eternal was with him</i>. When Rebbi Yose wanted to needle<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">Hebrew verb built on a Greek root; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:1:22" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.1.22"><i>Berakhot</i> 3, Note 96</a>.</i> Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose seems to have complained about a lack of leadership on the part of his son.</i>, he said to him, “before, he was with him.” In the days of Zimri<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:1-15" href="/Numbers.27.1-15"><i>Num</i>. 27:1–15</a>.</i>, he protested. In the days of the concubine at Gibea<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 19-21" href="/Judges.19-21"><i>Jud</i>. 19–21</a>. In the opinion of <i>Seder Olam</i>, based on the teachings of R. Yose the Tanna (who is meant here), the affair at Gibea happened at the start of the period of the Judges, when Phineas was High Priest. Cf. the author’s edition of <i>Seder Olam</i> (Northvale NJ 1998), pp. 122–123.</i>, he did not protest.
<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">The text here up to the quote from Bar Qappara’s Mishnah is corrupt, contradictory in itself and mostly missing in B. Since it is a careless copy of the text in <i>Yoma</i> (1:1 38b l. 26) <i>and Megillah</i> (2:12 71a l. 75), an explanation must be based on that text. The paragraph discusses the rules for the priest Anointed for War. It starts with an assertion that the Anointed for War officiates in the Temple in the High Priest’s garb while later it is asserted without dissent that he he barred from any service in the Sanctuary. The entire topic is a reconstruction of the environment in which one has to place David’s inquiries to God as recorded in the books of Samuel.<br>A consistent whole is found in the <i>Yoma</i>/<i>Megillah</i> text. In the following, standard font is used for the <i>Yoma</i> text; where the <i>Megillah</i> text deviates, it is given in different typeface.<br>מְנַיִין שֶׁהָיָה (<b>שֶׁהוּא</b>) נִשְׁאַל בִּשְׁמוֹנָה. ר׳ בָּא רִבִּי חִייָה בְשֵׁם רִבִּי יוֹחָנָן. וּבִגְדֵ֤י הַקּוֹדֶשׁ אֲשֶׁ֣ר לְאַֽהֲרֹ֔ן יִהְי֥וּ לְבָנָי֖ו אַֽחֲרָ֑יו. מַה תַלְמוּד לוֹמַר אַֽחֲרָ֑יו. אֶלָּא לִגְדוּלָּה שֶׁלְּאַחֲרָיו. וּמְנַיִין (<b>מְנַיִין</b>) שֶׁהוּא עוֹבֵד בִּשְׁמוֹנָה. רִבִּי יִרְמְיָה <b>ר׳ אִימִּי</b> בְשֵׁם רִבִּי יוֹחָנָן. וּבִגְדֵ֤י הַקּוֹדֶשׁ אֲשֶׁ֣ר לְאַֽהֲרֹ֔ן <b>יִהְי֥וּ לְבָנָ֖יו</b>. מַה תַלְמוּד לוֹמַר אַֽחֲרָ֑יו. אֶלָּא לִקְדוּשָּׁה (<b>לִגְדּוּלָּה</b>) שֶׁלְּאַחֲרָיו. אָמַר לֵיהּ רִבִּי יוֹנָה. עִמְּךָ הָיִיתִי. לֹא אָמַר עוֹבֵד אֶלָּא נִשְׁאַל. וּבַמֶּה (<b>בַּמֶּה הוּא</b>) נִשְׁאַל. אַייתֵי רַב הוֹשַׁעִיָה מַתְנִיתָא דְבַר קַפָּרָא מִן דְּרוֹמָא (<b>דְּרוֹמָה</b>) וְתַנָּא. וַחֲכָמִים אוֹמְרִים. אֵינוֹ עוֹבֵד לֹא בִשְׁמוֹנָה שֶׁל כֹּהֵן גָּדוֹל וְלֹא בְאַרְבָּעָה שֶׁל כֹּהֵן הֶדְיוֹט.<br>And from where that he was asked in eight<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">The Anointed for War has two jobs. One is to address the army as described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20" href="/Deuteronomy.20"><i>Deut.</i> 20: 1-9</a>, the other to ask the Urim and Tummim oracle on behalf of the army commander. Since this oracle is mentioned only in connection with the High Priest's garments (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 28:30" href="/Exodus.28.30"><i>Ex.</i> 28:30</a>) it is obvious that the Anointed for War must wear one of these garments for the oracle. But since all eight garments of the High Priest form an indivisible unit, he must wear all of them.</i>? Rebbi Abba Rebbi Hiyya in the name of Rebbi Johanan: <i>And Aaron’s holy garments shall be for his descendants in his stead</i><sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 29:30" href="/Exodus.29.30"><i>Ex</i>. 29:30</a>. As often, the proof is from the part of the verse not quoted: <i>Seven days the priest shall wear them who of his sons will stand in his stead to officiate in the Sanctuary</i>. The only hereditary office in Divine Service is that of the High Priest. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 72b-73a" href="/Yoma.72b-73a">Babli <i>Yoma</i> 72b/73a</a>.</i>. Why does the verse say, <i>in his stead</i>? For greatness after him<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 29:30" href="/Exodus.29.30"><i>Ex.</i> 29:30</a> continues: <i>To be anointed in them and inducted into office.</i> Since the one Anointed for War is anointed, he seems to quality.</i>. And from where that he officiated in eight<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">This seems logical. Since the Anointed for War is required to wear the High Priest's garb, "one increases in sanctity but does not decrease" (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:30" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.30">Note 151</a>). Otherwise one will have to disquality the Anointed for War from all office in the Sanctuary.</i>? For holiness after him. In what was he asked? They brought it, רבבהו, a Mishnah of Bar Qappara came from the South which stated: He officiates neither in the four of a common priest nor in the eight of a High Priest. Rebbi Abba said, it would be logical that he officiate in four<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that as a matter of principle, the Anointed for War could use the eight garments of the High Priest strictly for his duties outside the sanctuary and still be a common priest inside without violating the principle of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:30" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.30">Note 151</a>. Lest people say, we saw a simple priest who sometimes officiated in eight like a High Priest<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the Babli, this would be strictly a rabbinic rule, not based on biblical principles, and therefore not a historical reconstruction by a new rule for the days of the Messiah.</i>.
